THE EXPORT BEHAVIOUR OF SMALL AND
MEDIUM-SIZED MANUFACTURING FIRMS:
EVIDENCE FROM THE GARMENT & TEXTILE
INDUSTRY OF GHANA

By

Obi Berko O. Damoah

partial fulfillment of the requirements for the degree of

ii

ABSTRACT
The aim of this thesis is to examine why the majority of SMEs from the garment and textile
sub-sector decide to remain focused on the domestic market, while few choose to sell a
proportion of their goods abroad, even though they face similar market conditions and
operate in the same location. A variety of explanations to this problem exist in the literature
examining the export behaviour of small businesses. However, these explanations are
inconclusive and restricted in geographic scope (e.g. studies from the USA, Europe, and the
United Kingdom). Because geographic contexts are different it is uncertain whether or not the
recommendations based on studies from western countries can be implemented within the
Ghanaian economy. As a result, a research gap exists with regard to understanding the export
behaviour of SMEs from Ghana.
Following the recognition of a gap in the literature, this thesis seeks to shed light on the issue
from the Ghanaian context. The thesis proposes an integrated theoretical framework,
developed from an analysis of the extant literature review, to address an explanation why
some SMEs export and others do not. The proposed framework consists of a synthesised
integration of the following theoretical perspectives: the resource-based view of the firm;
stage theory; network theory, international entrepreneurship theory; and contingency theory.
The key assumption underlying the integrated framework is that SME export behaviour
represents a complex event, and therefore no single theoretical framework is robust enough to
explain the phenomenon in detail. In addition, as none of the theoretical frameworks are
without weakness it is argued their integration offsets the weaknesses of each, thereby
offering a deeper explanation of the factors underlying export behaviour.
Empirically, the thesis drawing on a set of quantitative data and more qualitative interviews
concerns the behaviour of both small and medium-sized exporting and non-exporting firms
from the garment and textile manufacturing sector in Ghana. An analysis of this data is
undertaken to empirically validate the proposed theoretical integrated framework. The
quantitative methods involve regression analysis, and the qualitative analysis is based on a
systematic content review of the data emanating from the interview data.
The results from the empirical analysis suggest that the proposed integrated framework
matches well with the empirical data. Overall, firm size, sector, workforce education levels,
and participation in international and domestic business networks are found to be the key
drivers explaining why some SMEs from Ghana choose to export, while majority of others
remained focused on the domestic market. Based on the findings it is concluded that SME
owner-managers in Ghana seeking to export should invest in the development of both
domestic and international networks as a means of improving their access to international
markets. From a policy perspective it is recommended that the government of Ghana should
provide further support to facilitate the activities of industrial associations in Ghana, since
their interaction and network development with indigenous firms has been shown to be of
importance in helping SMEs achieve export success.

CHAPTER ONE
INTRODUCTION
1.1

BACKGROUND TO THE RESEARCH

The internationalisation of small and medium-sized businesses (SMEs) has flourished in
recent times across the world (Etmad, 2004; Andersson et al., 2004; Orser et al., 2008).
About one-fifth of manufacturing SMEs obtain 10% - 40% of their sales revenues from
international sources (OCED, 2000). Various macro and micro benefits offered by
export business (referred to in the literature as the ‘export-led growth hypothesis’) have
been confirmed in many countries. The export-led growth hypothesis assumes that
export business causes growth at national and firm level. Support for this hypothesis has
been found in Canada (Serletis, 1992; Awokuse, 2003), the US, the UK, Japan and
Germany (Marine, 1992), Egypt (Abou-Stait 2005), Malaysia (Keong et al., 2005),
Argentina, Brazil, Mexico (Maneschiöld, 2008) and Jordan (Husein, 2009). Aboagye
(2006) argues that East Asia’s phenomenal economic performance over the last three
decades is accredited to export orientation policies.
The importance of SME internationalisation events has been recognised in sub-Saharan
Africa and Africa in general. In sub-Saharan Africa, Rankin et al. (2006) argue that the
domestic market is weak, and therefore, without export involvement, it will be unlikely
to propel firms into growth. In Africa, in general, Rankin et al. (2006), Kuada (2007)
and Wolf (2007) reported that the overall internal domestic market of the continent is
too small to propel firms’ growth. In addition, Etemad (2004) argues that because of the
influence of globalisation, firms are at increased risk of failure, if they choose to

Page 1

concentrate exclusively on their domestic market. Following these observations, it is
argued that internationalisation of firms from Africa represents one of the urgent and
most important policy initiatives to lead the African continent to socio-economic
prosperity (Rankin et al., 2006; Kuada, 2007; Wolf (2007).
Further to the evidence above, in most industrialised countries, researchers have
supported the importance of export manufacturing among SMEs in Africa. For example,
Bigsten et al. (2004) used firm-level data from the manufacturing sector in four African
countries to confirm the export-led growth hypothesis; in this study, the researchers
concluded that firms from Africa learn from export business. Ahmed et al. (2007) use
samples from five countries in sub-Saharan Africa (SSA) and also supported the
importance business and growth hypothesis. Likewise, Nyikam (2004) conducted a
similar study of 21 countries in SSA and found country-specific support for the
hypothesis.
Even though several entry strategies (e.g. franchising, licensing, exporting etc.) exist for
firms considering internationalisation as a strategic option, export business in particular
has been identified as the major internationalisation strategy pursued by SMEs. It is
argued that export business involves lower resource investments, lower risks and lower
total cost compared to other foreign investment modes (Zho & Zou, 2002; Sousa et al.,
2008; Leonidou et al., 2010). Efficient utilisation of scarce resources, innovation,
perceived success, goal achievement and sense of satisfaction are among the other
financial and non-financial benefits which internationalised SMEs gain compared to
similar others which do not pursue internationalisation.

Page 2

Additionally, export activity offers SMEs an opportunity to experience the ‘best
practice’ in their overall operations because they learn from successful international
competitors (Zou & Stan, 1998).
On the macro level, export activity among SMEs has shown the capacity to drive
economic development, according to the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and
Development (OECD, 2009). Export business integrates countries into the world
economy and mitigates them from external shocks from other countries. In addition to
this, export business serves as a source of foreign exchange to countries involved and
reduces potential balance of payment problems. Firms’ exports, therefore, create
employment in the domestic economy, which in turn increases consumption and private
spending. Furthermore, openness to trade improves international negotiations
concerning trade and tariff issues, and countries can use this platform for their own
benefit and build their respective welfare positions concerning trade barriers
(Maneschiöld, 2008).
Moreover, in addition to the specific macro and micro benefits, advancement in
international transportation and communication, reductions in trade barriers, the
shortening of product and technology life cycles, and large multinational enterprises
competing against SMEs in their own domestic market are also among the factors
facilitating the internationalisation of SMEs (Coviello & Munro, 1995, Rasmussen et
al., 2001; Etemad, 2004).

Page 3

1.2

THE RESEARCH PROBLEM

The main research problem this thesis seeks to address is: why is the export
involvement of manufacturing SMEs from the garment and textile sub-sector in Ghana
low and why do the majority of them focused on the domestic market? Extant empirical
findings in the field which relate to the problem above are based in a developed
countries context (e.g. North America & Europe). As a result, the understanding of
exporting and non-exporting behaviour of SMEs in Africa (i.e. Ghana) represents a gap
in the field. Therefore, this thesis seeks to identify key factors that determine exporting
behaviour of SMEs in Ghana. It is hoped that the outcome of this study will be to link
public policy (e.g. Ghana government’s manufacturing export-led programmes in
Ghana) more directly to firms that possess manufacturing export potential, in order to
encourage a more export involvement and export performance of Ghanaian
manufacturing SMEs. The intended outcome will also be to shed further light on the
extant assumptions and the behaviour of private business practitioners in Ghana through
application of a proposed integrated theoretical framework to the thesis.

1.3

RESEARCH AREA OF THE THESIS

The various issues raised above draw on the SMEs internationalisation research field. A
review of the extant theoretical assumptions in the field (e.g. the resource-based view
and the process theory), coupled with their corresponding empirical findings shed much
light on the SMEs export behaviour. For instance, among studies in the field, it is

Page 4

argued that the determinants of export decisions for the SMEs appear to consist of
external and internal factors, but it is the influence of the internal resource capacity
which significantly facilitates the export behaviour of the SME rather than the external
factors (McDougall et al., 1997; Rueber & Fischer, 1997; Ibeh, 2003; 2004; Westhead
et al., 2004; Ibeh, 2005; Ibeh & Wheeler, 2005; Sousa et al., 2008; Williams, 2008;
Lages et al., 2009). The core argument of these studies is that, although macro level
export promotion policies are necessary, they do not constitute the ultimate condition
for the SME to change its strategy from one of a wholly domestic firm to an
international firm.
Furthermore, neither do the macro level programmes constitute a sufficient condition
for existing exporters to improve their export performance. As a result, these authors
contend that for SMEs to respond positively to macro export promotions and change
from being wholly domestic firms to becoming exporters, the actual stimulus resides
within the firms’ internal resource capacity, including the personal aspirations and
subjective factors of its owner-manager. Although previous arguments appear to offer a
straightforward answer with regard to why export activity among Ghana’s SMEs is so
limited, the theoretical frameworks in the field were developed based on the export
behaviour of developed countries’ firms and so most of the empirical studies were also
mainly based on SMEs from the developed countries (e.g. North America & Europe).
Therefore, because the socio economic contexts of the developed countries differ from
those of developing countries (e.g. Ghana), the understanding of the export behaviour of
the SME from developing country contexts (e.g. Ghana) is limited in the literature. As a
result, the assumptions of extant theories applied in the field (e.g. the resource-based

Page 5

view and the stage theory), with their associated empirical findings, cannot
automatically be externally generalised to Ghana.
The present study therefore, extends the exporting behaviour of research into SMEs
using evidence from Ghana to shed light on the phenomenon. Building on the literature
review (Chapter 2), unlike previous studies (e.g., Bell, 1995; Coviello & Munro, 1995;
Reuber & Fischer, 1997; Westhead et al., 2001; Moen & Servais, 2002; Hall & Tu,
2003; 2004; Ibeh, 2005; Williams, 2008) that apply a single theoretical framework to
address the topic, a proposed integrated theoretical framework is applied in this thesis to
shed light on the export behaviour of the SMEs. The proposed integrated theoretical
framework applied in the thesis consists of combining the resource-based view, the
stage theory, the network theory and the international entrepreneurship theory.
The argument behind the proposed integrated theoretical framework that guides the
thesis follows that of Coviello & Martin (1999), Crick & Spence (2005), Coviello &
Cox (2006), inter alia, who contend that SME export behaviour is a complex
phenomenon that cannot be fully understood by applying a single theoretical
framework. Moreover, unlike other previous studies (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994;
Reuber & Fischer, 1997; Westhead et al., 2001; Moen & Servais, 2002; Manolova,
2002), this thesis also takes into consideration the influence

of the external

environment in analysing the export behaviour of the SMEs. As a result, contingency
theory is also applied; this theory is used to supplement the four theoretical frameworks
so as to shed greater light on the phenomenon. The proposed integrated theoretical
framework, developed from the literature review (Chapter 2) to guide the exploration of
the research questions behind the thesis, is presented in Figure 1.1.

Page 6

Figure 1.1: Integrated Theoretical Framework of SME Export Behaviour

Page 7

The main argument behind the predictive integrated framework in Figure 1.1 is that of
the eleven generally accepted internationalisation theories reviewed in Chapter Two, the
resource-based view (RBV), stage theory, network theory and international
entrepreneurship offer the best and most detailed explanation of export activities of
SMEs than others (e.g. product life cycle theory, internalisation and transaction cost
theory) (Ruzzier et al., 2006; Johanson & Vahlne, 2009; Johanson & Vahlne, 2010).
Therefore, in order to understand export activities of SMEs in detail in an entirely new
context, the purpose is to integrate all the four frameworks in addition to the contingent
theory, so that no significant variable is left out from the analysis. In addition, the
review of the literature showed that as none of the theoretical frameworks shown in
Figure 1.1 is without weaknesses, an approach based on integrating them complements
each others strengths and eradicates their weaknesses. Moreover, following Bell (1995)
SMEs’ exporting activities represent a complex, dynamic and interactive phenomenon
which no single theoretical framework can explain it in fully and so the integration
approach adopted in this thesis provide a robust method for addressing SMEs’ exporting
activities that will be appropriate for the context of developing countries (e.g. Ghana).
Figure 2.6 in Chapter 2 describes the main explanatory variables for each of the
integrated theoretical frameworks in detail.

1.4

RESEARCH QUESTIONS

The research questions behind this thesis draw on the statement of the problem in
Section 1.2 above. In accordance with the statement of the research problem, the basic
research questions the study seeks to address include the following considerations:

Page 8

1) Following the theoretical assumptions of the integrated theoretical framework
for the thesis, as examined in the literature review (Chapter 2), what is the
likelihood that the firm’s size, age, workers’ education, foreign ownership and
sector and/or industry will predict the likelihood of a small and medium-sized
enterprises from Ghana changing its strategy from engaging wholly in domestic
business to becoming an international business firm?
2) Following the assumptions of the proposed integrated theoretical framework
which guides the thesis, which ‘critical incidents’ (Chapter 4, sub Section 4.4.1
& Chapter 6, sub-section 6.2.5.1) trigger the export initiation of SMEs from
Ghana, and what are the main factors which facilitate their capacity to supply
their export orders ?
3) To what extent do non-exporting SMEs from Ghana experience the same
‘critical incidents’ that catapult their counterpart exporting firms to initiate
export business? What main barriers prevent these firms from taking advantage
of critical incidents and initiating exporting business, and what are the general
reasons for not exporting?

Page 9

1.5

RESEARCH HYPOTHESES

Following the first research question above, the hypotheses that this study seeks to
examine will include the following:
H1: Domestic SMEs that have foreign ownership will be more likely to respond
positively to export stimuli and change their strategy from engaging wholly in domestic
business and enter the export market than their counterparts that are wholly
domestically owned.
H2: The larger the size of the firm, the more the likelihood that it will respond positively
to export stimuli and change its strategy from engaging wholly in domestic business to
enter the export market.
H3: The older the firm, the more the likelihood that it would react positively to export
stimuli and change its strategy from engaging wholly in domestic business and enter the
export market and become an international business firm.
H4: The aggregate full-time workers’ education level in a firm will be positively related
to the export propensity of the firm.
H5: There will be a positive association between the sector and/or industry’s influence
and the export propensity of a firm.

Page 10

1.6

RESEARCH OBJECTIVES

Drawing on the research questions and hypotheses above, the thesis seeks to achieve the
following objectives.
1) To undertake an extensive literature review on the extant theories that seek to
address the small firm’s export and non-exporting behaviour, and to develop
and/or propose an integrative predictive framework to address the research
questions;
2) To identify whether or not firm size, age, aggregate full-time workers’
education, foreign ownership and the sector/industry will be positively
correlated with the likelihood that the small firm from Ghana will export;
3) To identify the ‘critical incidents’ which trigger export initiation of SMEs from
Ghana and the key factors which facilitate their capacity to meet export orders;
4) To ascertain whether or not the non-exporting SMEs from the garment & textile
sub-sector experience similar critical incidents to those that catapult their
counterpart exporting firms to initiate export business. If they do, the aim is to
explore their reactions, the main barriers which prevent them from activating
similar events and becoming exporters as well as the general reasons for not
exporting;
5) To identify the implications of the study that can be recommended for future
research, for theory, practising owner-managers and public policy makers.

Page 11

1.7

SMALL BUSINESS MANUFACTURING EXPORTS: THE AFRICAN

CONTEXT
Hausmann et al. (2007) argue that what a nation exports matters most. However, for
various reasons, in terms of export composition in general, manufacturing exporting is
considered to be of the most importance. Teal (2008) argues that the great economic
success being achieved by most of the industrial nations over the last thirty years
correlates directly with their abilities to achieve a sustained growth in manufacturing
output and export manufacturing. Export manufacturing constitutes a key driver of
productivity of firms, which accelerates significant developments in standards of living.
Babatunde (2002) argues that trade structure of sub-Saharan Africa’s (SSA) economies
shows great dependence on export of unprocessed primary commodities, which slows
their economic growth trajectory.
Evidence from both advanced and emerging economies has converged to show that on
average exporting firms tend to be more productive relative to their counterparts which
remain focused on the domestic market (King et al., 2010). Naude & Gries (2004)
contend that the main reason for Africa’s lack of industrialisation and its gloomy
economic growth performance is because of its lack of adequate manufacturing exports.
Soderbom & Teal (2003) argue that the most prominent feature of the Asian tiger
economies was the growth in their export of manufacturing products.
Elhiraika (2008) sampled 36 African countries and found that an increased share of
manufacturing in total output has the potential to raise GDP growth and reduce growth
vitality. Soderbom & Teal (2003) further argue that the principal cause of the collapse
of the African economies in the period since independence is due to the collapse of

Page 12

exports, especially manufacturing exports. The reason for this is that most countries in
SSA started on import substitution ‘industrialisation’ immediately following
independence, but when inefficiencies associated with this policy arose, the
industrialisation agenda collapsed. Rankin et al. (2006) found that poor performance of
SSA economies was because of the low level of manufacturing exports.
King et al. (2010) contend that although SSA possesses about 12 per cent of the world
population, its contribution of world manufacturing exports is less than 1 per cent. King
et al. further argued that only five economies in SSA (Mauritius, South Africa,
Botswana, Swaziland and Namibia) exported more than US$100 of manufactured goods
per capita between 1994 and 2008. According to these authors, for most other
economies in SSA, the figure was below US$10 per capita. Therefore, despite the
significance of manufacturing exports worldwide (OCED, 2009), Africa’s contribution
– especially sub-Saharan Africa – is abysmally low compared to the rest of the world
(Cline, 2004; Broadman, 2006; Rankin et al., 2006; Wolf, 2007).
Erwin et al. (1990) contend that one of the major factors which significantly distinguish
the leading industrial countries from other developing countries is the variation in their
export performance. It is not uncommon, therefore, to find a statement bemoaning
Africa’s poor manufacturing export contribution. For example, the recent commission
on Africa’s study indicated that:
The last three decades have witnessed stagnation in Africa. The composition of
Africa’s exports has virtually remained unchanged and this has contributed to
the collapse in Africa’s share of world trade…. Africa will not be able to achieve
the Millennium Development Goals, nor set itself on a sustainable path to
growth and poverty reduction, without increased trade.
(Commission for Africa, 2005: 256).

Page 13

The quote above also confirms the “statistics” Broadman (2006: 8) presented to the
International Monetary Fund (IMF)’s direction on trade with regard to the declining
state of Africa’s exports contribution, compared to that of other world economies. Wolf
(2007) notes that Ghana’s exports composition is still dominated by a few unprocessed
primary commodities (e.g. cocoa and certain mineral products). The reason for this has
been attributed to the fact that Ghana’s manufacturing sectors are generally small and
underdeveloped (Asante, 2008). The manufacturing sub-sectors in Ghana include foodprocessing, textiles and garments, furniture, sawmill and wood products. Of these-, this
study will focus on SMEs from the garment and textile sub-sector of Ghana’s
manufacturing sector. The reasons for this will be explained in detail in Chapter 3 (the
country context chapter). However, Asante (2008) maintained that since 1988 there has
been significant deterioration in the manufacturing performance of Ghana’s exporters.
Table 1.1 below from the database of the World Bank confirms the abysmal
contribution of Ghana’s manufacturing sector with reference to its annual growth rate.

Page 15

The manufacturing portion of Table 1.1 is underlined because the purpose of the table is
to throw light on only the manufacturing performance, aside from all the other themes
(e.g. agriculture, industry services). Overall, the table shows that the contribution of the
manufacturing sector to the Gross Domestic Product (GDP) declined in the years from
1988 to 2008, whilst its average annual growth recorded negative figures up to 2008;
although some data for the period are missing.
However, Ghana is among the countries of sub-Saharan Africa which have embraced
export-led growth policies, especially regarding the manufacture of export commodities
(Adjasi, 2006). The government of Ghana has been paying significant attention to its
export manufacturing sector for two main reasons. First, it was identified that Ghana’s
initial unprocessed primary export commodities make a poor contribution to the
national economy with regard to: (1) foreign exchange earnings, (2) employment
generation, and (3) firm level learning (Kastner, 2005).
Second, Ghana has been motivated to make its export manufacturing sector the main
focus of its socio-economic development paradigm. This is because it is influenced by
the argument that the economic success achieved by most of the industrialised countries
and the tiger countries (e.g. the USA and Japan) in the previous thirty years was mainly
driven by the contribution of their manufacturing for export (Teal, 2008). As a result,
about three decades ago, the government of Ghana implemented various export-led
programmes which aimed to facilitate export involvement of its firms. Among the
export-led programmes, those that focused on export manufacturing have been awarded
top priority in terms of the general export-led programmes of Ghana’s government.

Page 16

Among the government of Ghana’s export-led programmes, those that specifically
aimed to enhance the capacity of its export manufacturing include the Economic
Recovery Programme and the Structure Adjustment Programme (SAP) of 1983. Both
the ERP and the SAP were recommended and jointly implemented by the World Bank
(WB) and the International Monetary Fund (IMF). However, the desire of Ghana to
promote export business was first recognised in 1969, when the Ghana Export
Promotion Council was formed under the legislation of NLC 1 Decree 936 with the
mandate to develop and facilitate exports.
Ghana’s interest in export/outward orientation actually deepened within the framework
of the Economic Recovery Programme (ERP) 2 in the 1980s. As a result, it can be
argued that the ERP set the pace for Ghana’s international orientation. After the ERP,
subsequent export-led programmes have been implemented, which are captured in other
economic documents of the government of Ghana namely Ghana Vision 2020 3 and the
Ghana Poverty Reduction Strategy I & II (GPRS I & II) 4. These two policy documents
(the GPRS I & II) were also preceded by Ghana Vision 2020. The ultimate thrust of
these documents is to improve the macroeconomic environment of Ghana for
accelerated economic growth linked to export promotion.

1

NLC represents National Liberation Council, which was the then ruling government in
Ghana;
2
Economic Recovery Programme (ERP) was recommended and supported by the IMF
and The World Bank and ushered Ghana into a significant outward orientation regime.
The ERP consisted of a comprehensive set of policies to reform the monetary, fiscal and
trade sectors in Ghana;
3
Vision 2020 is a long-term economic framework by the government of Ghana, which
is intended to usher the country into a middle income status by the year 2020;
4
GPRS I & II is another socio economic framework introduced by the government of
Ghana for its poverty reduction agenda, which developed out of the Vision 2020.

Page 17

Other export-led programmes which have subsequently been implemented include: (1)
the implementation of US$41million credit in 1994 as Private Enterprise and Export
Development (PEED) programme; and (2) the creation of the Ghana Free Zones Board
(GFZB) & the Export Processing Zones (EPZs) in 1995. The GFZB Programme was
designed to encourage processing and manufacturing of goods through the
establishment of Export Processing Zones. Through the GFZB, the whole of Ghana now
is accessible to potential investors (locally and abroad), who have the opportunity to use
the free zones as focal points to produce goods for foreign markets. A further initiative
introduced was (3) the restructuring of the Ghana Export Promotion Council (GEPC).
The GEPC was set up to promote exports from Ghana, but due to the import
substitution policy of the government at that time, it was under-resourced from the
1980s until the 2000s, when it finally became fully resourced.
The many other programmes include: (4) the West Africa Trade Hub (WTH); the WTH
is an initiative of the US government through the United States Agency for International
Development (USAID), established to facilitate export business from West Africa
including Ghana; (5) the implementation of the African Cashew Alliance (ACA); the
African Cashew Alliance (ACA) is an industry organisation established in 2006
comprising 24 public and private sector organisations and operating in all the major
Cashew Producing countries (i.e. cashew processing) in Africa (e.g. Ghana, Tanzania,
Mozambique); ACA seeks to promote the competitiveness of Africa’s cashew through
value added processing for export; (6) the implementation of the Business Development
Services Fund (BDSF); the BDSF is the World Bank (IDA) initiative in Ghana that
supports micro, small and medium-sized enterprises, paying for the cost of consultancy
and/or experts’ services, including technical assistance that enhances their growth in

Page 18

export manufacturing (see Chapter 3, Section 3.6 & Chapter 6, Section 6.2. for details
of additional manufacturing export-led programmes being implemented in Ghana).
Regardless of the implementations of these manufacturing export-led programmes, it is
argued that Ghana’s current export involvement and performance has not met
government targets (Kastner, 2005 5). In addition, it is further argued that
internationally, Ghana is considered as one of the poorest export manufacturing
countries in sub-Saharan Africa (Zeufack, 2001; Soderbom and Teal, 2003; Rankin et
al., 2006; Robson and Freel, 2008). Table 1.2 below shows evidence of the decline in
involvement in manufacturing for export and of the poor performance of Ghana’s
economy.

SMEs dominate. The reason for this is because Quartey (2006) maintained that the
garment & textile sub-sector in Ghana is mainly made up of SMEs, with few large firms
(e.g. the Ghana Textile Manufacturing Company (GTMC), the Akosombo Textile
Limited (ATL), the Ghana Textile Products (GTP) and the Printex Ghana Ltd.). Overall,

with 37% of its firms exporting an average of 26% of their output. Within the subSaharan Africa, about 23% of Kenya’s firms export 11% of their output, whereas in
Ghana, only 6% of firms export their output and of those that do, less than 3% of their
output is sold abroad. This confirms the argument that Ghana remains one of the poorest
manufacturing export nations, since even among the firms that export, only 1% of them
are likely to export all of their output abroad compared to 21% and 7% in India and
Kenya, respectively. Additionally, Chapter 3 and Figures 3.1 & 3.2 present further
statistics about the declining involvement in export manufacturing and performance of
Ghana.
As shown in Tables 1.1 and 1.2, coupled with other evidence in Figures 3.1 & 3.2
(Chapter 3 - Country Context chapter), it can be contended that the argument that
‘Ghana’s manufacturing export involvement and performance is low’ can be supported.
In addition, from the World Bank data set used in this study, only 17 firms out 110
manufacturing small and medium-sized firms export (see Chapter 5, sub-Section 5.3).
Besides the low manufacturing export involvement and performance by Ghana’s firms,
it has also been identified that there has in general been a lack of research on the

Page 20

internationalisation of SMEs in Africa in comparison with the rest of the world (Zou
and Stan, 1998; Sousa et al., 2008). Following from this, Mitgwe (2004) contended that
most of the export-led efforts by governments in Africa lack the required firm level
research that should inform the various macro policies and the programmes developed.
Table 1.3 below sheds some light on the dearth of research on the internationalisation of
firms in Africa relative to the world at large.

Although the summary in Table 1.3 is not exhaustive, coupled other studies in the
literature review (Chapter 2) as well as studies presented in Appendices A – I, it can be

Page 22

argued that there is evidence to suggest that there is a lack of studies on the
internationalisation behaviour of firms from Africa compared to the rest of the world.
For instance, in Sousa et al.’s (2008) review, the authors indicate that they were happy
to find an improvement in the number of studies conducted outside the USA because
the USA remained the most researched country with regard to the topic. The authors
maintain that of the 52 studies they reviewed and synthesised, none included data on
African SMEs. They suggest that with the integration of the world economy, (including
African nations), the understanding of the research community in the field would not be
complete unless effort was put into building firm level research on Africa (Sousa et al.,
2008). Sousa et al.’s (2008) review confirms the findings of Rankin (2001), Bigsten et
al. (2001) and Granér & Isaksson (2007), who find that research on firms in Africa in
general is scarce, especially on firms from sub-Saharan Africa.

1.8

METHODOLOGY

The methodology used in this study reflects the research questions behind the thesis
(Section 1.5). The first research question is addressed and the secondary data set is
analysed using Logistic regression. Here, the aim is to ascertain the main determinants
of exporting and non-exporting small business behaviour. Research questions 2 and 3
(objective 3 & 4) use the case study research approach. With regard to these particular
questions the ‘critical incidents’ method coupled with the content analytical technique
are the main analytical tools used in the data collection and the analyses.

Page 23

1.9

SIGNIFICANCE OF THE STUDY

The purpose of the research is to address the current low involvement in manufacturing
export of SMEs in Ghana. The research seeks to ascertain exporting and non-exporting
SMEs behaviour in Ghana. By applying the proposed integrated theoretical framework,
the study intends to make a contribution to the field, using new insights on the exporting
and non-exporting behaviour of the small businesses in Ghana. Currently, the lack of
studies concerning the export behaviour of SMEs in Africa (e.g. Ghana) inhibits
understanding in this area. In addition, identifying the main firm-level capacity factors,
which significantly predict small business export decisions and export performance, will
lead to an efficient utilisation of resources. Moreover, public policy makers can draw on
the outcomes of the study to aid their decisions on which firms need export assistance in
order to make a contribution to the overall growth and prosperity of Ghana.

1.10

OUTLINE OF THE THESIS

The thesis comprises eight chapters. This first chapter introduces the background of the
study; it addresses the statement of the research problem, the research area of the thesis,
background of manufacturing exports from Africa (including Ghana, especially
regarding the main issues that inform the statement of the problem). Following the
problem statement, the chapter defines the research questions, the hypotheses, the
research objectives and the study’s significance. Chapter Two reviews the export and

Page 24

non-exporting behaviour of SMEs in general. In this chapter, on the basis of
amalgamation of various theoretical frameworks in the field, an integrated framework
(comprising five theoretical frameworks) is proposed to guide the thesis. The third
chapter introduces the study’s context, focusing on the manufacturing sector of Ghana,
including its various sub-sectors and the current trends of Ghana’s involvement and
performance in export manufacturing (e.g. Figures 3.1 & 3.2). In Chapter 3, the reason
for focusing the thesis on the garment and textile is explained.
Chapter 3 further substantiates the argument that Ghana’s involvement in export
manufacturing is low, even though the phenomenon has been identified as key to
Ghana’s socio-economic development. Chapter Four, the methodology chapter, presents
an overview of the various research approaches and methodologies applied in the
empirical chapters (i.e. Chapters 5, 6, & 7). It starts by outlining the main stages and
steps that were followed to achieve the research objectives behind the thesis. The
chapter also deals with the analytical techniques that were applied in the empirical
chapters. In this chapter, the concepts’ validity and reliability and how they were
observed in the thesis are also discussed. The chapter ends with a clarification of how
SMEs are operationalised in the thesis.
Chapters Five, Six and Seven focus on the empirical data analysis and discuss the key
research questions behind the thesis. Chapter Eight, the final chapter, summarises the
entire thesis, including the main findings, conclusions and implications for public
policy, practice and future research, as well as the limitations of the thesis.

Page 25

1.11

CONCLUDING REMARKS

The chapter presents a clear direction with regard to the focus of the study. It gives the
background concerning the general importance of the phenomenon worldwide and
specifically in relation to Ghana. As a result of Ghana’s current low level of
manufacturing export involvement and performance, coupled with the fact that the
phenomenon is extensively researched mainly in the developed economies, a research
problem is posed. Following the definition of the research problem, specific hypotheses
and research questions are deduced which are examined in detail in the empirical
chapters of the thesis (i.e. Chapters 5, 6, & 7). In addition, the organisation of the thesis
is also presented. In the absence of relevant literature, the study attempts to use new
evidence (based on the Ghanaian context) to address the phenomenon under
investigation. As a result, the outcome of the study will be a significant contribution to
the field deriving from the theoretical assumptions of the proposed integrated
framework. This will also answer the call in the literature to replicate studies from
developed countries to developing countries, especially African nations, in order to fully
understand the export behaviour of the small firm.

Page 26

CHAPTER TWO
LITERATURE REVIEW
2.1

INTRODUCTION

The chapter seeks to achieve research objective one of the thesis. The thesis concerns
the exporting and non-exporting behaviour (i.e. the export propensity and the export
performance) of the SME sector and the literature review consists of two main parts to
address this. The first part starts by introducing the historical background of the
internationalisation phenomenon. An overview of the extant internationalisation
theories

follows

(i.e.

the

classical

theories

and

the

generally

accepted

internationalisation theories). Following the examination of the generally accepted
internationalisation theories, a proposed integrated theoretical framework, comprising
the

resource-based

view,

stage

theory,

network

theory

and

international

entrepreneurship theory, developed to address the research questions behind the thesis.
In addition, contingency theory examines the influence of the external environment on
the behaviour of the firm, and is also applied to supplement the integrated theoretical
framework in order to shed fuller light on the topic.
The analytical review of previous studies is discussed in the main body of the chapter,
whilst others are presented in the appendices portion of the thesis. These previous
studies consist of the factors which facilitate SMEs export behaviour. Major limitations
of some of the selected theoretical frameworks, which the thesis seeks to resolve in the
empirical chapters, are discussed. In addition, firm size, age, the aggregate education

Page 27

level of the full-time workers, foreign ownership and sector and/or industry’s influence,
which emerged as influential through application of the proposed integrated theoretical
framework, are reviewed extensively as the focus of the empirical analysis beginning in
Chapter 5. The first part of the literature review continues with a representation of the
proposed integrated framework, including explanations regarding its assumptions.
Finally, part one of the literature review ends with a table showing differences and
similarities among the proposed integrated theoretical frameworks.
The second part of the literature review focuses on the non-exporting SMEs’ behaviour;
it starts by discussing the types of firms that are non-exporting SMEs and whether or
not any theory has sought to address the behaviour of this category of firms. Part two of
the literature review continues with an analysis of the types of studies that address the
non-exporting behaviour of the SMEs. Three themes are identified in the literature with
regard to the studies that focus on non-exporting SME behaviour. These studies
comprise: (1) those that seek to differentiate between exporting and non-exporting
SMEs, based on certain firm characteristics such as the size, the age, the sector, the
owner-manager’s age, the firm’s ownership structure; (2) studies that seek to examine
the perception of the non-exporting SMEs about the factors triggering export initiation;
and (3) studies that attempt to examine the perceptions of non-exporting small firms and
barriers to export business. The second part of the literature review ends with a
discussion of strategies to encourage non-exporting SMEs to participate in export
business (i.e. export stimulation).

Page 28

2.2

HISTORICAL BACKGROUND OF THE INTERNATIONALISATION
PHENOMENON

The initial scholarly writing about the internationalisation phenomenon is credited to
Adam Smith (1776) (e.g. Simões & Crespo, 2002; Mtigwe, 2006). Adam Smith used
the nation as the unit of analysis in the study of internationalisation and advocated the
theory of absolute advantage to examine the wealth of nations. According to researchers
(e.g. Simões & Crespo, 2002; Mtigwe, 2006; Ruzzier et al., 2006), the
internationalisation of the firm was resurrected in the 1950s after the Second World
War, with the large multinational firm as the main unit of analysis. These authors
contend that the research on the internationalisation of SMEs started in the 1970s. Since
the 1970s, research on the internationalisation of the small and medium-sized
enterprises has been flourishing. Currently its most recent theoretical trend centres on
the internationalisation of the small new venture firm/international entrepreneurship
(McDougall et al., 1994; Zahra, 2005; Schulz et al., 2009). Overall, Table 2.1
summarises the evolution of internationalisation processes to date.

Page 29

Table 2.1: Evolution of Research into Internationalisation Activities

1.
Formal writings on the internationalisation phenomenon started with Adam
Smith in the seventeenth century (Simões & Crespo, 2002; Mitgwe, 2006).
2.
The nation as the unit of analysis dominated internationalisation research until
the 1950s (Simões & Crespo, 2002; Mtigwe, 2006).
3.
Research into internationalisation of the firm was revived in the early 1950s
(Simões & Crespo, 2002; Mtigwe, 2006; Ruzzier et al., 2006).
4.

Page 30

2.3

THEORIES OF INTERNATIONALISATION

Although this thesis does not seek to evaluate the extant theories of the
internationalisation research field as its main purpose, it does provide a general
overview of the major theories in the field.
2.3.1

Dimensions of Internationalisation Theories

Different researchers classify theories in this field differently. Mtigwe (2006) classified
internationalisation theories in the field into four dimensions. Mtigwe’s dimensions
comprise: (1) the classical internationalisation theories (i.e. the theory of absolute
advantage; the theory of comparative advantage; and the Heckscher-Ohlin factor
proportion theory); (2) the early market imperfection theories (i.e. the foreign direct
investment theory and international product life cycle theory); (3) the later market
imperfection theories (i.e. the portfolio theory; the internalisation theory; the eclectic
theory; and the resources advantage theory); and (4) the internationalisation theories
(i.e. the incremental theories; the network theory; and the international entrepreneurship
theory). In addition, Ruzzier et al. (2006) classified international business theories into
two dimensions, namely: (1) theories focusing on the large multinational enterprises
(i.e. the internalisation theory; the transaction cost theory; the eclectic paradigm; and the
monopolistic advantages theory) and (2) theories focusing on SMEs internationalisation
(i.e. stage theories; network theory; resource-based view; and international
entrepreneurship theory).

Page 31

Furthermore,

Andersson

(2000)

and

Hermannsdottir

(2008)

categorised

internationalisation theories into two dimensions, which comprise: (1) the economic
theories (i.e. the eclectic paradigm; the international product life cycle; and the
transaction cost approach) and (2) the behavioural theories (i.e. Aharoni’s decision
theory; the stage theory; the network theory; and the international entrepreneurship
theory). Moreover, Axinn and Matthyssens (2002) described the following theories: (1)
the industrial organisation theory, (2) the internalisation theory, (3) the transaction cost
theory, and (4) the eclectic theory as the traditional theories and those that focus on the
large multinational enterprise.
Lack of uniformity and consistency exist among the above dimensions; for instance,
what Mtigwe (2006) referred to as the traditional theories (i.e. the theory of absolute
advantage, the theory of comparative advantage, and the Ohlin factor proportion theory)
contradict what Axinn and Matthyssens (2002) classified as the traditional theories (i.e.
the industrial organisation theory, the internalisation theory, the transaction cost theory,
and the eclectic theory). Critically, what Axinn and Matthyssens described cannot be
classified as traditional and/or classical theories when considered in terms of Mtigwe’s
(2006) traditional and/or classical theories in the field. Furthermore, there is some
confusion about Mtigwe’s (2006) dimensions. Mtigwe referred to the following theories
(e.g.

incremental

and/or

stage

theory;

network

theory;

and

international

entrepreneurship theory) as ‘internationalisation’ theories. Such a dimension seems a bit
unclear because every theory in the field, from the absolute cost advantage theory to the
most recent theory (i.e. the international entrepreneurship theory), can be referred to as
an internationalisation theory.

Page 32

Moreover, whilst Ruzzier et al.’s (2006) dimensions, especially for theories that focus
on large multinational enterprises ( i.e. the internalisation theory; the transaction cost
theory; the eclectic paradigm; and the monopolistic advantages theory) resemble those
of other researchers (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Westhead et al., 2001; Axinn &
Matthyssens, 2002), their second dimension with regard to the theories focusing on
SME internationalisation (e.g. the stage theories; the network theory; the resource-based
view; and the international entrepreneurship theory) has been criticised (by e.g.
McDougall et al., 1994; Westhead et al., 2001) on the basis that these theoretical
frameworks are not solely focused on explaining the internationalisation behaviour of
the SME. According to McDougall et al. (1994) and Westhead et al. (2001), while these
frameworks account for both the large and SMEs, they explain the export behaviour of
the SME in detail. Taking the noted inconsistency in the extant dimensions of
internationalisation theories presented above into consideration, these dimensions are
re-categorised and summarised in this thesis in order to make them clearer, as shown in
Figure 2.1.

Figure 2.1 classifies the theories in the field into four main dimensions and argues that

its unit of analysis. Dimension 2 consists of theories that largely use the firm as the unit
of analysis. Dimension 2 implies that its theories are applicable to both the SMEs and
the large multinational enterprises (MNEs). Dimension 3 implies that although the
theories in dimension 2 apply to all firms in general (i.e. SMEs & MNEs), not all of the
theories explain internationalisation behaviour of SMEs in detail. As a result, dimension
4 represents theories that explain the internationalisation behaviour of SMEs in detail
and they are composed of the resource-based view, the stage theory, the network theory
and international entrepreneurship.

2.4

THE

BASIC

ASSUMPTIONS

OF

INTERNATIONALISATION

THEORIES

Having presented the main dimensions of internationalisation theories, the next subsection examines the main assumptions of each theory. This part is also sub-divided into
two sections, namely: (1) the basic assumptions of the classical theories, and (2) the
basic assumptions of the generally accepted internationalisation theories. For instance,
with the exception of the classical theories (i.e. the absolute cost advantage,
comparative cost advantage and factor proportion theory - Mitgwe, 2006), researchers
(e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Morgan, 1997; Westhead et al., 2001; Andersson, 2004;
Ruzzier et al., 2006; Wright et al., 2007) refer to the rest of the theories (e.g. the

Page 35

international product life cycle theory, monopolistic advantage theory, internalisation
theory, transaction cost theory, theory of international production, oligopolistic reaction
theory, stage theories, resource-based view theory, network theory, innovated related
models and international entrepreneurship theory) as the generally accepted
internationalisation theories in the field. As a result, with regard to the assumptions of
the internationalisation theories, the discussion in this thesis is limited to these two main
themes – (1) the basic assumptions of the classical internationalisation theories, and (2)
the basic assumptions of the generally accepted internationalisation theories.
2.4.1

The Basic Assumptions of the Classical Theories

The three main classical theories are Adam Smith’s (1776) absolute cost advantage
theory, David Ricardo’s (1817) comparative cost advantage, and Hecksher-Ohlin’s
(1933) factor proportion theory. Each of these theories mainly uses the nation as its unit
of analysis. The comparative cost advantage theory seeks to improve upon the absolute
cost advantage theory whereas the factor proportion theory also improves upon the
comparative cost advantage theory.
First of all, the basic assumption of the absolute cost advantage theory is that trade
between countries can be made possible if nations allocate their scarce resources to
producing the set of goods or services for which they possess absolute advantage, and
leave those for which they possess absolute disadvantages entirely to other countries
(Morgan & Katsikeas, 1997; Andersson, 2004; Mtigwe, 2006). Second, according to
Morgan and Katsikeas (1997), Andersson (2004) and Mtigwe (2006), unlike Smith’s
absolute cost advantage theory, Ricardo’s comparative cost advantage assumes that
trade among countries will be more beneficial even when each country focuses on the

Page 36

production of every good or service, but in this case allocates the most resources to
producing the set of outputs for which it possesses comparative advantage, and focuses
less on the other set of outputs for which it has less advantage.
The main difference between the two theories is that whilst Smith (1776) placed
emphasis on solo, absolute and exclusive production principles, Ricardo (1817)
emphasised dual and/or all-inclusive production principles. Whilst the comparative cost
advantage theory sought to improve the absolute cost advantage theory, Mtigwe (2006)
argues that the theory was not able to justify why countries continue to use protectionist
policies to limit trade among themselves, despite the claim that trade between them is
profitable and beneficial. In addition, the basic assumption of the factor proportion
theory is that factor endowments should inform the basis of competitive advantage with
regard to trade among nations.
The basic difference between Ricardo’s theory and Hecksher and Ohlin’s theory is that
whilst Ricardo considered efficiency in production as the basis for national advantage,
Hecksher and Ohlin’s factor proportion theory assumes that natural endowment of
resources and the corresponding cost advantage in production should be the main
sources of a nation’s advantage with regard to trade with other nations.

2.4.2

The

Basic

Assumptions

of

the

Generally

Accepted

Internationalisation Theories
According to researchers (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Morgan & Katsikeas, 1997;
Westhead et al., 2001; Andersson, 2004; Mtigwe, 2006; Ruzzier et al., 2006; Wright et
al., 2007), the main generally accepted internationalisation theories are the following:

Page 37

(1) the international product life cycle theory (Vernon, 1966); (2) the monopolistic
advantage theory (Hymer, 1976); (3) the internalisation theory (Buckley & Casson,
1976); (4) the transaction cost theory (Teece, 1986); (5) the theory of international
production (Dunning, 1988); (6) the oligopolistic reaction theory (Knickerbocker,
1973); (7) the stage theory (Johanson & Vahlne, 1977); (8) the innovative-related
models (Bilkey & Teser, 1977; Cavusgil, 1980; Reid, 1981); (9) the network theory
(Johanson & Mattsson, 1988); (10) the resource-based view of the firm (RBV) (Barney,
1991); and (11) the international entrepreneurship theory (McDougall et al., 1994).
Of the eleven generally accepted internationalisation theories, the main assumptions of
the seven (i.e. the international product life cycle theory, the monopolistic advantage
theory, the internalisation theory, the transaction cost theory, the theory of international
production, the oligopolistic reaction theory and the innovated-related models) which
are not used to help address the research questions behind the thesis are examined
briefly, whereas the other four theories (i.e. the network theory, the resource-based view
(RBV), the stage theory and international entrepreneurship theory), which are integrated
to address the research questions in this thesis, are examined in detail. The reasons for
selecting these four theories are discussed in Section 2.6 below.
The first of the seven theories not used to inform this thesis is the product life cycle
theory (Vernon, 1966), which assumes that the internationalisation development of the
firm is similar to the product life cycle of a firm, which in turn consists of: (1) the
formation stage, (2) the growth stage, (3) the maturity stage, and (4) the decline stage.
According to this theory, a firm’s decision to enter the international market is tied to its
product life cycle trajectory. As a result, the time to internationalise and the choice of
the foreign market are linked to the product’s life cycle development of the firm.

Page 38

Secondly, the monopolistic advantage theory (Hymer, 1976) assumes that the firm
internationalises because it possesses a monopolistic advantage that competitors in the
home market and the host country do not possess. Third, the internalisation theory
(Buckley & Casson, 1976) and the transaction cost theory (Teece, 1986) seem alike
according to Wright et al. (2007).
Therefore, the internalisation theory and the transaction cost theory jointly assume that
cost reduction through undertaking international transaction activities in-house is the
main driver of international involvement. As a result, the international firm will enter
the

international

market

by

performing

international

transactions

activities

independently. Furthermore, the theory of international production (Dunning, 1988)
assumes that international involvement is driven by three main sets of explanatory
variables (i.e. ownership-specific advantages, internalisation-specific advantages, and
location-specific advantages). Ownership advantages and internalisation advantages
emanate from the firm’s internal capacity and help it towards internationalisation, whilst
the location advantage refers to the macro level advantage in the host country which the
firm enjoys, thereby enhancing its capacity to internationalise.
Moreover, the oligopolistic reaction theory (Knickerbocker, 1973) assumes that a firm
enters foreign markets to reduce the liability of being different from other competitor(s)
that enter the international market. The purpose is to reduce the risk associated with
international market involvement in case the firm makes a solo attempt and fails. The
oligopolistic reaction theory assumes that internationalisation activities are risky and so
it is better for a firm to learn from others, than to go it alone. The innovative-related
models claim that the firm’s internationalisation behaviour is based on a linearly
progressive trajectory and argues that each trajectory represents innovation for the firm

Page 39

(i.e. Bilkey & Tesar, 1977; Cavusgil, 1980; Reid, 1981). These generally accepted
theories were developed to explain the internationalisation behaviour of the large firm,
and so they barely explain the internationalisation behaviour of the small business. As a
result, the RBV, the stage theory, the network theory and the international
entrepreneurship theory are preferred in the field (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994;
Westhead et al., 2001; Hall & Cook, 2009) with regard to the internationalisation
behaviour of the small firm. Therefore, in this thesis, they are proposed and integrated
to address the topic of this thesis and are examined in detail in the following subsections.
2.4.3

The Basic Assumptions of the Proposed Integrated Theoretical Framework
for the Thesis

Each of the four theoretical frameworks selected to address the objectives of the thesis
is discussed in terms of the following seven themes: (1) the originating discipline of the
theory; (2) the alternative name (s) (i.e. whether more than two names are commonly
used to refer to the same theory); (3) the people and/or person(s) behind the theory (i.e.
its accredited writers); (4) the main assumptions of the theory with regard to the SME
export behaviour (i.e. explanatory variables); (5) the justifications for including the
theory in the thesis; (6) evidence of previous application of the theory to the SME
internationalisation phenomenon (i.e. samples of previous studies); and (7) the strengths
and/or weaknesses of the theory. Therefore, the basic assumptions of the four theoretical
frameworks are examined in turn.

Page 40

2.4.3.1

The Basic Assumptions of Stage Theories of Internationalisation

According to researchers (e.g. Andersen, 1993; Bell, 1995; Morgan & Katsikeas, 1997;
Fillis, 2002; Ruzzier et al., 2006), more than one stage theory exists. The main stage
theories are the Uppsala/process/stage theory and the innovative-related models (i.e.
Bilkey & Tesar, 1977; Cavusgil, 1980; Reid, 1981). As already mentioned above, the
innovative-related models assume that the internationalisation of the firm occurs
linearly through a progressive trajectory and contend that each trajectory represents
innovation for the firm. In this thesis, only the stage theory (Uppsala/process theory) is
selected and applied because: (1) the innovative-related models and the stage theory are
alike (i.e. they both argue that the internationalisation behaviour of the firm occurs in
stages and in a gradual succession); and (2) unlike the innovative-related models, the
stage model is more commonly applied to SME internationalisation (Bell, 1995;
Coviello & McAuley, 1999; Hall & Cook, 2009). As a result, testing and understanding
the assumptions of the stage theory in a new context (Ghana) will enable the result of
this study to be compared with a wide array of previous studies. In addition, from
Figure 2.1 it emerges as one of the theories that best explain SME export behaviour in
detail.
The stage/process theory originates from the internationalisation discipline and it is
similarly referred to as the Uppsala theory because it was first advocated by researchers
from the University of Uppsala, Sweden. Johanson and Wiedersheim-Paul (1975) and
Johanson and Vahlne (1977) proposed the stage theory. Johanson and WiedersheimPaul (1975) investigated the international growth patterns of four multinational Swedish
firms and found that the international development patterns of the firms could be
directed along certain small incremental changes. According to the researchers, these

Page 41

changes together are generally called the internationalisation processes of a firm. The
incremental changes consist of four phases, namely: (1) no regular export activity (i.e. a
firm is not interested in export business); (2) export activity starts, but through
independent representatives or agents; (3) export activity continues, but through the
focal firm’s own overseas subsidiary; and (4) internationalisation through foreign direct
investment, as the ultimate means of serving the foreign market.
Johanson and Vahlne (1977) later built on Johanson and Wiedersheim-Paul’s (1975)
work and confirmed the stages of firms’ internationalisation behaviour. They
maintained that the gradual progression is driven by a lack of prior experiential
knowledge of the foreign market. In addition, Johanson and Vahlne (1977) contend that
the gradual development of export behaviour by the firm also reflects its lack of
appropriate resources to meet the demands of international markets.
Moreover, the gradual process reflects an assumption that firms are generally risk
averse regarding the prospects of internationalisation. As a result, because of the lack of
the appropriate resources, coupled with being risk averse, firms adopt a gradual
approach and reactive approach to mitigate potential risk associated with the
international market involvement. Furthermore, the risk aversion that a firm
demonstrates towards its international market development influences it to
internationalise only into countries whose psychological and geographical features are
similar to its own (defined in the field as psychic distance – i.e., similarities in laws,
language and business practices). These assumptions of the stage theory (Johanson &
Vahlne, 1977) are presented in Figure 2.2.

Page 42

Stages

Figure 2.2 implies that a firm starts in the domestic market (with no interest in
international business), acquires experience and builds its resource base, and later
begins international business (for example by being approached to deliver export
orders) through an independent agent in the foreign market, owned sales subsidiary and
eventually foreign direct investment. From Figure 2.2, the entry into market B is shown
to be gradual because of the lack of experiential knowledge and requisite resources, but
the subsequent entry into the foreign market C is quicker because of the previous
experience gained in market B. Also the firm gravitates towards market B because it
shares geographical and psychological characteristics. Over time, the firm is able to
penetrate foreign market D because the experience it has gained in markets B and C

Page 43

gives it the confidence to enter D, even though initially this market (D) may have
seemed very distant psychologically and geographically.
Johanson and Vahlne (1977) called the changes a firm goes through on its
internationalisation trajectory as the state aspect (i.e. the stage of development that the
firm has reached in relation to internationalisation) and change aspect (the requisite
restructuring and re-strategising in relation to the resource commitment that a firm has
to make). Figure 2.3 shows such changes.
Figure 2.3: The State and Change Aspects of Internationalisation

knowledge

commitment
(state)

commitment
decisions
(change)

Figure 2.3 is a cyclical model that, reading clockwise, shows how market knowledge
influences a firm’s decisions about committing resources to its internationalisation (i.e.
the changes it makes, and how these impact on the way it performs its activities in its
current markets and the intensity of its market internationalisation (i.e. its change
aspect). In the process, the level of its market knowledge affects its market commitment
(i.e. the state aspect).

Page 44

Overall, the gradual internationalisation process, the need for prior experiential
knowledge, and the psychic distance assumptions behind the stage theory imply that
larger and older firms will be more likely to export. In addition, it also implies that
firms with older managers will be more likely to export, develop their export business,
and eventually sustain the export venture. The reason is that by having these resources
(large size, older managers, prior experience in the domestic market) a firm will
overcome the barriers to export market entry.
Furthermore, the theory implies that a local firm acquires and develops all the required
stock of resources for its export business in-house without any collaboration with
formal/contractual/business and/or information/non-contractual actors (networking
parties). Moreover, the theory implies that it is only the internal influence that counts
with regard to the SMEs’ export behaviour and the influence of the external
environment is relatively unimportant. As a result, it is these factors that predict the
time, the entry mode, the choice of foreign market, and the subsequent export
development of the SME.
Following these assumptions, implicitly, the main explanatory variables of the stage
theory: (1) the firm’s size; (2) firm’s age; (3) the age of its owner-manager; (4) its
previous experience in the domestic market; (5) its prior export experiential knowledge;
(6) the nature of the geographical and psychological situation of the country it wants to
export to; (7) its own efforts at resource development for export business; (8) gradual
export development and (9) the influence of its internal environment lead the firm to
respond positively to the triggers of export initiation and facilitate its capacity to meet
export orders. In the light of the problem statement behind the thesis, these assumptions
imply that it is these variables that explain why one SME responds positively to export

Page 45

stimuli, initiates export business and successfully meets its export orders, whereas
similar others do not, regardless of the fact that they all operate in the same industry,
same location and face the same market conditions.
Stage theory is included in this thesis as it is one of the frameworks most commonly
applied to SME internationalisation, which will allow a wide array of previous studies
to be compared. The literature offers abundant evidence of previous application of the
stage theory to SME internationalisation. Some of the previous applications of the
framework involved the following countries: (1) the USA (Brush et al., 2002), (2)
Sweden (Svante et al., 2004) and (3) the UK (Hall & Cook, 2009). In addition,
Appendix A contains additional detailed evidence of previous applications of the stage
theory to SME internationalisation.
Despite the fact that the stage model is commonly applied to SME export behaviour, it
has been heavily criticised. First, in trying to explaining exporting activities of SMEs,
the stage theory excludes the role of owner-entrepreneur behind the firm and focuses on
only the firm (Wright et al., 2007). Second, the ‘gradualism’ assumption in the stage
theory has been heavily counteracted by the ‘born’ global literature (McDougall et al.,
1994; Zahra, 2005). In addition, the stage theory’s assumption that to enter the
international market a firm must first operate in the home market is challenged by the
international entrepreneurship theory (McDougall et al, 1994; Zahra, 2005).
Furthermore, while the stage theory assumes that a firm must overcome all the barriers
to participation in the export market, before it initiates export business in that direction,
the network theory (e.g. Coviello & Munro, 1995; 1997; Gorman & Evers, 2008)
challenges that assumption, and contends that SMEs’ exporting activity is an exchange
process whereby the focal firm benefits from networking with other businesses, buyers,

Page 46

suppliers, private sector organisations and government departments, ministries and/or
agencies, thereby bringing forward the time and accelerating the pace of export market
entry. Empirically, using samples from three countries (i.e. Finland, Ireland, &
Norway), Bell (1995) failed to find support for the ‘established chains’ proposed from
the stage theory. In addition, Calof (1993), employing a Canadian sample, found an
inverse relationship between firm size and degree of internationalisation. Furthermore,
Etemad (2004) argued that the forces of change (e.g. increasing homogeneity of
markets, efficiency and lowering cost of international communication and
transportation) nowadays lead the small and medium-sized owner-managed firm to
exploit an international market which hitherto was the exclusive preserve of large
multinational firms.
2.4.3.2

The Basic Assumption of the Network Theory

According to Portes (1998), network theory originates from sociology and it is
alternatively referred to as the social capital theory. Johanson and Mattsson (1988)
explored the influence of network relations on the internationalisation of a firm,
following the stage theory (Johanson & Vahlne, 1977). Stage theory predicts that export
market development is a solo activity for a firm and as a result the required stock of
resources and knowledge for internationalisation is developed and built through its own
efforts.
Network theory, on the other hand, challenges this premise and argues that
internationalisation is a collaborative activity that makes it easier (e.g. in terms of the
cost, time, resources and pace) for a firm to enter the international market than if it was
acting alone (Johanson and Mattson, 1988). Johanson and Mattson conceptualise

Page 47

internationalisation as relationships between network partners, both in domestic and
international markets. The network members (relations and/or ties) may be firms,
business associates and/or individuals, either in the home country or in the international
market. The network relations enable SME to access new, different and abundant
resources, including export information, both locally and abroad. Therefore, whilst the
stage theory prescribes that firms must possess all the essential resource stocks in-house
before they contemplate internationalisation, the assumptions from network theory
counter that argument. The enhanced resource capacity achieved through networking
with others (e.g. business associates and social relations) counteracts the gradual
assumption in the stage theory and facilitates the decision and the pace of
internationalisation.
Moreover, the benefits gained by associating with network relations eliminate the need
for

automatic

prior

export

experiential

knowledge

before

contemplating

internationalisation, as is claimed in the stage theory. Furthermore, through the benefits
achieved from involvement in network relations, the stage theory requirement of
psychic distance also fails to be a pre-condition for the choice of the export market
destination. Furthermore, a firm’s size, its age, the age of its owner-manager and/or
managers are not the absolute determinants of its export behaviour. Overall, it is the
benefits of being in a pool of network relations that enhance the SMEs’ capacity to
respond positively to the triggers of export initiation and meet its export orders, develop
its export business and further sustain it.
Therefore, in the light of the SME export behaviour, in relation to the problem
statement behind the thesis, it can be argued that it is the benefits derived from
collaborating with network relations that enable SME to respond positively to export

Page 48

stimuli and which also facilitates its ability to meet its export orders whilst similar
competitors do not respond in this way, although they may all be operating in the same
domestic market, the same industry and face the same market conditions. Johanson and
Mattsson (1988) assumed further that the influence of network relations on international
market development consists of international extension, penetration, and international
integration. These mechanisms are represented in Table 2.2.
Table 2.2: The Mechanisms of Network Internationalisation
Stages

Mechanism

Precise description

Entering

the

foreign

through an established network

Ability to coordinate networks in
multiple countries

Source: Modified from Johanson and Vahlne (1990: 20)
The implicit assumption of the mechanisms in Table 2.2 is that psychological distance
and experiential knowledge do not wholly determine the choice of foreign market and
entry mode (see stage 1). As a result, it is the type and amount of network ties a firm
possesses which constitute its main determining factors in export business. In Stage 2,
because the involvement in the network relations has positively impacted on the firm’s
export development, the focal firm deliberately invests in its network relationships. As a
result, in stage 3, the use of the network relationships has become the norm of the firm’s

Page 49

internationalisation behaviour, and so it transcends into a variety of international
markets where the firm is involved in business.
Other researchers have extended Johanson and Mattsson’s (1988) framework SME
internationalisation, and have proposed different forms of network structures. Among
these structures are the internal and external network structures (Chetty & Agndal,
2007). According to Chetty & Agndal, the internal network structure consists of the ties
between and among staff members within the firm, while the external network structure
comprises the relation between the firm and its outside stakeholders (e.g. the customers
and the suppliers). There is also the personal and inter-organisational network structure
(Boojihawon, 2007). From Boojihawon, the personal network structure comprises the
owner-manager and his/her personal relationships (e.g. friends and family members),
whilst the inter-organisational network relations also consists of the SME’s
relationships with external stakeholders (e.g. customers, suppliers and other business
associates). A third structure is the formal and informal network structure (Gemeser et
al., 2004).
Following Gemeser et al., the formal network structure comprises the relationship
between the SME and its customers as well as suppliers, whilst the informal consists of
the relationship between the owner-manager and his friends and family members. The
final structure to discuss is the contractual and non-contractual network relationships
(Gorman & Evers, 2008). Contractual network relationships, which they also termed as
economic relationships, consist of the relation between the SME and its outside
stakeholders (e.g. the customers and suppliers agents including intermediaries, and
government agencies), whilst the non-contractual relationships (which they also termed

Page 50

non-economic relationships) comprise the relationships between the SME and its
informal/supportive relations (i.e. friends and family relations).
Generally, the structures offered by these studies are similar because they can be
summarised as comprising two main sub-divisions, namely: the formal network
relation/structure and the informal network relation/structure. Although some of the
authors use different terminologies (e.g. internal and external networks - Chetty &
Agndal, 2007; contractual and non-contractual - Gorman & Evers, 2008), they are
broadly referring to the same concepts (i.e. formal and informal structure). However, of
these studies, that of Chetty and Agndal (2007) failed to consider the informal
networking relations/structure of the entrepreneur firm (i.e. the role of the family
members and friends) in interpreting Johanson and Mattsson’s (1988) original
framework. However, the SME owner-manager’s network relations with friends and
family members are crucial to its development of internationalisation. For instance,
there is abundant evidence of the positive impact of the role of informal networking
relations on an SME’s internationalisation development (e.g. Coviello & Munro, 1997;
Meyer & Skak, 2002; Rutashobya & Jaensson, 2004).
Gorman and Evers (2008) have extended Johanson and Mattsson’s (1988)framework to
include government departments and agencies, including trade associations, further
extending the structure of the network relationship to include vertical and horizontal
relationships in more detail.
The categories and sub-categories of Gorman and Evers’ (2008) dimensions are shown
in Figure 2.4. In their framework, the only aspect they failed to include is the location of
the network relation(s). This is significant because the network relationship(s) (whether

Page 51

business or social, vertical or horizontal) can exist either in the domestic market or in
the international market or in both simultaneously. As a result, their framework is
modified in the figure below.
