Horizontal

The modified framework in Figure 2.4 implies that network structuring, whether
vertical or horizontal, can take place in the domestic country or the export destination or
both simultaneously. Overall, the main explanatory variables in network theory on the
SME export behaviour include the benefits deriving from the network relations with
business associates, agents, customers and suppliers, family members and friends,
government department/agency/ministry and local business associations, either in the

Page 52

local market or the international market or both simultaneously. It is assumed that out of
these ties flow all kinds of resources that go to enhance the SME’s capacity to export. It
also determines the time of export, the mode to choose and how to develop and sustain
the export venture. Network theory is included in the thesis because it is widely applied
to SME internationalisation field, likewise the stage theory. For instance, some studies
based on developed countries have confirmed the predictive power of network theory on
SME internationalisation behaviour (e.g. see Coviello & McAuley, 1999; Wheeler &
Harris, 2005; Sandberg & Susanne, 2008). Among the developing countries in Europe,
it is also found that the network theory facilitates SME export behaviour (see Meyer &
Skak, 2002; Ruzzier & Antoncic, 2007). Studies (e.g. Ghauri et al., 2003: Wheeler &
Harris, 2005; Sandberg & Susanne, 2008) also confirm the importance of the
framework in both the developed and developing countries.
In developing countries such as in Asia, it has also been shown that network theory
enhances the export behaviour of SMEs (Ghauri et al., 2003). For example, in Ghauri et
al.’s (2003) study, whilst the authors confirmed the importance of network theory to
SME internationalisation in developing countries (excluding Africa), they also
presented a set of other studies in their paper that confirmed their support for network
theory. The sample studies cited by Ghauri et al. in their paper are shown below.

Studies in Table 2.3 provide useful insights for investigating the impact of network
relations on internationalisation behaviour of SMEs in Africa. The reason for this is that
Ghauri et al. (2003) found similarities with regard to SMEs, whether from Asia or

Page 54

Africa, although the degree and/or magnitude may differ. Therefore, in terms of the
benefits of networking relations and their positive impact on SME export behaviour, it
can be argued that if the export behaviour of SMEs from developed and other
developing (e.g. in Europe and some parts of Asia) countries is facilitated, it seems
plausible to argue that similar firms in Africa, which are regarded as the most resource
constrained, will benefit even more from the impact of network relations on their
internationalisation behaviour. For instance, Rutashobya and Jaensson (2004) have
found evidence of the impact of the network theory (relations) on SME
internationalisation in Tanzania. In addition, the network framework is applied to the
thesis in order to resolve the weakness inherent in the stage theory with regard to its
assumptions concerning a firm’s export behaviour (e.g. the gradualism, own exports’
resource mobilisation process and the psychic distance assumptions).
As already indicated, a sample of evidence from previous applications of the network
theory to SME internationalisation comprises studies in the following countries: (1)
Scotland (McAuley, 1999); (2) New Zealand (Chetty & Holm, 2000); and (3) Ireland
(Gorman & Evers, 2008). In addition, Appendix B contains a sample of further studies
that have applied network theory to SME internationalisation.
As regards the strengths and weaknesses of network theory in the light of SME
internationalisation, numerous researchers (e.g. Coviello & Munro, 1997; Chetty &
Holm, 2000; Gemser et al., 2004; Ruzzier & Antoncic, 2007) have contended that SME
have derived much benefit in developing export activity from their diverse network
relations, depending on the market. In Table 2.4, the various impacts of network
relations on SME internationalisation are presented.

SME internationalisation development.
2.4.3.2.1

Gender & Networking

Researchers (e.g. Cromie & Birley, 1992; Ibarra, 1997; Neergaard et al., 2006) has
extended the network theory to examine gender and its effects on SME owners’
networking and how that impacts on the behaviour of SMEs. The main argument from
this line of research is that women business owners differ significantly from men with
regard to: (1) the structure of the networking they choose (e.g. whether or not women
will use formal and/or informal networking and also whether or not within either
structures women will network only with women, or only with men or both), (2) the
kind of networking interactions they will maintain (e.g. whether or not women will
differ with regard to networking intensity, frequency of interactions, activeness, and/or
will loosely engage in networking concerning intensity, interaction and activeness) and
(3) the kind of networking content they will select (i.e. whether or not women will seek
emotional support, companionship support, informational support & tangible resource
support from the network relationships). The implicit assumption from this line of
research is that networking dynamics across the gender divide affect firm development
differently with regard to positive and negative firm outcomes.

Page 57

Following on from these assumptions, some researchers (Cromie & Birley, 1992; Shaw
et al., 2001; Neergaard et al., 2005) argue that the activity level of women
owner/managers concerning networking interaction, will be weak compared to that of
men. With regard to intensity, they further argue that women business owners will have
a sparse networking base compared to men and will be less active in networking
activities, regardless of whether it is formal networking, informal networking or both.
Furthermore, the researchers argue that between the two structures, women will network
more with fellow women compared to male counterparts. In addition, they further
contend that concerning network content, women will tend to seek emotional and
companionship supports rather than business and information supports that will impact
positively on their businesses outcomes.
Though this line of research is still evolving, its empirical front is already divided. For
example, Ibarra (1997) found that women tend to choose two business networking types
(i.e. one for friendship and emotional support and another for advice and information).
Moore (1990) and Renzulli (1998) found that women business owners include more
close associates (e.g. family members’ friends) in their business networking than men.
Therefore, they fail to acquire the required business advice and key resources that can
impact on their business economically. In contrast, Aldrich et al. (1997) found that
women owner-managers are just as active, serious and active in their business
networking activities (i.e. as regards network structure, network interaction or dealing
with network content). As a result, they appear to be as successful as their male
counterparts in achieving their networking goals. In addition, Cromie & Birley (1992)
found that the propensity of women to network, the activity level of their networking
and density as well as the profile of their network contacts were remarkably similar to

Page 58

those of the men. According to Cromie & Birley (1992), women were no more likely to
consult family and friends than were men, rather, both groups appear to draw upon the
skills, wisdom, and expertise of fellow business associates, especially people with
commercial or organisational experience.
2.4.3.3

The Basic Assumptions of Resource-based View of the Firm (RBV)

The resource-based view (RBV) is based on the early economic theory of Penrose
(1959), which has since been extended by a range of other researchers (e.g. Ansoff,
1965; Wernerfelt, 1984; Prahalad & Hamel, 1990; Barney, 1991; Grant, 1991; Teece et
al., 1997; Helfat & Peteraf, 2003). Various versions of the RBV framework have been
developed. These versions include but are not limited to: (1) dynamic capability theory
(Teece et al., 1997); (2) resource scarcity theory (Castrogiovanni et al., 2006); (3) the
resource dependency theory (Pfeffer & Salancik, 1978); (4) resource-exchange theory
(Zacharakis, 1997); and (5) the resource-based view of the firm (Barney, 1991).
However, whilst these versions derive their roots and inspirations from the writings of
Penrose (1959), many (e.g. the dynamic capability theory and the resource scarcity) also
build on of Barney’s (1991) framework, which achieved widespread popularity.
Barney’s version originates from strategic management and is alternatively referred to
as the resource-based theory (RBT). The basic assumption of the RBV (Barney, 1991)
is that a firm consists of different resource stocks that are immobile, rare, nonsubstitutable and non-imitable from one firm to another. According to Barney, the
causality between these covariates (i.e. resources that are immobile, rare, nonsubstitutable and non-imitable) and the performance of a firm or a group of firms is
highly ambiguous, equivocal and opaque, even to the beneficiary firms themselves.

Page 59

Following Barney, these explanatory variables mainly account for the variations among
firms’ performance and/or competitive advantage in a given industry.
The theory further implies that it will take competitors in the same industry longer to
discover the secrets behind the leading firm’s success (competitive advantage) because
of the heterogeneity and immobility of resources the beneficiary firm(s) possess.
Therefore, whilst the possession of such resources will accrue competitive advantage to
the industry leader(s) in the short term, the industry leader (s) can still sustain this
advantage for a long period and/or in the long run because rival firms will normally take
a longer time to catch up. As a result, the leading firm (s) can achieve long-term
sustainable competitive advantage.
With regard to SMEs’ export behaviour, the RBV (Barney, 1991) implies that an
exporting SME consists of resource stocks (i.e. resources that are immobile, rare, nonsubstitutable and non-imitable). As a result, it is these unique resources that determine
variations among SMEs’ export involvement and performance. Following this theory,
the time to decide on export involvement, the type of entry mode to be used, and the
choice the export market to be selected are all based solely on the internal resource
capacity of the SMEs.
Implicitly, the framework assumes that every resource needed by the firm for its export
business development is solely achieved by its own efforts without consideration for the
impact of network partners’ resources on SME’s export behaviour. Moreover, the
theory further assumes that the determinants of SMEs’ export behaviour are wholly
internal factors, without any regard for the influence from the external environment. In
the light of the problem to be examined by this thesis, it can be argued that, from

Page 60

Barney’s (1991) perspective, the ownership of the unique resources is hypothesised as
accounting for why one SME will respond positively to export stimuli, initiate export
business and be in the position to meet its export orders whilst similar others do not.
This could be despite their operating in the same industry and location, facing the same
market conditions. In general, the main explanatory variables of the RBV on the SMEs’
export behaviour include every internal resource variable of the SME, either tangible or
intangible, including personality influence and/or characteristics of the owner-manager
behind the small firm.
Barney (1991) indicated that the peculiar resource characteristics (i.e. the immobility,
rarity, non-substitutability and non-limitability) that confer competitive advantage on a
firm cannot be tested empirically (i.e. the relationships among these factors are causally
ambiguous, as indicated above). As a result, researchers (Westhead et al., 2001; Hall &
Cook, 2009) distinguish between the strong version and the weak version of Barney’s
theory (1991). The weak version of Barney’s (1991) RBV (Westhead et al., 2001; Hall
& Cook, 2009) implies that whereas the basic assumptions of the RBV theory (Barney,
1991) remain unchanged with regard to ownership of key stock of resources, the
assumptions about its core characteristics (i.e. the immobility, rarity, nonsubstitutability and non-limitability) are relaxed in its empirical application. Therefore,
although this study extends the basic tenets of Barney’s (1991) RBV, it uses the weak
version of the RBV (e.g. Westhead et al., 200).
The RBV is incorporated into the thesis because, just like the stage theory and network
theories, it is widely applied to the incidence of SME internationalisation. For example,
in the SME internationalisation literature the argument that the export success of SMEs
resides in its internal resource capacity draws on the RBV theory (e.g. see McDougall et

Page 61

al., 1994; Bloodgood et al., 1996; Reuber & Fischer, 1997; Westhead et al., 2001;
Dhanaraj & Beamish, 2003; Ibeh, 2003; Westhead & Ucbasaran, 2004; Ibeh, 2004;
2005; Ibeh & Wheeler, 2005; Rialp et al., 2005; Ruzzier et al., 2006). In addition, these
writers have argued that unlike the stage theory, which excludes the influence of the
SME’s owner-manager’s personality when analysing its internationalisation behaviour,
the RBV considers the firm (i.e. SME) as well as the personality influence of its ownermanager in analysing its internationalisation behaviour. The prediction/assumptions of
the RBV are presented visually in Figure 2.5.

Figure 2.5: RBV Framework
---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------Internal environment
Tangible

Internal

Competitive/

-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------Source: Modified from Barney (1991: 112)

Figure 2.5 predicts that the SME’s stock of resources is developed by its own efforts
(i.e. all happening in the firm’s internal environment). These resources can be tangible
(e.g. financial capital) or intangible (e.g. brand name) and reside within the SME. It is

Page 62

the level of the resource advantage (independent of external environment/industry
influences) that enables the SME to decide whether or not to respond positively to
triggers of export initiation, meet its export orders and confer on it short- and long-term
sustainable export advantage over and above similar competitors.
Evidence of previous applications of the RBV theory to SME internationalisation
include studies from the UK (Hall & Cook, 2009), Nigeria (Ibeh, 2003a) and the USA
(Manolova et al., 2002). Furthermore, Appendix C contains a sample of additional
evidence of previous applications of the RBV to the SME’s internationalisation
behaviour.
With regard to strengths and weaknesses, the resource-based view is widely applied to
the internationalisation behaviour of the SME, and yet it remains silent on the influence
of the external environment on the SME’s export behaviour. In addition, like the stage
theory, it remains silent and fails to account for the benefits the SME may derive from
networking with various network relations (e.g. business associates, government
departments, industry associations, family and friends). Moreover, it is argued that the
resource characteristics outlined by Barney (1991) are abstract because their sources are
difficult to ascertain, a problem Barney himself indicated (Helfat & Peteraf, 2003). In
spite of such limitations, the RBV model is a framework that has been frequently
applied to the SME internationalisation phenomenon (Peng, 2001).

Table 2.6 shows various definitions of IE applied by different authors and also confirm
the increasing recognition of the phenomenon amongst researchers. With regard to the
SME internationalisation, the basic assumption of IE theory is that certain unique
entrepreneurial behavioural characteristics cause some SME to enter the international
market at their inception. In addition, the framework implies that the time to enter the
international market, the nature of entry mode to be used, the choice of export market to
be selected and subsequent development of the export business are driven by
exceptional entrepreneurial factors to spot export business opportunities. In relation to
the problem statement the thesis seeks to investigate, the IE assumption implies that it is

Page 67

the possession of these unique entrepreneurial characteristics that determines why some
owner-manager-entrepreneurs enter the foreign market while similar others remain
focus on the domestic market, although they may all be operating in the same industry
and location, and face the same market conditions (McDougall et al., 1994; Oviatt &
McDougall, 1994).
Excessive alertness to profitable international opportunities, positive international
attitudes and international orientation, international business networking behaviour,
excessive proclivity for international ideas, a high need for international achievement,
strong personal drive, ambition and motivation for internationalisation and international
risk-taking behaviour are among the unique entrepreneurial behavioural characteristics
that set internationally entrepreneurial small firms apart from ordinary small
entrepreneurship firms and explain the speed of internationalisation (McDougall et al.,
1994; Oviatt & McDougall, 1995; Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki, 2003).
Initially, it was assumed that internationally entrepreneurial small firms were mostly
found in the USA in the highly technological and knowledge-intensive sectors. This
suggests that the nature of a firm’s product and the industry it operates in are parts of
the explanatory variables of the event. In recent times, there has been a great deal of
evidence to show that the phenomenon is not country or industry-specific (Oviatt &
McDougall, 1994; Zahra & George, 2002b). For example, McDougall et al. (1994) find
that the geographical coverage of the event has been extended to cover Europe, North
America, South America, Asia, the Middle East and the South Pacific.

Page 68

In addition, McDougall et al. found that among the high-tech businesses, there were
also firms from the aquaculture and services sectors. An extensive review of 38 papers
by Rialp et al. (2005) on international entrepreneurship events between 1993 and 2003
found that 15 studies focused on firms outside the high-tech sectors, with samples from
Australia, the US, Canada, Switzerland, Ireland, New Zealand, the UK, Germany,
France, Spain, Israel, Denmark, Sweden, Norway and Finland.
With regard to the entrepreneurialism assumptions associated with the IE theory, an ongoing debate concerning the framework suggests that although Oviatt and McDougall’s
(1994) assumptions (e.g. IE firms internationalise at inception, are mostly from the
highly intensive technological sectors etc) are widely accepted, researchers (e.g. Zahra
& George, 2000b; Zahra, 2005; Zahra et al., 2005) argue that entrepreneurial actions
found to be associated to the event cannot be limited to a particular firm’s age (new
SMEs), size (small and/or medium-sized firms) and the time of a firm’s international
market entry (e.g. internationalisation at inception). Other researchers (e.g. Zahra &
George, 2000b; Knight & Cavusgil, 2004; Zahra, 2005; Ruzzier et al., 2006) concur
with the argument and state that entrepreneurial attributes (e.g. (1) international vision,
(2) international risk-taking behaviour and (3) international opportunity identification
(among others) as indicated for example by McDougall et al. (1994) cannot be limited
to the specific age of a firm (e.g. the born global), the specific size of a firm or the
specific industry (e.g. the highly technological and knowledge-intensive sector). As a
result, Dimitratos and Plakoyiannaki (2003) argue that entrepreneurial behaviours are
part of an organisational-wide process that is embedded in the culture of a firm.
Dimitratos and Plakoyiannaki, imply that the event can happen both in the small and the
large firm.

Page 69

As can be seen from Table 2.6, because of the criticisms from researchers (e.g.,
Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki, 2003), McDougall and her colleagues have continued to
revise their definitions in recent years (1994, 1997, 2000 and 2005). As a result, the
current argument on the phenomenon is that although the entrepreneurialism
assumption (entrepreneurial behaviour) behind the phenomenon is accepted, it is not
limited to a particular firm. Consensus has been reached among researchers that the
event is not limited to a firm’s age, size, country and sector, although the ideal small
and medium-sized new venture firm from the high technology sector may exist
(McDougall & Oviatt, 2000; Zahra & George, 2000b; Oviatt & McDougall, 2005;
Zahra et al., 2005). Whereas it is argued that the event is not limited only to SME, in
this thesis, the event is applied only to SME as this constitutes the area of interest.
Extra characteristics of the international entrepreneurship event concerning the SME as
identified by other researchers are presented in Appendix D.
Moreover, Andersson (2004) and Etemad (2004) argue that in addition to the
entrepreneurial behavioural characteristics associated with the IE phenomenon, as
discussed above, the event is also facilitated by environmental factors such as
technological advancement in international transportation and communication and the
removal of trade barriers. The justification for incorporating the IE into the thesis is that
although it is argued that the RBV takes care of the influence of the SME ownermanager’s personality and its impact on SME export behaviour, it is also arguable that
personality factors and/or the influence of the owner-manager of SME and their impact
on export behaviour can be understood in detail from the perspective of
entrepreneurship (McDougall et al., 1994; Ruzzier et al., 2006).Furthermore, it is found
that one of the most important variables behind SME internationalisation is the owner-

Page 70

manager (McDougall et al., 1994; Lloyd-Reason & Mughan, 2002; Ibeh, 2005). As a
result, because the thesis focuses on the entrepreneurial firm, it is thought that any
framework that focuses specifically on the influence of the entrepreneur owner-manager
of the SME and its internationalisation behaviour cannot be left out.
Although the IE event is associated with the speed of internationalisation, sector (e.g.
high technology industries), products (e.g. software products), developed countries (e.g.
the USA), new small and medium-sized firms (e.g. international new venture firms), the
time of internationalisation (e.g. internationalisation at inception) and the unique
entrepreneurial characteristics as discussed above, the thesis examines only the
entrepreneurial behaviour characteristics associated with the event. So it seeks to
explore the extent to which the entrepreneurial behaviours associated with the event
would trigger initiation of export activity by SMEs from Ghana, and the extent to which
they would facilitate the capacity of the firms to meet their export orders.
The entrepreneurial behavioural assumptions explored in this thesis are in line with the
findings by researchers (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Oviatt and McDougall, 1994;
Oviatt & McDougall, 1995; McAuley, 1999; Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki, 2003).
Previous studies that apply the IE theory to SMEs internationalisation research include
countries like the USA (Knight & Cavusgil, 2004) and Finland (Autio et al., 2000;
Wickramasekera & Bamerry, 2003). Additional evidence of previous applications of the
IE theory to SMEs internationalisation is presented in detail in Appendix E.
In relation to its strengths and weaknesses, for example, IE theory was initially
criticised on the grounds that the entrepreneurial behaviour associated with the
phenomenon cannot be limited to small new venture firms (as already mentioned

Page 71

above), but it is a behaviour that can be manifested in the domestic firm, international
firm, the SME as well as the large multinational corporations. Another of its strengths is
that this framework has the capacity to capture the personality influence of the ownermanager of a SME in detail when explaining its export behaviour.

2.5

LIMITATIONS OF THE PROPOSED INTEGRATED THEORETICAL
FRAMEWORK TO BE ADDRESSED BY THE THESIS

As can be seen from the above, none of the theoretical frameworks discussed are
without their limitations, but this thesis does not seek to resolve all the issues. However,
by examining the research questions behind the thesis, it seeks to address the following
limitations. First, stage theory predicts that: (1) a firm’s export behaviour is gradual, (2)
a firm internationalises into only psychologically close countries, (3) a firm’s resource
mobilisation towards its internationalisation activity is achieved by its own efforts and
(4) that the influence on a firm towards export activity derives wholly from its internal
environment, with no consideration given to the impact of the external environment.
Furthermore, stage theory focuses on the firm and excludes the owner-manager behind
it in analysing its export behaviour.
Second, the RBV theory also fails to consider the influence of the external environment
(e.g. the role of industrial associations & government’s departments) on the SME’s
export success. In addition, the RBV framework fails to consider the influence of
network partners’ resources, which can sustain export business where the firm’s own
capacity is exhausted and/or inadequate. Furthermore, RBV also fails to account for the
importance of the external environment on the SMEs’ export behaviour. It is assumed in

Page 72

this thesis that these limitations, associated with these two theoretical frameworks in
particular, limit in-depth understanding of the event under study. As a result, it is
expected that the integration of other theoretical frameworks (i.e. network and
contingency frameworks) to these frameworks in this thesis, with the evidence from
Ghana, would help resolve these weaknesses and shed fuller light on the topic.

2.6 JUSTIFICATION FOR THE PROPOSED INTEGRATED THEORETICAL
FRAMEWORK BEHIND THE THESIS

Although some studies (e.g., Bell, 1995; Coviello & Munro, 1995; Reuber & Fischer,
1997; Moen & Servais, 2002) apply a single theoretical framework to analyse the
SME’s internationalisation behaviour, this thesis departs from that line of research. The
thesis builds on the argument that the internationalisation behaviour of the SME
signifies a complex phenomenon that cannot be fully accounted for by any single
theoretical framework. As a result, the thesis builds on the work of researchers (e.g.
Coviello & Martin, 1999; Crick & Spence, 2005; Coviello & Martin, 2006; Rialp et al.,
2006; Hall & Cook, 2009) who apply more than one theoretical framework and thus
adopt a similar line of argument in their respective studies on the topic. Moreover, it is
stressed that an integrated theoretical framework offers greater potential to explain the
SMEs’ export behaviour than a single theoretical framework. As a result, it is not
uncommon to find a statement such as:
We are better able to understand the internationalisation process of
small businesses by expanding our research focus to include the models
of incremental internationalisation with the network perspectives.
(Coviello & Munro, 1997: 379)

Page 73

There is considerable justification for applying more than one theoretical framework.
For instance, apart from the fact that the event constitutes a complex phenomenon, none
of the theories is without weakness and hence integration offers mitigation of their
individual limitations. Previous studies have applied more than one theoretical
framework to address the topic in the following ways: (1) three integrated theoretical
frameworks (i.e. the network theory, RBV and international entrepreneurship theory)
(Coviello & Cox, 2006); (2) three integrated theoretical frameworks (i.e. the FDI
theory, stage theory and network theory) (McAuley, 1999); (3) three integrated
theoretical frameworks (i.e. the RBV, network theory and contingency theory) (Crick &
Spence, 2005); and (4) two integrated theoretical frameworks (i.e. the RBV and stage
theory) (Hall & Cook, 2009). Appendix F contains further synthesis of studies that have
used integrated theoretical frameworks to address the event in detail.
In this thesis, four theoretical frameworks are integrated and applied, since most studies
that address the topic employ one or more of the four selected theoretical frameworks
(see e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Bell, 1995; Coviello & Munro, 1995; Oviatt &
McDougall, 1995; Covielo & Munro, 1997; Reuber & Fischer, 1997; McAuley, 1999;
Westhead et al., 2001; Moen & Servais, 2002; Reuber & Fischer, 2002; Ibeh, 2003;
Dimitratos & Plakoyiannki, 2003; 2004; Gemser et al., 2004; Knight & Cavusgil, 2004;
Ibeh, 2005; Ibeh & Wheeler, 2005; Boojihawon, 2007; Ruzzier & Antoncic, 2007; Hall
& Cook, 2009; Williams, 2009; Zhang et al., 2009)..
The factors in the four theoretical frameworks are integrated in such a way as to provide
the thesis with a streamlined and robust line of argument. Overall, it is proposed in this
thesis that the light shed by the integrated theoretical framework on the SME export
behaviour will be deeper than could be achieved using more limited alternatives.

Page 74

Finally, it is assumed in the thesis that full, detailed understanding of the event could
not be achieved, even with application of the four selected frameworks, unless the
strategies (planned or unplanned) of the SME are considered in the light of the changing
trends of the external environment. Consequently, the contingency framework is applied
in the thesis to supplement the integrated theoretical framework in order to address any
influences emanating from the external environment that impact on the SME export
behaviour.

2.7

CONTINGENCY THEORY

Contingency theory originates from the organisational management discipline. Kuada
and Sorensen (2000) credit the writings on contingency theory to Lawrence and Lorsch
(1967). According to Kuada and Sorensen (2000), Lawrence and Lorsch (1967) argue
that there cannot be an independent emergent and/or strategic approach by a firm,
because the structure of the environment and its conditions directly or indirectly shape a
firm’s strategy. As a result the firm adapts either directly or indirectly to the changing
trends of the environment, popularly called organisational adaptation and/or strategic fit.
With regard to the contingency theory and the SME’s internationalisation behaviour, the
contingency theory’s main assumption is that the internationalisation behaviour of the
SME is dynamic and multifarious because it is partly dependent on the firm’s capacity
and partly the environmental conditions (e.g. the demand condition, type of industry,
industry structure, general technological development and government programmes)
(Ibeh, 2003b; Crick & Spence, 2005; Li et al., 2004). Implicitly, although the

Page 75

environmental factors are exogenous to the focal SME, the SME’s behaviour is
consequently shaped directly or indirectly (formal or emergent) by the contingency
factors. Therefore, it is assumed that it is the forces of the four theoretical frameworks,
coupled with the contingency factors, which determine the SME’ capacity to respond
positively to triggers of export initiation and enable the SME to meet its export orders
and subsequently to develop and sustain its export business.
In terms of the problem statement the thesis seeks to examine, the contingency theory’s
assumptions imply that the ability of one SME to adapt the influences and requirements
of the external environment to its internal environment, whilst similar others cannot do
so, regardless of the fact that they all operate in the same industry, location and face the
same market conditions, would explain why that firm exports while the others do not.
Sample variables of the external environment include: competitor action, government
action, industry influence, technology and the influence of trade associations.
From the studies reviewed in this study, it can be assumed that insufficient research has
as yet been undertaken on the impact of the external environment on SME export
behaviour. This observation confirms the call by Sousa et al. (2008) for researchers to
advance knowledge in that area. Application of the contingency theory in this thesis
increases the robustness of the proposed integrated framework through seeking to
address some of the weaknesses in the RBV and the stage theory discussed above
(Section 2.5). For instance, the assumptions of contingency theory counteract certain
predictions made of RBV and stage theory by Barney (1991) and Johanson and Vahlne
(1977) respectively, as the two frameworks assume that the export success of a firm
emanates wholly from factors relating to its internal resource capacity.

Page 76

2.8

SELECTED VARIABLES BASED ON THE PROPOSED INTEGRATED
THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK

Drawing on the assumptions of the five proposed theoretical frameworks integrated to
guide the thesis, the following variables, namely (1) foreign ownership structure, (2)
firm size, (3) firm age and (4) human capital (i.e. the aggregate education level of fulltime employees), are examined in detail. These five main variables form the basis of
statistical analysis of the thesis (Chapter 5). They are discussed in turn.

2.8.1

Ownership Structure and Export Propensity

The body of studies in this area focus on the relationship between the ownership
structure of the SME and its influence on whether or not the firm will export. Within
this body of research, different dimensions of ownership structure are researched. Some
researchers study the influence of family ownership versus corporate ownership, and
whether or not this would lead the SME to export (Fernández & Nieto, 2006). Another
group (Hall & Tu, 2003) research the effect of independent ownership (e.g. managed by
the owner (s)) and subsidiary ownership (e.g. corporate ownership) and whether or not
the SME would invest abroad (Hall & Tu, 2003). Moreover, others have examined
public ownership (public limited liability) versus private ownership (private limited
liability), and its influence on the export propensity of the SME (Javalgi et al., 2000). In
addition, some have also examined the different institutional investors and their
influence on the SMEs’ scope to pursue international activities (Tihanyi et al., 2003;
George et al., 2005; Fernández & Nieto, 2006).

Page 77

Further to this, other researchers have investigated wholly domestic ownership versus
partial domestic ownership (i.e. partly foreign-owned and partly locally-owned), and
their influence on the export decisions of the SME (Cerrato & Piva, 2007). This present
study extends the latter line of research and examines the influence of wholly domestic
ownership versus partial domestic ownership on the export decisions of SMEs. Unlike
other studies (e.g. Javalgi et al., 2000; Tihanyi et al., 2003; Hall & Tu, 2003; George et
al., 2005), which examine the effect of a particular ownership structure (e.g. wholly
public ownership and wholly private ownership) on the SME export decision, the
uniqueness of the latter line of research is that it examines the combination of two
ownership structures and its effect on the SMEs’ international decisions (i.e. the hybrid
ownership structure). It is argued in this thesis that the sum of such a synergistic effect
will therefore be more than the effect of the individual ownership structures which the
previous studies above examine.

For instance, as a result of the importance of synergy between foreign partners and
indigenous local firms and its positive influence on the SME’s propensity to export,
most governments develop policies which attract foreign firms to invest in the host
country. Tax allowances and the lifting of duty as well as investment allowances are
among the policies most governments use to attract foreign investors to their countries
(Greenaway et al., 2004). Although studies have been carried out on the general effect
of foreign partners’ participation (spillover effect) in the host country, as regards the
activities of the indigenous firms, they mainly focus on the developed world. Therefore,
in this study, the debate is extended to cover Ghana since the government of Ghana has
implemented various policies over the years which seek to attract foreign investors to
the country (Abor & Harvey, 2008).

Page 78

Foreign firms which operate in various host countries augment the local firms’ resource
capacity with new technology, physical capital, management expertise, international
market knowledge and global distribution network opportunities (Zhang &
Felmingham, 2001). Moreover, while these advantages benefit the host country’s firms,
the foreign firms also gain, since in developing countries (like those of sub-Saharan
Africa for instance), they have access to cheap labour, land and raw materials. However,
most of the benefits will accrue to the local firms, as in Africa most firms are SMEs and
are more resource constrained than their counterparts in the developed world.

Kneller & Pisu (2007) classified the benefits which accrue to host country firms as a
result of partnerships with foreign businesses in terms of productive spillovers and
export spillovers. On one hand, as a result of the competition between the foreign firms
and the indigenous firms, the capacity of the indigenous firms is indirectly strengthened
(i.e. productive spillovers). On the other hand, some of the activities of the foreign firms
(e.g. partnership and/or alliances) will directly influence some of the indigenous firms
to initiate export business (export spillovers).
Despite the attention this line of research is gaining, its empirical front is divided. In a
recent study, Kneller and Pisu (2007) used a British manufacturing sample from 1992 to
1999, and considered whether export spillovers flow from foreign firms to the
indigenous UK’s firms. They found that the export decisions of the UK’s domestic
firms do not seem to be influenced by the contacts they may have with foreign
multinational enterprises, this being consistent with their 2005 paper (Kneller & Pisu,
2005). Barrios et al. (2003) studied a Spanish sample from 1990 to 1998 to investigate
the effect of foreign multinationals’ activities on the export behaviour of firms in Spain.

Page 79

In this study, the researchers failed to find evidence to support the proposition that
export spillover emanates from foreign firms’ activities to the domestic firms.
Likewise, Ruane & Sutherland (2005) studied an Irish sample from 1991 to 1998 to
examine the effect of similar influences; they found that the export intensity of foreign
owned enterprises was negatively associated with the export propensity and intensity of
Irish domestic owned firms. It would appear that the negative evidence above
discourages governments from developing policies to attract foreign direct investment
into their countries, but other reports exist to counter the above evidence. For instance,
Greenaway et al. (2004) studied a UK sample from 1992 to 1996 to analyse a similar
proposition; however, the authors found a strong association between the activities of
multinational firms and the export propensity of the UK firms.
In addition, Cerrato & Piva (2007) used 1,324 cross-sectional samples from Italy and
found that the presence of foreign partners among SMEs in Italy is positively associated
with their export propensity. Similarly, Aitken et al. (1997) researched 2,104 plants
from Mexico, from 1986–1990 to investigate the same association. In that study, the
researchers found that the foreign firms served as significant catalysts for the decisions
of Mexican firms to export. Furthermore, there has been some argument about whether
or not China’s export growth has been partly influenced by foreign investment flows
into the country. Following this debate, Zhang & Song (2000) found strong evidence to
show that the activities of foreign firms promote the successful export decisions of
Chinese firms, maintaining that the influence impacted on China’s export boom.

Page 80

Additionally, Sojöholm & Takii (2008) focused on 26,987 plants in Indonesia between
1990 and 2000 to examine the impact of foreign ownership and the export propensity of
Indonesian SMEs. The researchers found that plants in the Indonesian manufacturing
sector with foreign ownership were substantially more likely to start exporting than
wholly domestic-owned plants. Niringiye et al. (2010) found that in Uganda, firms with
foreign ownership were more likely to export. Rankin et al. (2006) also found that
foreign ownership predicted the propensity to export from sub-Saharan Africa. Using a
sample from Africa, Clark (2005) found that foreign ownership significantly predicts
the export involvement of firms from Africa. In a study on Slovenia and Estonia by
Matija et al. (2001), the researchers found evidence to show that foreign ownership
matters most as far as the export propensity of domestic firms is concerned.
In recent years, the government of Ghana has implemented policies which aim to attract
foreign investors. These policies include the Ghana Freezone Policy and the
establishing of the Ghana Investment Promotion Council (GIPC) (Abor et al., 2008). In
the wake of these measures, it is reported that between 1994 and 2002, the Ghana
Investment Promotion Centre registered 1,309 foreign direct investment projects
(FD1s). According to Abor et al. (2008), the project distribution among the various
sectors consisted of: the service sector - 388 projects; the manufacturing sector - 368
projects; the tourism sector - 153 projects; the building and construction sector - 106
projects and the agriculture sector - 105 projects. Of the 1,309 projects, the authors
report that 912 were joint foreign-Ghanaian ownerships, while 397 were wholly
foreign-owned. As a consequence of the above investment flow to Ghana, it can
therefore be argued that similar export spillovers will accrue to Ghanaian local firms.

Page 81

Hence, in line with the assumptions of the resource-based view (i.e. the total human
capital resource capacity the foreign partners (management team) bring to bear on the
indigenous firms’ capacity) and the network theory (i.e. networking/partnering with
foreign business people), the contributions by which foreign firms augment the capacity
of the domestic firms will place the beneficiary firms at an advantage. As a result, the
new level of resource capacity of the local firms will put these firms in a more
advantageous position to respond positively to export stimuli, organise themselves to
initiate export business and meet their export orders. The relationship between foreign
participation in the host country’s enterprises and their propensity to export will
therefore be explored statistically in Chapter 5 of the thesis.

2.8.2

Firm Size and Export Propensity

The assumptions of the resource-based view of the firm and the stage theory of
internationalisation stress the importance of the ownership of essential resource stocks,
commonly defined in the literature as the size of the firm and/or the productive capacity
of the firm (Calof, 1993; Obben & Magagula, 2003; Hall & Cook, 2009). The RBV and
stage theory predict that larger firms will be more likely to export because they will
possess much greater resource capacity, enabling them to serve the international market:
unlike SME that are resource constrained. Of these two theories, stage theory in
particular stresses the bulk of a firm’s resource capacity, while the resource-based view
focuses not only on the bulk, but also on the nature of the resource stocks (i.e. their
quality). As a result, both theories predict that an adequate volume of vital resource
capacity must precede initiation and subsequent development of export business.

Page 82

Although the empirical association between firm size and the probability of exporting
has been researched extensively, there is not complete concurrence among the
outcomes. This is illustrated by Banoccorsi (1992), who reviewed and synthesised
proportions of the previous research concerning the predictors of export propensity prior
to 1992; Table 2.7 summarises the papers.

Table 2.7: Summary of Findings Concerning the Effect of a Firm’s Size on its

The findings in Table 2.7 reflect divided positions. Some studies found no relationship
between size and export behaviour while others found a strong relationship. Further,
Bonaccorsi (1992) concluded that his empirical survey on Italian firms supported the
positive association between size and export propensity. More recently, Hall & Cook
(2009) sampled 74 firms in the UK and tested the effect of size on whether or not the

Page 83

small firm would export; but found no significant results to support either effect. In
addition, Calof (1993) used 38 samples from Canada to conduct a similar examination.
Here it was concluded that size did not appear to limit a firm’s ability to initiate export
business. However, large firms in particular demonstrated a higher propensity to export
than their smaller counterparts. Furthermore, Philip (1998) sampled 280 manufacturing
firms in Australia and investigated the effect of the very small firm (VSF) and the
likelihood of its export propensity. In this study, it was concluded that the size variable
was not a significant discriminator of export propensity and that it made very little
contribution in explaining probability of export in Australia.

It seems paradoxical that the firm’s size, which represents its productive capacity, might
not be a predictor of a decision to export, and in contrast, Hall & Tu (2004), in studying
42,721 plants from the UK in 19 sub-sectors, found a strong association between size
and export propensity. Mittelstaedt et al. (2003) sampled 2,822 plants from 49 different
industries in the USA and also found a significant relationship between size and export
propensity. They concluded that, regardless of a firm’s productivity level, capital
intensity, product and service characteristics, size constitutes a significant discriminator
of the export propensity of firms.

Here, the authors recommended a minimum

workforce size of 20 employees as the threshold for firms that intend to consider export
business. Obben and Magagular (2003) undertook a similar investigation into
manufacturing SMEs in Swaziland, in the course of which they found a significant
effect between size and the propensity to export. Latterly, Mittelstaedt & Ward (2006)
followed up their earlier study in 2003; they sampled 43,707 manufacturing SMEs
located across eight states in the USA and found support for the effect of size on export

Page 84

propensity, consistent with their 2003 findings. However, they maintained that smaller
firms with less than 20 employees could export if they were located in urban centres.

Furthermore, in another recent study, Poff et al. (2008), although they agreed that the
larger firm is more likely to export than the SME, challenged the initial threshold
requirement of 20 employees of Mittelstaedt et al. (2003) and contended that the SMEs
– even those with zero or one employee – were capable of exporting. As a result of the
positive evidence and the counter findings above, it appears that more research is
needed in order to establish the existence and the direction of such a relationship. In
fact, the variations concerning the construct measurements with regard to the actual
definition of size could be one of the major causes of division on the empirical front.
For example, while in some studies micro firms are defined as those having nine or
fewer employees (e.g. Philip, 1998), in other studies the definition used can be fewer
than two (e.g. Obben and Magagular (2003).

Inconsistencies also occur in studies which use a sales value as the proxy for size, with
regard to the exact definition of figures (see Calof, 1993). Furthermore, Soderbom &
Teal (2003), using macro and micro evidence from nine African countries, found that
the most important factor in predicting whether or not a firm will export both regionally
and internationally is its size.

Regardless of the above divisions on the empirical front concerning the effect of size
and the propensity to export, the theoretical and intuitive reasons behind the hypothesis
remain strong because of the fixed cost element associated with export business. Hall &
Tu (2004) and Mittelstaedt et al. (2003) argue that the dimension of the fixed cost
attached to export business justifies the need to build the firm’s size, this being an

Page 85

indicator of its productivity, especially prior to initiating export business. Mittelstaedt et
al. (2003) contended that the cost of International Standard Organisation (ISO) training,
which appeared to be compulsory for firms planning to internationalise, ranged from
US$30,000 to US$750,000 in South Carolina, USA. According to the authors, such a
pre-condition may be difficult for the SMEs to bear.

As a result, following the economies of scale and fixed cost assumptions, it seems
reasonable to argue that the size of the firm is an important determining factor of
whether or not a firm will export. The economies of scale assumption implies that the
impact of fixed costs will be lesser on firms that are larger (e.g. due to their large
activity/sales levels and also profitability). This proposition is important to this study’s
context because in Ghana firms tend to be extremely small and medium-sized compared
to similar firms in developed economies. As a result, firms in Ghana will have to ensure
that they are large enough to meet the demands of the international market in order to
fund the search and negotiation costs associated with international business. Such a test
could be of benefit to both policy makers and practitioners. Based on the argument
above, the relationship between the size of the firm and its propensity to enter the export
market will be examined statistically in chapter 5 of the thesis.

2.8.3

The Age of the Firm and the Propensity to Export

Stage theory and the RBV theory provide considerable support for the positive effect of
a firm’s age on its export propensity. The resource-based view predicts that the
ownership of important resources accounts for the variations in firms’ performance in
an industry, whether domestic or abroad. In addition, stage theory predicts the need for
prior experiential knowledge in the export business and also predicts that a firm can take

Page 86

a long time to accrue experiential knowledge. What can be implied here is that the two
theories predict that older firms will possess more experience (resources) than new and
younger firms in the same industry.
As with the size of the firm and its ownership structure (sub-Section 2.8.1), the
empirical outcomes regarding the effect of age and whether or not the SME will export
also vary. Zahra (2005) and Schulz et al. (2009) argue that the unusual presence of
small and medium-sized new venture firms in the international market at their inception
challenge the assumption of a positive effect of the age of a firm on its propensity to
export (i.e. as claimed by the stage theory). McDougall et al. (1994) examined the
behaviour of new international venture firms and found that the new small and mediumsized venture firms do not follow the gradualism hypothesis suggested by the stage
theory, which indicates that younger firms are unlikely to export. Moen and Servias
(2002) examined the export behaviour of small and medium-sized enterprises in
Norway, Denmark and France. In this study, the authors did not find evidence to
support the proposition that export market decisions are influenced by the age of the
firm. Similarly, Bell (1995) undertook a cross-national investigation in Finland, Ireland
and Norway of the gradual export development assumption in the stage theory, but
failed to support the hypothesis. In addition, Wolff & Pett (2000) used sample of 157
firms from the US to examine the link between the gradualism hypothesis and the
export behaviour of the SME, but also failed to find support for the hypothesis.
Andersson et al. (2004) investigated a set of contingent factors and their impact on the
international activities of small businesses. In their study, using data from Sweden, the
authors found that the number of years a firm had been in existence was not a
significant determinant of the level of its internationalisation. Some researchers argue

Page 87

that, as firms grow in years, they face structural inertia, which makes them relax, even
as regards decisions on export business (Autio, 2000; Williams, 2006).
Etemad (2004) contends that globalisation forces have rendered irrelevant the criterion
adopted by the stage theory of the time which firms have to wait before they gain the
necessary experience enter the export market. That is, although some firms may follow
stages, the stages are now shorter. According to Etemad (2004), because of
globalisation, firms have access to a constant stream of diverse information, which
counters the gradualism hypothesis in the stage theory; as a result, age may not count in
the decision as to when to enter the export market. Hall & Cook (2009), using a UK
sample, also found that age was not a significant predictor of export propensity.
Boojihawon (2007) and Ruzzier & Antoncic (2007) further argue that the gradual
process of resource acquisition and learning do not hold since network ties enable SMEs
to access a wide range of resources and consequently to enter the export market at a
faster pace.
In contrast, Brouthers and Nakos (2005), using a Greek sample, found that older firms
were associated with a greater level of export activity than younger firms. Javalgi et al.
(2000) sampled 20,204, manufacturing firms from the US and tested the effect of firm
age on the propensity to export; here they found support for the effect of age on the
firm’s propensity to export. In this study, the authors contended that both at the
aggregate and firm level, the increase in age increased the propensity to export. As a
result, despite the division of this proposed association, it is theoretically and intuitively
plausible to argue that a firm’s years of practice in an industry constitute its stock of
knowledge and experience (resources). Therefore, drawing on the resource-based view

Page 88

of the firm as well as the stage theory, the relationship between the age of the firm and
whether or not it will export will also be examined empirically in the thesis.
2.8.4

Human Capital and Export Propensity

Within the internationalisation research field, the effect of human capital on the export
decisions of the small business is contentious. Various lines of research exist in this
debate.

Generally, human capital refers to factors related to the owner-manager’s

personality and the impact this has on the firm’s export behaviour. This perspective is
popular in the literature (see Manolova, 2002; Ruzzier et al., 2007; Hall & Cook, 2009).
Dimensions of the entrepreneur’s human capital that have received recent attention
include the entrepreneur’s international business skill, international business travel and
orientation, management know-how and perception of the level of environmental risk
(Manolova, 2002; Ruzzier et al., 2007).
In addition, Hall & Cook (2009), drawing on a UK sample, added the following human
capital dimensions of the entrepreneur: (1) the possession or lack of a degree – which
they defined as the entrepreneur’s education; (2) the years of previous business
experience; (3) previous managerial experience in the export firm, and (4) proficiency
in one additional foreign language. Other researchers have used some of the above
dimensions and combined them with other variables, depending on the research
question they wished to address; in particular, the entrepreneur’s education level, gender
and age have received attention (Obben & Magagula, 2003; Orser et al., 2009).
In contrast, some researchers have shifted the focus away from the owner-manager’s
human capital dimensions and examined instead the human capital dimensions of the
management team from the entrepreneurial firm and its influence on the SME export

Page 89

behaviour (Reuber & Fischer, 1997; Hutchinson & Quinn, 2006). Another group of
researchers have turned their attention to the human capital of the general workforce of
the entrepreneurial firm and its effect on the firm’s propensity to export. This line of
research is motivated by the argument that workers’ skills and competence are linked to
their levels of education and, therefore, the higher the quality of the workforce, the more
likelihood there is that the entrepreneurial firm will export (Bernard & Jensen, 2004;
Alvarrez, 2007; Cerrato & Piva, 2007).
The present study extends the latter line of research, arguing that in respect of this
information, neither age nor one person nor a group of persons (e.g., the entrepreneur
and/or his top management team) can be the sole major factor behind a firm’s success.
Therefore, successful organisations in the international market will be those that draw
on the skill set of the entire workforce and not solely on one entrepreneur or the
management team.
Following Varga (2000), Audretsch & Thurik (2001) and Audretsch et al. (2005),
workforce human capital and its influence to enhance a firm’s decision to enter the
export market can be said to be linked to the knowledge spillover theory. Knowledge
spillover theory concerns the direct and indirect transfer of knowledge from one firm to
another (Audretsch & Thurik, 2001; Audretsch et al., 2005; Gilbert et al., 2008). The
spillover theory assumes that innovative activities of firms (e.g. technological
innovation, innovative information about new products or services and innovation about
organisational processes and routines) spill over to other firms, which impacts
positively on the recipient firms’ behaviour, including export decisions. The beneficiary
firms may be in the same industry, operate as clusters, operate in the same location or
sometimes may be operating in regional centres. Studies (e.g. Almeida, 1996; Lee et al.,

Page 90

2000; Fabel et al., 2002) contend that the primary means for knowledge spillover within
a geographic region is through employees’ mobility. Lee et al. (2000) argue that
workforce mobility from firm to firm facilitates knowledge spillover; consequently, the
whole nation gains as knowledge spreads throughout the regions. In relation to export
propensity, it can be argued that workers who have worked previously in export
companies are likely to carry over their tacit knowledge into the current firm, thereby
facilitating the firm’s export decision.
This line of research is not yet popular in the field, but some empirical evidence does
exist. For example, Bernard & Jensen (2004) used a panel of US manufacturing plants
to examine factors which increased the probability of the firm’s entry into the export
market. In the study, the authors found that the quality of the workforce was defined in
terms of high average wage and level of white collar employment, which were
significantly correlated with the probability of exporting.
In addition, Alavarex (2006), using a sample from Chile, found that firms with qualified
workers were more able to enter the export market. Furthermore, Cerrato & Piva (2007),
using a sample of 1,324 SMEs in Italy, discovered that the number of graduates in the
workforce, in relation to the firm’s total number of employees, positively and
significantly influences the decision of SMEs to export. Gilbert et al. (2008) confirmed
the knowledge spillover hypothesis, using a US sample. Therefore, following the RBV
of the firm, organisations with an educated workforce will possess a greater variety of
skills and abilities than similar firms which do not possess such an educated workforce.
This diversity of skills of the educated workforce will then enable the entrepreneur firm
to respond positively to the export stimuli and enter the export market. In accordance

Page 91

with this line of argument, the effect of the workers’ education on decisions whether or
not to export will also be examined statistically in the thesis.
2.8.5

Sector’s Influence and Export Propensity

It is argued that the understanding of the event under study would not be fully
unravelled unless the impact of the external environment is understood (Sections 2.7).
In the literature, the importance of the external environment’s influence on the SME
export behaviour has received some attention. Among the external factors, the influence
of foreign and domestic market characteristics on the export behaviour of the SME has
been emphasised (Zou & Stan, 1998; Zhao & Zou, 2002; Sousa et al., 2008). Among
domestic market characteristics, a sector’s export intensity and propensity, sector size
and industry concentration have all received researchers’ attention (Zhao & Zou, 2002;
Suárez-Ortega & Ălamo-Vera, 2005; Iyer, 2010). As the firms in the data set used in
this study (Chapter 5/Empirical Chapter 1) belong to different manufacturing sectors
(i.e. the machine sector, the metal sector, the furniture sector, the garments & textiles
sub-sector and the wood sector), industry specific effects come under examination.
Therefore, in the light of assumptions of contingency theory concerning the influence of
the external environment on the small firm’s export behaviour, the relationship between
sector/industry influence and export propensity of the SMEs are examined statistically
in the thesis.
The table below provides a summary of the selected empirical findings in relation to the
effect of foreign ownership, firm size, age (among others) and export propensity.

An overall illustration of the proposed integrated theoretical framework applied to the
thesis is supplied in Figure 2.6 below.

Figure 2.6 implies that each of the theoretical frameworks integrated into the thesis has
its limitations. Therefore, this integration highlights and attempts to offset their
respective weaknesses. In addition, in order to provide a fuller understanding of what is
assumed to be a complex phenomenon, the figure represents the combination of factors
from: (1) stage theory (e.g. prior operating domestic experience, prior experiential
knowledge in the export market, receipt of unsolicited orders, psychic distance, firm
size, firm age, gradual export development, and firms with older managers); (2) network
theory (e.g. the network relation with business associates, agents, customers and
suppliers, family members and friends, government department/agency/ministry and

Page 95

industrial associations, either in the local market or the international market or both); (3)
the RBV theory ( e.g. internal resource stocks of the small firm, including the
personality characteristics of the owner-manager); (4) IE theory (e.g. excessive alertness
to profitable international opportunities, positive international attitudes and orientation,
international business networking behaviour, international orientation, an excessive
proclivity for international ideas, a high need for international achievement, strong
personal drive, ambition, and motivation for internationalisation, international risktaking attitude) and (5) contingency theory factors (e.g. competitor action, government
action, industry influence, and the influence of trade associations).
Furthermore, it is assumed that the integrated framework will offer a full understanding
of why one SME responds positively to export triggers, initiates export business and is
able to meet its export orders while similar others do not, even though they all operate
in the same industry, location and face the same market conditions. In addition, the
proposed framework further implies that whilst the firm can explore export
opportunities through its own motivations and/or capacity (i.e. RBV and stage theories
influence), such internal capacity alone is not enough, especially when considering a
firm in a developing African country (e.g. Ghana). As a result, it is a firm’s access to its
network relations’ stock of resources that brings it into the position to achieve export
success. The framework suggests that, in today’s fast-changing world, a successful
decision requires a firm to possess the ability to align its strategy (planned and/or
emergent) to the changing trends of the external environment. Hence, to be successful in
the export market a firm must be alert to the contingency factors in the external
environment.

Page 96

In addition to the graphical representation of the proposed integrated theoretical
framework, each theory is briefly summarised by comparing and contrasting them using
the following themes, namely (1) the accredited writer(s), (2) any acronym, (3) the
alternative name, (4) the main dependent variable, (5) the main independent variables
and (6) originating discipline, (7) time to enter the international market, (8) mode of
entry (9) sustaining internationalisation operations, (10) pace of internationalisation,
(11) resource mobilisation and development for internationalisation activity (12)
whether the theory focuses on the firm and/or the entrepreneur, or both in explaining
export behaviour and (13) environmental influence (Table 2.9).

2.9.1

The Link between Importing and Exporting Activity

While much research attention has been paid to outward internationalisation (e.g. export
business), it is also noted that conducting import business before exporting might
impact positively on export business. However, very little research as yet exists to shed
light on how initial importing activities might impact positively on later exporting
activities (Korhonen, 1996; Karlsen et al., 2003; Overby & Servais, 2005). As a
consequence, private practitioners and public policy makers have very little relevant
information to draw on with regard to how import business can impact on export
development. According to Korhonen (1999) and Karlsen et al. (2003), linkage exists
between import and export business and the causality runs from import business to
exporting. The basic assumptions from these researchers are that import activities (e.g.
purchasing of raw materials and purchasing of equipment and machinery) create the
opportunity for importers to build networks with foreign actors. These connections
generate benefits, including information about foreign businesses. Another assumption
is that through involvement in import business SMEs owner-managers learn the
dynamics of international business from their own experience. Implicitly, as a result of
importing experience and its attendant benefits, SME owner-managers’ export
behaviour is facilitated with regard to time of export market entry, the pace of the
exporting process, the choice of export market and the resources needed for further
export development.

While the proposed link between initial import business and subsequent export business
development seems plausible, contrasting results do exist. For instance, a European
study by Karlsen et al. 2003, failed to established support for the proposed link between

Page 101

initial export business and later export business. In another European study, Forsman et
al. (2002) failed to confirm the benefit of initial importing activities on later exporting.
In contrast, in a study of Finnish SMEs, Korhonen et al. (1996) found that most of the
Finnish firms start internationalisation activities on the importing side, before starting to
export, thereby supporting the assertion of the benefit of conducting initial importing
activities before exporting.

Page 102

2.10

NON-EXPORTING BEHAVIOUR

This section of the literature review focuses on the behaviour of non-exporting SMEs.
Non-exporting firms are classified into two dimensions in the literature: (1) firms that
have exported before but have now withdrawn from export business, and (2) firms that
have not exported before (no export experience) (Leonidou, 1995a; Williams, 2006).
Whereas most of the internationalisation literature focuses on the export behaviour of
the SME, only a few studies pay attention to the proportion of SMEs that do not engage
in export business (Leonidou, 1995b, 1995c, Fillis, 2002).
Leonidou (1995a) argued that such a gap exists because most policy makers find it
easier to encourage existing exporting firms to increase their export activities than to
expend fresh resources on encouraging non-exporters to initiate export business.
Another contributory reason may be that the earlier philosophical paradigm of this
research field was based on positivist philosophy. Generally, positivist philosophy seeks
to test existing theories, whereas qualitative research uses case studies to encourage new
theory building. As a result, researchers were not motivated to build new theories from
unexplored areas (e.g. on non-exporting small business behaviour).
Although there is a call to undertake qualitative research through case study research in
the field (Yang et al., 2006; Sousa et al., 2008), not many studies on non-exporting exist
compared to the number of studies that discuss the export behaviour of the SME. With
regard to existing studies on non-exporting behaviour, three main themes have
appeared: (1) studies that focus on the factors that discriminate between exporters and
non-exporters; (2) studies that focus on the non-exporters’ perceptions about the factors

Page 103

that trigger export initiation/export stimulating and/or facilitating factors; and (3)
studies that focus on the non-exporters’ perceptions of the problems exporters encounter
(i.e. export barriers). The first category of studies mainly aims to identify the
characteristics of non-exporting SME by comparing them with their counterpart
exporters on similar factors. A sample of the studies that have compared behaviour of
exporting and non-exporting SMEs is presented below.

The studies in Table 2.10 indicate that by regressing variables (e.g. firm age, firm size
etc.), it can be argued that compared with exporting firms, non-exporting SMEs: (1) are
smaller in size; (2) do not contribute much to the employment growth in a country; (3)
do not have much sales growth; (4) do not contribute much to a county’s gross domestic

107

product (GDP); (5) are generally younger in industry experience; (6) mostly operate in
sectors other than manufacturing sectors; and (7) are less likely to have foreign
ownership. The implications of such studies on public policy and practice are that in
order to increase employment growth and GDP, attention must be paid to encouraging
firms to export. Although the types of studies in Table 2.10 reveal which types of firms
are non-exporters, they fail to account for why these firms remain as non-exporters. As
a result, another group of researchers has sought to identify the perception of nonexporters about factors that trigger export initiation/and or facilitating factors.
Among this group of researchers is Leonodou (1995c). The author used 22 export
stimuli from a review of previous empirical studies on export stimulation. It was found
that the non-exporting group of SMEs held a positive perception of export business, just
as the exporters did. In that study it was found that factors such as (1) the need for
potential for extra sales, (2) the need for potential for extra growth resulting from
exports, (3) the need to achieve economies of scale from exporting, (4) the need for
potential extra profit resulting from exporting and (5) the production of goods with
unique qualities were among the positive perceptions non-exporters held about export
business (i.e. in order of importance).
This type of non-exporting study (Leonidou, 1995c), however, avoids some of the
weaknesses of the type of non-exporting studies listed in Table 2.10. The reason for this
is that the latter study addresses the issue of whether or not non-exporting SMEs
perceive anything positive at all from export business, rather than merely identifying,
for example, that they are of a smaller size and contribute less to domestic employment
growth. However, a limitation of a study such as this (Leonidou, 1995c) is that it fails

108

to identify why there is still such a concentration of non-exporters in the domestic
market, even though they have such positive perceptions about export business.
Furthermore, because the concept of perception is different from that of experience,
Leonidou’s study failed to consider whether or not any of the non-exporting firms from
his sample had the opportunity to export, but could not or did not do so, and for what
reasons. As a result, in Chapter 7 of this thesis the gap is partly filled through an attempt
to identify the non-exporters’ perception of the factors that trigger export initiations and
the factors that enable exporters to meet their export orders. Moreover, Chapter 7 seeks
to ascertain whether some of the non-exporters have had the occasion to initiate export
activity (e.g. been approached to export), but declined to do so for one reason or
another. The main reasons that made it impossible for them to meet such export orders
are also explored in Chapter 7 of the thesis.
The next type of non-exporting study in the literature focuses on the examination of
non-exporters’ perception about export barriers. Exemplar studies of this type include
research by Yaprak (1985), Keng (1989), Leonidou (1995b), Fillis (2002) and Shaw and
Darroch (2004). This class of non-exporting studies sought to identify the reasons why
non-exporters do not export and the degree of severity of the challenges they perceive
with regard to the export business. Again, these studies extend and improve the nonexporting literature because they depart from non-exporters’ perceptions about export
stimulating factors and explore new areas by examining non-exporters’ views about
barriers to exporting. However, within this type of study none of the researchers
incorporate examination of whether any of these challenges have actually prevented any
of the non-exporting firms from exporting (i.e. experience versus perception). Here
again, Chapter 7 will examine the reasons for non-exporting and also aim to ascertain
109

which of the problems non-exporters have actually experienced previously when they
made attempts to export. The justification for this is that, as already indicated,
perceiving a barrier is different from having experienced it. A sample of non-exporters’
perceptions of export barriers is shown in Table 2.11.

confirms Ghauri et al.’s (2003) assertion that small business problems appear to be
similar, regardless of the geographical location, though the magnitude may differ. To
complement the information on non-exporters’ perceptions of the barriers to export

Shaw and Darroch’s (2004) study is based on small and medium-sized manufacturing
businesses, and was conducted in New Zealand. In addition, Appendix H lists additional
studies about the non-exporters’ perceptions of the barriers to export business (for

111

example, based on Leonidou (1995b) and Fillis (2002)). However, taking all these
studies into consideration, the core message they present is that the perceived obstacles
to export business consist of both internal and external factors (e.g. foreign exchange,
legal issues, taxation, economic and regulatory challenges and small firm size).
Moreover, it can be concluded from these studies that the perceived difficulties
associated with internal barriers outweigh the external perceived barriers.

2.11

GENERAL EXPORT BARRIERS

Apart from studies (e.g. Yaprak, 1985; Keng & Jiuan, 1989; Leonidou, 1995b; Shaw &
Darroch, 2004) that examine the perceptions of non-exporters of the barriers to export
business, there is a body of studies that research the general barriers to export business,
based not on non-exporters perceptions, but actual barriers to export business which
exporting SMEs encounter. Such studies include Leonidou (2004) (a comprehensive
analysis of 39 export barriers extracted from a systematic review of 32 empirical studies
between 1960 and 2000), Fillis (2002) (an examination of 55 craft small business
exporters in the UK about export barriers) and Shaw and Darroch (2004) (i.e. the
examination of 285 international small new venture exporting firms, mostly in the
manufacturing sector of New Zealand, about export barriers).
Other studies include: Tesfom and Lut (2006) (a systematic review of 40 papers
published between 1980 and 2004 on manufacturing SMEs in developing countries
concerning export barriers), Neupert et al. (2006) (an examination of 19 exporting
SMEs in the USA, Idaho and Vietnam, concerning export barriers), Hutchinson et al.
(2009) (an examination of export barriers based on 12 small exporting business in the

112

UK’s retail sector) and OECD (2009) (findings concerning barriers to SME
internationalisation in OECD and APEC member countries). Building on these studies,
actual export barriers represent any challenge that obstructs the ability of a SME to
initiate (i.e. pre- and during export business), develop and sustain export business. A
common theme that also runs through the studies on export barriers (i.e. the actual
barriers exporters encounter) (e.g. Fillis, 2002; Leonidou, 2004; Shaw & Darroch, 2004;
Tesfom & Lut, 2006; Hutchinson et al., 2009; OECD, 2009) is that these barriers
consist of both internal and external factors.
According to these authors, internal actual export barriers consist of inability by the
SMEs to initiate, develop and sustain export business because of problems endogenous
to the firm. Some researchers have further sub-divided the internal export barriers into
informational barriers, functional barriers, marketing barriers, logistical barriers,
financial barriers and human resource barriers (see e.g. Leonidou, 2004; Tesfom & Lut,
2006). In addition, the actual external barriers represent barriers that are exogenous to
the SME (i.e. barriers emanating from the external environment). Researchers have subdivided these types of barriers (e.g. Leonidou, 2004; Tesfom & Lut, 2006) into industry
barriers, procedural barriers, social cultural factors and customer barriers (i.e. customer
perception of product characteristics and lack of government incentives). Figure 2.7
shows additional actual barriers as cited in Shaw and Darroch (2004).
