Additionally, Appendix I contains extra evidence of previous findings on the actual
export barriers. Overall conclusions can be drawn from the studies that focus on export
barriers (i.e. actual and perceived export barriers of the small firm) (e.g. Figure 2.7,
Tables 2.12, 2.13, and those compiled and synthesised in Appendix I). First, as the nonexporters, like the exporting SMEs, perceive export barriers as deriving mostly from the
115

firm’s lack of capacity, it can be seen that barriers to their export initiation also arise
more from the internal capacity of the firm than from external factors. This finding in
the literature supports the researchers (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Bloodgood et al.,
1996; Reuber & Fischer, 1997; Westhead et al., 2001; Ibeh, 2003; Ibeh, 2005; Ibeh &
Wheeler, 2005; Ruzzier et al., 2006; Iyer, 2010) who argue that, the export success of
SMEs resides more within their internal resource capacity.
Overall, although the previous studies into export barriers to the SME are different with
regard to the author(s), the location and time of the research, there are likenesses among
the findings/barriers, except in relation to “the country of origin effect” (Testform &
Lutz, 2006: 277). This effect implies that a product coming from a developing country
(e.g. African country) will signal low confidence with regard to attitudes among
international buyers towards

these products compared with those coming from

developed countries (e.g. the USA, the UK). Otherwise the barriers are similar across
the world, though their degree and magnitude may vary.
Another observation identified from these studies (exporting barriers) is that very few
focus on developing African nations (e.g. among the reviews on export barriers, only
one study focused on South Africa, one on Kenya and one other on Zimbabwe). The
majority of the studies focus on developed countries and other developing countries in
Latin America and Asia.

116

2.12

HOW TO ENCOURAGE NON-EXPORTERS TO EXPORT

2.12.1

Export Stimulation

It appears that practitioners and public policy makers can draw on the factors reviewed
above to encourage non-exporting firms into exporting activities. However, a group of
researchers (e.g. Hutchinson et al., 2007; Leonidou et al., 2007; Williams, 2008) focus
their studies on export stimulation factors, which can be the starting point with regard to
encouraging non-exporting small firms to export. For the purpose of parsimony, these
researchers classify export stimulation factors according to whether or not the trigger
emanates from the firm’s internal proactive behaviour (e.g. the desire for higher
corporate growth), their internal reactive behaviour (e.g. accumulation of unsold
inventory), external proactive behaviour (e.g. responding positively to external
government assistance) or external reactive behaviour (e.g. close physical proximity to
the export market).
Among the export stimulating factors, Leonidou et al. (2007) synthesised 32 empirical
studies conducted between 1974 and 2005. In that synthesis, researchers identified 40
factors that trigger export initiation. In addition, Williams (2008) identified 20 factors
that trigger export initiation by SME in the Caribbean (Jamaica). Furthermore,
Hutchinson et al. (2007) found similar factors among small businesses in the UK which
participate in international business. Here, again, despite the differences in these studies
(i.e. Hutchinson et al., 2007; Leonidou et al., 2007; Williams, 2008) with regard to: (1)
author (s), (2) geographical location of the study, (3) time of the study and (4) type of
industry, one can find that the factors generally look alike, although their importance

117

(i.e. frequency and ranking) will vary with regard to the firm or industry in question. A
sample of export stimulating factors is presented in the table below.

118

Similarly, studies concerning the triggers of export initiation can be summarised as
follows. The findings agree that the triggers consist of firm-level factors and external
factors. Here the evidence with regard to the triggers of export initiation counteracts the
assumptions of the stage theory (Johanson & Vahle, 1977) and the RBV (Barney, 1991)
that export determinants are wholly internally influenced. In addition, of the selected
studies reviewed (e.g. Hutchinson et al., 2007; Leonidou et al., 2007; Williams, 2008),
none focuses on African firm(s). As already mentioned above, this gap consolidates the
call by Sousa et al. (2008) regarding the need to extend research on SME
internationalisation to SMEs in Africa.

2.13

CONCLUDING REMARKS

The literature review mainly examines the exporting and non-export behaviour of the
SMEs. With regard to the exporting behaviour, the chapter unravels the general
theoretical frameworks that predict the behaviour of the exporting firm. Among these
theoretical frameworks, not all are appropriate for application to the SME, and only five
are proposed for integration to address the research questions behind the thesis. The
justification for the proposed integrated theoretical framework is then presented, along
with selected integrated variables which arose as influential in the course of the review
and therefore are analysed in detail in the empirical examination in Chapters 5, 6 & 7 of
the thesis. The second part of the literature review addresses non-exporting small firms’
behaviour. It is identified that although some studies address the export stimulating
factors and export barriers with regard to the perceptions of the non-exporting firms,
most of the studies fail to ascertain whether some of the non-exporters have actually
119

experienced any of the factors in an attempt to initiate export business. As a result, part
of this thesis (Chapters 6 & 7) contributes to filling this gap in the field. The chapter
ends by addressing the problem of how to encourage non-exporters to export and
presenting the overview of export stimulating factors as the starting point in stimulating
non-exporters to consider export business. From the literature review as a whole
(including the studies presented in the appendices), it is evident that there is a marked
lack of studies focusing on exporting and non-exporting behaviour of SMEs in subSaharan Africa, and Africa in general.

120

CHAPTER THREE

THE STUDY’S CONTEXT

3.1

INTRODUCTION

Various countries in sub-Saharan Africa (e.g. Nigeria, Kenya, Zimbabwe & Ghana)
have shifted their development framework from an inward (i.e. import substitution
coupled with protectionist policies) to an outward-oriented approach (i.e. exportled/internationalisation-led approach). It is argued that an outwardly oriented
development model can make a greater contribution to the nation’s socio-economic
prosperity compared with the inward-orientation development model (Ruzzier et al.,
2006).

3.2

BACKGROUND OF GHANA

Ghana covers an area of about 92,000 square miles. It is located on the coast of West
Africa and its population is about 22 million (Robson & Freel, 2008). It was a British
colony before gaining independence in 1957. Ghana is endowed with mineral resources
such as gold, diamonds, and manganese among others, and was the first sub-Saharan
Africa country to gain independence (Robson & Freel, 2008). According to Robson &
Freel, Ghana’s GDP per capita used to be higher than South Korea’s, but currently is

121

about 35 times lower than that of South Korea. Teal (2002) also shows that Ghana’s
export per capita peaked at US$300 in 1954, but by 1998 it had fallen to around
US$100. Furthermore, it has been indicated that Ghana has fallen behind its previous
peers within the Africa sub-region with regard to socio-economic development (IMF,
2006).

3.3

THE MAIN SECTORS OF GHANA’S ECONOMY

Ghana’s economy, according to the Institute of Statistical Social and Economic
Research (ISSER, 2007), consists of three main sectors. First, the agricultural sector
comprises sub-sectors of cultivation of mangoes, pineapples, cocoa, cereal, vegetable
and other crops. Other sub-sectors comprise the farming activities of poultry production,
cattle and sheep rearing and a fishing sub-sector/industry. In Ghana, the majority of
crop farmers are smallholders who lack appropriate machines and as a result have low
productivity. This low productivity is partly because of a heavy reliance in Ghana on
natural factors such as rain and bush burning in farming activities. The agriculture
sector represents the mainstay of the Ghanaian economy as it is the leading contributor
to the country’s GDP. Its contribution to GDP is about 39% and it accounts for about
55% of employment (ISSER, 2007).
Second, the services sector of Ghana’s economy consists of six main sub-sectors: (1)
transport, storage and communication; (2) the wholesale and retail trade as well as
restaurants and hotels; (3) finance, insurance, real estate and business services; (4)
government services; (5) community, social and personal services, and (6) producers of
122

private non-profit services (ISSER, 2007). According to ISSER (2007), the services
sector is the second mainstay of the Ghanaian economy after the agricultural sector. The
manufacturing sector is the least important of the three mainstay sectors of Ghana’s
economy, and its sub-sectors include: (1) garments and textiles, (2) food processing, (3)
soap and detergents, (4) timber products (wood), (5) furniture, (6) cement, (7) metal, (8)
machinery and (9) paper production. According to ISSER (2007), while it is hoped that
the manufacturing sub-sector of Ghana will drive the overall growth of the country,
allowing it to achieve its middle income status goal, it still continues to record the
lowest growth compared to the agriculture and services sectors (ISSER (2007).
3.3.1

Background of Garment and Textile Manufacturing Sub-sector

The garment and textile manufacturing sub-sector of Ghana is one the dominant sectors.
For instance, it was most represented sample (26%) among the sub-sector represented
entire World Bank (RPED)’s study on Ghana’s manufacturing sector. Concerning
portion of the World Bank data set used in Chapter 5 of this thesis, the garment and
textile sub-sector ranked the highest in terms representation, consisting of 35 % of the
total sample. In addition, in a study concerning the export involvement of key selected
manufacturing sub-sectors of Ghana in Figure 3.1 below, the garment and sub-sector is
the second most represented apart from the wood sub-sector.
Therefore, as indicated in Chapter 1, Section 1.7, the study largely focuses on the
garment and textile manufacturing sub-sector of Ghana. The reasons for this include,
but are not limited to, the following. First, the government of Ghana has targeted the
garment and textile sub-sector to drive its socio-economic prosperity agenda because it
possesses much manufacturing export potential which can impact on manufacturing

123

export behaviour in similar sub-sectors in Ghana (Quartey, 2006; Kuffour, 2008).
According to Kuffour (2008), 90% of the people employed in the garment and textile
sub-sector in Ghana are women, owner-entrepreneurs as well as workers, and because
women dominate Ghana’s population, promotion of the sub-sector has become a key
strategy for the government of Ghana

in pursuing its poverty reduction agenda,

including attaining its Millennium Development Goals. This strategy is enshrined in
Ghana’s development documents (GPRS, I & II, Ghana’s Budget Statement, 2008) as
indicated in Chapter 1, Section 1.7. Moreover the garment and textile sub-sector has the
potential to combine indigenous technology with modern technology and to maximise
one of Ghana’s competitive advantages – cheap labour (Soderbom & Teal; 2000;
Soderbom & Teal, 2003).
3.3.2

Specific Export-led Programmes in the Garment & Textile Sub-sector

The Ghana government’s drive to promote the garment and textile sub-sector and
exploit its export potential has included the following specific export-led programmes:
(1) the provision of GH2.00 million (about $2 million USD) for the rehabilitation and
operation of the redundant Juapong Textile Company under the new name - Volta Star
Company Limited (ISSER, 2007); (2) the provision of 3,200 new sewing machine
operators at the Clothing Technology Centre in Accra (ISSER, 2007); (3)
implementation of the President’s Special Initiative (PSI) for the garment and textile
sub-sector in Ghana. The PSI was launched in 2001 as a public partnership programme
which aimed to support, accelerate and develop the garment and textile industry and
enable it to become a lead export sector and the primary source of employment
generation in Ghana (Kuffour, 2008). Within the PSI, the government supports
businesses in this sub-sector with modern factory machinery, clothing technology and a
124

training centre, as well as technical assistance programmes. A whole garment village,
which benefits from customised industrial facilities, has been created for the sector in
Tema, near Accra (Kuffour, 2008);
(4) The Africa Growth Opportunity Act (AGOA) is another policy initiative aimed at
boosting the export capabilities of the sub-sector. AGOA is a United States initiative
that was signed into law on May 18, 2000 as the Trade and Development Act. Under
AGOA, Ghana is one of the 34 sub-Saharan African countries eligible for duty and
quota free access to the US market for the textile and garment trade. Quratey (2006)
explains that some of the PSI programmes for the garment and textile sub-sector aim to
enhance the sector’s ability to maximise the AGOA initiative and (5) The Textile and
Garment Cluster established by the government of Ghana in collaboration with UNIDO
(United Nation Industrial Development Organisation) aims to bring together and
enhance the capacity of the micro, small and medium scale operators in the garment and
textile industry in another specific export-led programme. This cluster aims to remove
obstacles that hinder the sub-sector’s ability to export. Quartey (2006) argues that since
its establishment it has assisted in training firms in mass production strategies (a key
requirement in handling large export orders), sub-contracting as well as up-grading of
members’ technical, marketing, financial and managerial skills.
Another initiative is the Garment and Textile Training Centre, set up by the government
of Ghana in collaboration with UNIDO, which also aimed to enhance the capacity of
firms in the sub-sector to take advantage of AGOA (USA) and other export destinations
of Ghanaian firms. In addition, Quarter (2006) maintained that the sub-sector is also
supported by 21 national vocational training institutes which provide basic practical and
theoretical training in tailoring and dressmaking. Such export-led initiatives, specific to
125

the sub-sector, make exploration of the key factors and their potential to promote export
growth, extremely feasible.

3.4

GHANA’S DEVELOPMENT FRAMEWORK AFTER INDEPENDENCE

As indicated in Section 3.1, Ghana began to implement the import substitution
industrialisation development framework after gaining independence in 1957. This
framework focused on large scale and capital intensive manufacturing enterprises.
Within this development framework, private enterprises were replaced by direct state
intervention. The import substitution policy coupled with the direct state intervention
carried high tariff barriers and relied heavily on short to medium-term foreign
borrowing (Dinye & Nyaba, 2001). In the literature, it is argued that because of
inappropriate domestic policies, natural disasters, low foreign exchange inflow, poor
industrial competition, as well as a lack of effective firm level learning, Ghana recorded
a serious economic decline throughout the period of implementation of its import
substitution and protectionist development framework (Kuada & Sørensen, 2000;
Sørensen & Nyanteng, 2000; Dinye & Nyaba, 2001). Therefore, due to the deteriorating
economic performance up until the early 1980s, Ghana changed its development
framework from inward orientation based on import substitution and protectionist
policies to a development framework of total liberalisation based on outward orientation
and export-led growth policies (Baah-Nuakoh et al., 1996; Dinye & Nyaba, 2001).
Table 3.1 presents sample evidence of Ghana’s economic decline from the 1970s until
1983.

Table 3.1 confirms Ghana’s economic decline up to the mid 1980s. In terms of the

change from the inward development model to one of wholesale liberalisation and
outward orientation included the Economic Recovery Programme (ERP) and the
Structure Adjustment Programme (SAP) of 1983. The ERP and the SAP consisted of
two phases. Phase 1 (ERP I) covered four years, 1983– 1986, while the second phase
(ERP II) took place from 1986–1991. The purpose of Phase 1 was to liberalise the
Ghanaian economy in order to set the pace of a new macro-economic framework to
reverse the economic decline suffered from the 1970s to 1983. Among the policies in
the first phase were monetary and fiscal policies (which sought to reduce the level of
inflation indicated in Table 3.1), rationalisation (e.g. depreciation) of the exchange rate
to stimulate exports and reallocation of resources to productive sectors of the economy.
The second phase included structural adjustment policies which were intended to
achieve sustained growth for the country by expanding the productive sectors of the
country and further boosting exports, especially export manufacturing.

127

3.5

EXPORT DIVERSIFICATION AND EXPORT MANUFACTURING IN
GHANA

Until the 1980s, Ghana’s exports consisted of unprocessed agricultural products
(cocoa), timber and mineral commodities. The initial phase of the structural adjustment
programme focused more on the reallocation of resources to agricultural commodity
exports, since it was thought that Ghana’s competitive advantage lay in commodity
agriculture (Owusu-Frimpong & Mmieh, 2007; Robson & Freel, 2008). However,
Robson & Freel (2008) argue that as worldwide agricultural tariffs ran at 40%, in
contrast to 4% on manufacturing goods, the government decided to diversify its export
base to cover non-traditional export (NTEs 6). On this basis the government emphasised
manufacturing exports commodities as the main driving force, and increased the
competitiveness of the manufacturing sector to drive Ghana’s economic development.
The export diversification policies in Ghana, also known as the non-traditional export
commodities’ promotion, are classified into three themes: (1) new agricultural export
products, namely: fruits, vegetables, roots/tubers/plantains (fresh and processed) and
other food items; (2) new timber products (including handicraft products); and (3) new
processed and semi-processed manufacturing products such as: wood products, machine
products, furniture, metal products, chemical products, bakery products, and garments
and textiles (Owusu-Frimpong & Mmieh, 2007).
In 1986, towards the end of the first phase of the ERP & the SAP (1983-1986), the total
export earnings of the non-traditional export commodities (NTEs) increased to $23.8
million – representing 3.2% of that year’s total export earnings – and then rose to
6

NTEs refer to new export products that did not form part of Ghana’s traditional export
commodities – cocoa, raw timber and mineral commodities.
128

US$62.34 million or 7.0% of the total export earnings in 1990 (Kastner, 2005). This
initial increase in the export contribution brought considerable optimism to the nation.
As a result, and based on a study jointly undertaken in 1997 by the government of
Ghana and the United States Agency for International Development (USAID) in North
Carolina, it was predicted that Ghana’s exports would need to grow to about US$16.3
billion by 2020 if the middle income status goal was to be achieved. It was estimated
that approximately US$11.9 billion of this amount would come from NTE (NonTraditional Exports, especially manufacturing exports), which would represent 73% of
the total required export earnings (Kastner, 2005).
From the 1980s onwards, various export-led policies have been implemented by the
government of Ghana with the aim of increasing manufacturing exports in order to
achieve its middle income status for citizens. Therefore, to augment the sample of
manufacturing export-led programmes discussed in Chapter 1, the next section
discusses a further sample of major manufacturing export-led policies implemented in
Ghana to make export manufacturing more competitive and become the driving force of
the economic development agenda.

3.6

ADDITIONAL MANUFACTURING EXPORT-LED PROGRAMMES IN

GHANA
Further such manufacturing export-led programmes include the following. The first is
the establishment by the government of Ghana in 1997 of the Export Development Fund
(EDIF), which operated under the Bank of Ghana (under the Exim Guarantee Company
Scheme). Textiles and garments, wood processing, food processing and packing
129

industries are among the sub-sectors which have been given priority under the scheme
(Aryeetey & Ahene, 2005). The second is the Tax and Duties Incentive Regime. Under
this regime, the non-traditional exporters bear only 8% tax, and are exempt from most
export duties. Among the incentives for exporters is the Export Proceed Retention
Scheme (EPRS). This scheme allows exporters to retain 35% of foreign earnings in
foreign bank accounts, which are intended for related import purchases and expenses
(Aryeetey & Ahene, 2005).
There is also a Customs Duty Drawbacks package which allows manufacturing
exporters to claim back up to 100% of any import duty and other taxes on imported raw
materials that are meant for the export business in Ghana. Moreover, manufacturers of
agricultural products (e.g. food processing) who export either part or 100% of their
output abroad are entitled to claim a 40-70 % tax rebate (under the Corporate Tax
Rebate Scheme). The government of Ghana also allows export manufacturers a
Customs Licence which enables them to hold any imported raw materials intended for
export in a Bonded Warehouse (secured storage) without payment of duty.
In addition, the government specifically designed and implemented a Medium Term
Plan for development of Non-Traditional Exporters called the non-traditional export
scheme (1991-1995). The Ministry of Trade and Industry (MOTI) jointly implemented
this scheme with the Ghana Export Promotion Council (GEPC) (Aryeetey & Ahene,
2005). The US government sponsored the Trade and Investment Programme (TIP)
through its agency (the United States Agency for International Development/USAID).
The TIP programme aimed to increase firm level and national export earnings, to boost
employment and attract more business into the export manufacturing sector of Ghana.
According to Aryeetey & Ahene (2005), the scheme was also devised to influence
130

government policies and lessen the various bottlenecks that hinder export growth, as
well as to provide support services to the Ghana Export Promotion Council (GEPC) and
the Ministry of Trade and Industry (MOTI).
Aryeetey & Ahene (2005) explain that in addition to the above macro policy
programmes, there have been other donor-funded programmes, including the Private
Sector Development Programme (PSDP), funded by the International Development
Association (IDA), the Capacity Development and Utilisation Programme (CDUP),
funded by the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP) and the DFIDsupported Challenge Fund, funded by the UK’s Department for International
Development (DFID).
In addition, Ghana opted for debt relief under the Heavily Indebted Poor Country (HIP)
programme (recommended by the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund) in
2002 and signed up with the Millennium Challenge Corporation (MCC) in 2006. The
MCC’s scheme was offered by the US government as a bilateral scheme and sought to
transform Ghana’s agricultural output through internationalisation (Central Intelligent
Agency, 2010). The list of the export-led programmes is almost endless, but because
the focus of the thesis is not to evaluate these measures, it is not the intention to review
all in detail here. However, records show that despite the government’s vigorous efforts
at

implementing

all

these

manufacturing

export-led

programmes,

Ghana’s

manufacturing export involvement and performance is still low. Figure 3.1 below
supports this argument.

131

Figure 3.1: Percentage of Manufacturing Exports by Industry

Source: Söderbom (2003: 1)

Figure 3.1 shows that of these countries within the sub-Saharan Africa sub-region,
Ghana is the lowest exporter of manufactured goods, although it has some advantage in
the wood sub-sector. From the three countries compared in Figure 3.1, Ghana and
Kenya’s export performances are inferior to that of Zimbabwe. This poses something of
a puzzle because among SSA countries, Ghana and Kenya implemented comprehensive
liberalisation policies in the early 1980s, before corresponding action was taken by
Zimbabwe in the 1990s, yet their export response is below that of Zimbabwe in this
case. This picture confirms Zeufack’s argument (2001) that while Ghana and Kenya
score highest with regard to the Sachs and Warner index of openness 7 rankings
compared to other countries in SSA (i.e. 0.23 and 0.11 respectively on a scale from 0 7

The Sachs and Warner index, which ranks countries on a scale of 0-1, based on the
depth of trade liberalisation policies undertaken since 1960, is widely used in the new
geography literature as a proxy for policy reforms. The trade openness database is
available at www.cid.hardvard.edu/ciddata
132

1), it is not understood why Ghana is considered one of the lowest exporters among
African countries. Implicitly, it is argued that what may be causing Zimbabwe’s export
supply to be above those of Ghana and Kenya stems from a number of factors.
Soderbom (2003) contends that while Ghana and Kenya’s manufacturing intensity are
denominated in few sub-sectors, but that of Zimbabwe is spread across industries. Biggs
& Raturi (1999) argued that unlike most countries in sub-Saharan Africa (SSA), firms
in Zimbabwe exhibit strong capabilities (e.g. production capability, technological and
firm learning). In addition others (e.g. Soko & Balchim, 2009; Kaminski & Ng, 2011)
attribute Zimbabwe’s export potentials to its closeness to the South African market.
In addition, the Institute of Social Statistical and Economic Research (ISSER) has
argued that Ghana’s export manufacturing output does not in any way reflect the
amount of export-led policies implemented so far. For instance, according to the
Institute, the total export as a percentage of GDP continued to decline from 2002 –
2007, as shown in Figure 3.2 below.

133

Figure 3.2: Ghana’s Trends in Merchandise Exports as a Ratio of its GDP: 20022007

Source: ISSER, (2007: 103)
In Figure 3.2, the vertical axis denotes the value of exports in percentages, while the
horizontal axis represents the years 2002 - 2007. Figure 3.2 indicates that between 2002
and 2007, Ghana’s manufacturing exports as a proportion of GDP declined. Therefore,
by comparing the stylised facts on export-led policies discussed in Chapter 1, Section
1.2, with the data added in this chapter, it could be argued that it is possibly paradoxical
that a variety of export-led strategies are being implemented in Ghana and yet export
involvement and performance among its firms continues to decline, both within the
country and compared to the rest of the world. The evidence from the different sources
presented in this thesis confirms Ghana’s low export involvement and performance,
although it is unclear why so few firms choose to export whilst the majority of others
remain focused on the domestic market.

134

However, it is argued that whereas Ghana’s current export’s manufacturing involvement
and performance is low, the country has little choice but to refocus its attention on the
export of manufacturing output if the country is to achieve its socio-economic
objectives and develop a middle income status (IMF, 2006; Kuada, 2007; Teal, 2008).
Here it is possible to argue that the government of Ghana’s policy to use its
manufacturing sectors (especially export manufacturing) can be achieved if the
manufacturing export-led programmes continue to be strengthened and also if such
policies are linked with firm level research.
Consequently, in accordance with Ghana’s current socio-economic status, this study
seeks via recommendations to public policy makers, owner-managers and future
researchers to play a significant role in Ghana’s future economic transformation. The
reasoning behind this is that the outcome of the study will be the identification of
potential exporting firms that merit government support, coupled with insights into how
SME owners can improve on their practice. Furthermore, in the light of this study’s
Ghanaian context, acting on the outcomes of this research will accelerate the socialeconomic prosperity Ghana hopes to achieve.
The aim of this thesis to facilitate Ghana’s export growth agenda through studying the
garment and textile sub-sector is driven by the fact that, currently, Ghana continues to
depend heavily on international financial and technical assistance from both bi-lateral
and multi-lateral organisations (e.g. the World Bank, the IMF, and DFID). Here, gold,
cocoa outputs (though mostly unprocessed) and individual remittances still constitute
the principal sources of foreign exchange for Ghana. Even though large deposits of oil
have been discovered in recent times, processing is yet to commence (Central Intelligent
Agency, 2010). As a result, strengthening manufacturing export growth through SMEs
135

small which make up the bulk of businesses in Ghana, will contribute greatly to
Ghana’s development agenda.

3.7

CONCLUDING REMARKS

This chapter has presented the context of the thesis (i.e. in Ghana). It has traced the
development framework followed by Ghana from independence until the acceptance
and implementation of the liberalisation framework in the early 1980s. The conclusion
is that whilst Ghana has identified that the attainment of its socio-economic
development goals hinges on its manufacturing exports; its current performance is
extremely low. As a result, the present thesis becomes timely with regard to facilitating
manufacturing export growth in Ghana and attaining its socio-economic goals.

CHAPTER FOUR
RESEARCH APPROACH & METHODOLOGY
4.1

INTRODUCTION

This chapter presents the outline of the methodology used to validate the proposed
integrated theoretical framework (Chapter 1, Figure 1.2). The main themes of this
chapter include: (1) the stages followed from the start of the thesis to its end (Section
4.2 below); (2) the research population and the sample, (3) the analytical techniques; (4)
how internal validity, external validity, objectivity, reliability, informed consent,
guarantee of privacy, preferred language, avoidance of deception and accuracy were
ensured in the study and (5) the definition of SMEs applied to the thesis.

4.2

10 steps; the steps are: (1) 3 steps – employed in stage 1; (2) 3 steps – used in stage 2;
(3) 3 steps – applied in stage 3 and (4) 1 step – applied in stage 4. The four main stages
running through the thesis are outlined in detail as follows. First, the 3 main steps
followed in stage 1 were:

POPULATION AND SAMPLE OF THE THESIS

The population of interest behind this research consists of exporting and non-exporting
small firms from the Ghanaian manufacturing sector. Of this population, samples used
in the thesis (i.e. Chapter 5) were originally drawn by the World Bank (i.e. a secondary
data set) for the Regional Project on Enterprise Development (RPED) in 2002 in Ghana.
The application of a secondary data set in research is popular among academics since
this offers the advantage of speed and low cost. Consequently, some of the papers
reviewed for this thesis also use a secondary data set (e.g. Westhead et al., 2001; Hall &
Tu, 2003; 2004). However, the use of a secondary data set is not without its
138

disadvantages: some data sets may have questionable sources and therefore, will have
problems with reliability. As a result, it is contended that academics and researchers
should therefore be cautious and use data sets that have been collated by credible
universities and organisations (Sorensen et al., 1996).
The World Bank’s study was implemented in collaboration with Oxford University (the
UK), the University of Ghana and the Ghana Statistical Service. The data set is named
the Regional Programme on Enterprise Development (REPD). Oxford University partly
controls the data set for academic purposes, and invites academics and the research
community to draw on it for various studies via the university’s website. Most
researchers (e.g. Söderbom & Teal, 2000; Rankin et al., 2002; Frazer, 2005) have drawn
on the data set in their various studies and attest to its reliability. Here the data set was
accessed via http//www.csae.ox.ac.uk/datasets/main.html.
The World Bank’s randomly drawn samples from five sub-sectors of the Ghanaian
manufacturing sectors included: the wood, metal, machine, furniture and garment and
textile sub-sectors. This secondary data set supplements the interviews concerning the
garment and textile sub-sector, which is the main focus of the thesis.

139

4.4

ANALYTICAL TECHNIQUES

Logistic multiple regression is the main quantitative analytical technique used to
address the first research question of the thesis (Chapter 5). Multiple regression explains
the relationship between multiple independent variables and the response variable. In
this the response variable is qualitative and so compared to OLS, logit is appropriate.
Further qualitative analysis techniques were applied in the last two empirical chapters
(i.e., Chapters 6 & 7). Here, the critical incident technique, coupled with content
analysis, were the main analytical tools used.
4.4.1 The Critical Incident Method
Critical incident techniques are credited to Flanagan (1954), but since their original
formulation they have been extended by other researchers (e.g. Chell, 2004; Neupert et
al., 2006 and Zineldin, 2007). Flanagan (1954) first used the critical incident method on
a study concerning the aviation psychology programme of the United States’ Army Air
Force in World War II. Over the subsequent years, the critical incident technique has
become popular within the social science research field (Chell, 2004). According to
Flanagan:

140

The critical incident technique consists of a set of procedures for collecting
direct observations of human behaviour in such a way as to facilitate their
potential usefulness in solving practical problems and developing broad
psychological principles. The critical incident technique outlines procedures for
collecting observed incidents having special significance and meeting
systematically defined criteria. By incident, Flanagan (1954) meant any
observable human activity that is sufficiently complete in itself to allow
inferences and predictions to be developed about the object, in this case a
person performing the act. To be critical, an incident must occur in a
circumstance where the purpose or intent of the act seems fairly clear and direct
to the actor and where its consequences are sufficiently definite to leave little or
no doubt concerning its current and subsequent results.
Flanagan (1954: 1)

In the light of this initial definition, other researchers operationalised the technique to
suit their research purposes. For example, Chell in applying the technique to suit his
research defined the critical incident as:
The qualitative interview technique which facilitates the investigation of
significant occurrences (events, incidents, processes or issues), identified by the
respondent, the way they are managed, and the outcomes in terms of perceived
effects. The objective is to gain an understanding of the incident from the
perspective of the individual, taking into account cognitive and behavioural
elements.
Chell (2004: 48)

Cope, too, applied the critical incident technique, defining the technique as:

A positive or negative event with certain perceptual and chronological
parameters that is memorable to the individual concerned and has perceived
significance in personal or business terms, or both.
Cope (2003: 8)

141

From the three definitions above, it can be inferred that the context within which the
writers conceptualise the technique differs, and Flanagan maintained that his definition
should not be perceived as static, claiming it could be adapted. For instance, while
Flanagan’s (1954) conceptualisation is based on the direct observation research setting,
those of Chell and Cope are based on retrospective memory recollection of critical
incidents. In this study, the definition of Chell (2004) is adopted because the interview
process in this study follows the same procedure. That is in Chell (2004)’s study,
respondents were asked to tell a retrospective story of a critical incident, in a similar
way to the present study. Although in this research field not many critical incident
studies exist, a selected few are shown in detail in the table below.

Table 4.1: Selected Critical Incident Studies in this Research Field

With regard to the studies in Table 4.1, the focus of the critical incidents in each case
concerns certain antecedents which were connected to a specific firm’s behaviour (e.g.
the critical incidents that led to the establishment of Swedish companies in Mexico
(Zineldin, 2007). The application of critical incidents in this thesis (e.g. Chapter 6)
follows a similar pattern.
4.4.2

Content Analysis

In addition to critical incident analysis, content analysis according to Buglear et al.
(2004) represents a step which adds a quantitative element to the analysis of qualitative
data (i.e. words). Therefore, the frequencies with which the themes and issues (words)
appear in the interview transcripts are measured using descriptive statistics such as
frequency, percentages and rankings. The content analytical technique facilitates the
classification system of the theme and sub-themes (categories and main categories) in
the analysis of the critical incidents’ interview transcripts. Consistent with other
researchers (e.g. Katsikeas & Morgan, 1994; Owusu-Frimpong & Mmieh, 2007),
frequencies, percentages and rankings were the main analytical tools used to analyse the
critical incidents that emerged from the interview transcripts (Chapters 6 & 7).
4.4.3

Analysing the Interview Transcripts

Overall, the process of the data analysis involved reading the transcripts several times to
obtain a sense of the general themes (critical incidents). These were then triangulated
with the interview notes. The process enabled the researcher to understand the accounts
of each firm in depth. In accordance with Strauss and Corbin (1990), the interview
transcripts were then coded (i.e. open coding). On one hand, Leonidou (1995b) and
Leonidou et al.’s (2007) technique with regard to the categorisation of export

144

stimulating factors was adapted. As a result, Leonidou et al.’s dimensions of export
stimulation factors guided categorisation of the critical incidents that triggered the
export initiation of the firms identified from the interviews. Following Leonidou et al.
(2007), the themes (the critical incidents) which triggered export initiation among the
firm were categorised depending on whether or not they fall under proactive internal,
proactive external, reactive internal or reactive external triggers.
On the other hand, the main factors and/or influence which facilitated the capacity of
the firms to meet their export orders were also categorised and/or open coded based on
whether or not they pertained to the RBV, the network theory, the stage theory, the IE
theory factors and the contingency theory factors. Overall, the themes which emerged as
the critical incidents were examined for differences, similarities and connectedness,
according to which the categories were grouped. This approach builds on the ‘within
case’ and ‘across the cases’ analysis suggested by Miles & Huberman (1984). As a
result, a theme in each case study is viewed as information requiring replication in other
case studies, and thus the analysis proceeds by analysing within-case evidence, which
then leads to a search for a cross-case pattern (Eisenhardt, 1989) (see Appendix N Table
2 – 6 for the within-case and cross-case synthesis).

145

4.5

VALIDITY & RELIABILITY CONSIDERATIONS IN THE THESIS

Validity and reliability criteria consist of built-in mechanisms in the research process
that ensures rigour and/or credibility. These credibility criteria include: (1) internal
validity, (2) external validity, (3) objectivity and (4) reliability.
4.5.1

Internal validity

Chapter 5 of the thesis aims to ascertain the cause and effect relationship between a
dependent variable and its independent variables. Here, the internal validity concerns
whether or not the research evidence justifies the claim (Ghauri & Grønhaug, 2004;
Fisher et al., 2007). Various statistical tests such as the f-test, the likelihood ratio, the ttest, z-test and chi-square were all applied in order to ensure that the result of each
variable was internally valid. Related to internal validity is construct validity. As
regards the construct validity, while internal validity ensures that claims of relationships
among variables supply statistical justifications, construct validity ensures that variable
operationalisations and/or definition actually measure the concepts they seek to measure
in order to bring about a meaningful interpretation of the research findings. According
to Ghauri & Grønhaug (2004), construct validity can be ascertained in a number of
ways, namely:
(1) Face validity – which ensures whether or not the operationalisation of the variables
makes sense both theoretical and institutive;
(2) Convergent validity – which also establishes whether or not multiple measures of
the same variable produce comparable results; and

146

(3) Discriminant and/or divergent validity – which ascertains whether or not variables
have a meaning of their own which does not overlap with other variables.
To ensure that construct validity is not threatened in this thesis, the constructs and/or the
measurements of the variables (e.g. the export propensity, firm size, firm age, aggregate
full-time workers education level, foreign ownership and sector) on which the
hypotheses were based, were measured in a similar way to previous applications in
published studies in the field.
4.5.2

External Validity

One of the core tenets in positivist research is a generalisation of the research findings;
consequently, external validity concerns whether or not the study’s findings can be
generalised to comparable units of analyses in comparable settings. As a result of this
criterion, the positivist tradition uses probability sampling techniques and a large
sample size to prevent threats to external validity (Fisher et al., 2007). The stratified
random sampling method was applied by the World Bank’s team to draw the RPED
data set in large samples and therefore any possible threat to external validity is avoided
in this thesis, and external validity with regard to the regression results in Chapter 5 is
largely ensured.
4.5.3

Objectivity

The positive tradition assumes that the objective knowledge systematically pursued by
researchers is based on general causal laws. Furthermore, the philosophy assumes that
as knowledge is externally objective, researchers take strictly neutral and detached
positions towards the phenomenon being investigated (i.e. value free research process).
Such a stance ensures that the values and biases of the researcher do not affect the study
147

and thereby threaten its validity. In accordance with this criterion, closed ended
questions were used to capture the World Bank data set used in this study. In addition,
in analysing the data set, various statistical tests were applied.
4.5.4

Reliability

Reliability in positivist research encompasses the reliability of variable measurements
and the extent to which results are consistent over time. Moreover, reliability concerns
the extent to which the results can be repeated and replicated in comparable settings.
This concept relates to the external validity (sub-Section 4.5.2) Once all the
assumptions of the positive research are met, positivist research can exhibit a high
likelihood of reliability, enabling confident replication and/or repetition in similar
settings. In this thesis, the various robust checks were further performed on the
regression results. Consequently, other researchers can replicate the study in the study
in similar contexts.
4.5.5

Validity & Reliability Considerations with the Interviews

The major validity and reliability considerations involved in the interview portion of the
thesis concern two main issues. First, for an incident to be judged as critical incident, it
should be differentiated from a mere influence; as a result the critical incidents selected
in the study were screened against certain key criteria, usually associated with critical
incident method (i.e. Chapter 6, sub-section 6.3.6.1). This is in accordance with
previous studies (e.g. Cope, 2003; Chell, 2004). Second, the variables which were
explored in the interview process in accordance with the proposed integrated theoretical
framework were themes and/or variables used in previous studies in the field (e.g.,
Chatty, 1999; Gorman & Evers, 2008).

148

4.6

ETHICAL CONSIDERATION OF THE THESIS

The secondary data set put together for the first empirical chapter (Chapter 5) was
obtained freely from the Oxford University website in 2008. This source is duly
acknowledged and cited in this thesis, as well as those from studies which have used the
same data set. With regard to the interview portion of the thesis, the following ethical
standards were observed: (1) informed consent (the extent to which the consent of
potential respondents was sought in advance); (2) guarantee of privacy and
confidentiality (the extent to which every interviewee’s privacy was duly respected); (3)
language (the extent to which the preferred language of the interviewee was used); (4)
deception (the extent to which no respondent was coerced to offer information); and (5)
accuracy (the extent to which accuracy is ensured in the data collection and analysis of
the study).
4.6.1

Informed Consent

With regard to conducting the interviews, first, some key organisations in Ghana (e.g.
the Ghana Statistical Service/GSS, the Ministry of Trade and Industry/MoTI and the
Association of Ghana Industries/AGI) which provide widespread support to businesses
in Ghana were contacted and informed about the study. Following these initial contacts,
three lists were compiled to form the sample frame for the study. Following receipt of
authorisation from the AGI, telephone calls were made to introduce the study to
prospective interviewees. From the lists obtained, small businesses from the garment
and textile sub-sector in Ghana which met the size criteria on the basis of the first

149

empirical analysis (Chapter 5) were contacted to request their participation in the study
(see Chapter 6, Section, 6.3.4for the criteria for selecting the companies).
In the process of seeking their consent, owner-managers were made aware that
permission to call them had been obtained from the AGI. The main aim of the study, its
nature, data generating method, the instrument to be used (i.e. the audio tape recorder
and handwritten notes) and the consequences of the study were made clear to them. As a
result, the participants understood the data gathering process in advance and recognised
that their participation was purely voluntary and that the study was purely for academic
purposes.
4.6.2

Guarantee of Privacy and Confidentiality

In the case studies section of the thesis, during the time of seeking the consent of the
owner-managers to participate in the study, the researcher assured the participants of
their rights to: (1) confidentiality and anonymity; (2) ask questions and seek
clarification from the researcher; (3) refuse to participate without any consequences; (4)
refuse to answer particular questions. These explanations gave the participants advanced
notice of what to expect in the interview process and the type of information that would
be required of them. They were also assured that neither their personal names nor their
companies would be used in the discussion without their permission. In addition, copies
of the official introductory letter sent from the researcher’s university were given to the
participants (i.e. those who requested for it) for their records. The introductory letter
described who the researcher was and also contained the contact details of the names of
people to contact at the researcher’s university in case they needed more evidence to
support the source of the researcher or if further queries arose.

150

4.6.3

Language

English is the official language in Ghana since it is used to be a British colony. During
the interviews, the respondents were given the choice to either speak English or their
local dialect (the common Ghanaian Language called Twi). However, all the interviews
were conducted in English as the interviewees preferred it, with exception of one
owner-manageress (case study 23 among the exporters) who offered to speak Twi. Her
portion of the interview was later transcribed into English.
4.6.4

Deception

No information (e.g. the list of the firms, the name of their owners and their contact
phone numbers) was obtained without the appropriate permission/authorisation. In
addition, the purpose of the study and what the results would be used for were all made
clear to the respondents in order for them to decide whether to participate or not.
4.6.5

Accuracy

The study has as far as possible ensured the accuracy of its data generation by avoiding
omissions and fabrications. As a result, the source of the secondary data set is explicitly
indicated, whilst the procedures used to collected interview data (e.g. authorisation from
the appropriate institution in Ghana, the use of the audio tape coupled with hand written
notes) ensured accuracy in the data collection and analysis of the data.

151

4.7

DEFINITION OF SME USED IN THE THESIS

For various reasons this study focuses entirely on SMEs rather than large firms. It is
argued that the average African firm is a small and/or medium-sized enterprise (Rankin
et al., 2006). According to UNIDO (1999), SMEs represent 90% of private businesses
in Africa and account for more than 50% of employment and 50% of GDP. In the
Republic of South Africa, it is found that SMEs represent approximately 91% of the
formal businesses and contribute about 57% and 61% to GDP and employment
respectively (Berry et al., 2002). In sub-Saharan Africa, it is said that SMEs represent
more than 95% of businesses (Fjose et al., 2010). In Ghana, SMEs make a significance
contribution to the economy by providing about 85% of the employment in
manufacturing in Ghana and accounting for 92% of the business (Kuffour, 2008; Abor
& Quartey, 2010). Moreover, they contribute about 70% of Ghana’s GDP and are
considered as crucial to industrial development in sub-Saharan Africa (Fjose et al.,
2010).
The government of Ghana has targeted the SMEs as the catalyst for economic growth
due to the fact that they are the major providers of income and employment (Agyapong,
2010). In addition, in Ghana SMEs are used by the government as the key strategy to
boost employment, poverty reduction and economic growth through accelerating the
capacity and activities of their manufacturing activities (GPRS II, 2003 8). Moreover,
development of manufacturing among SMEs is hailed by Ghana’s government as a
significant measure in attainment of anti-poverty objectives of the United Nations

8

Ghana’s Poverty Reduction Strategy II (GPRS II) is an extension of the government of
Ghana’s economic strategic document, developed out of GPRS I for its poverty
reduction, growth and human development agenda in the light its Vision 2020
152

(UN)’s Millennium Development Goals (MDGs) (GPRS II, 2003, Budget Statement,
2008).
However, the question is: how do SMEs in Ghana compare with their counterparts in
the rest of the world? As shown in Appendix Q, SME definition varies in both official
and research sources and these trends are no different in Ghana. In Ghana, various
organisations and individuals use different definitions to describe this group of firms.
First, the Ghana Statistical Service (GSS) defined a small-scale business as a firm with
9 or less workers, and those with 10 and above as medium and large firms (Kayanula &
Quartey, 2000; Abor & Quartey, 2010). Second, Ghana’s National Board for Small
Scale Industries (NBSSI) uses both the number of employees and the amount of fixed
assets to define a small business.
NBSSI considers small business to be firms with 9 employees or less that possess plants
and machinery (excluding land, buildings and vehicles) and which do not exceed GHC
10, 000, 000 (i.e. about $1,000.00 USD). The NBSSI revised this definition in 1998 and
distinguished a micro-firm from a small business. In the new definition, the NBSSI
defined a micro-firm as any business with 5 or less workers which has fixed assets,
excluding land and buildings and not exceeding US$10, 000, while it considered small
businesses as firms with 29 or less employees (i.e. 6-29) that have fixed assets,
excluding land and buildings and not exceeding a turnover of US$100,000 (Puplampu,
2005).
Besides drawing on the preceding sources for an official definition of SMEs in Ghana,
some researchers (e.g., Steel & Webster, 1991; Osei et al., 1993) have operationalised
other definitions of an SME to suit the purpose of their research concerning Ghana. For
example, Steel & Webster (1991) used the number of employees as a cut-off point of 30
153

or less to describe small scale businesses from Ghana; here, without any division
between small and medium-sized firm. On the other hand, Osei et al. (1993) broke
down the concept of the small firm into micro, small and medium-sized firms, defining
them as follows: (1) micro firms – firms employing 6 or less workers; (2) very small
firms – those employing 7–9 people, and small firms – those employing 10–29 staff.
A recent definition, now applied by most researchers (e.g., Adjasi, 2006; Aboagye,
2006; Abor & Harvey, 2008; Abor & Quartey, 2010), is that formulated by the World
Bank’s study on SSA, called the Regional Project on Enterprise Development (RPED).
The RPED categorised firms into: (1) micro, (2) small, (3) medium-sized, and (4) the
large firms; the study classified SME in Ghana into: (1) micro firms – those employing
5 or less; (2) small firms – those employing between 6 and 29 workers; and (3)
medium-sized firms – those having between 30 and 99 workers. However, firms with
100 workers and above are considered to be large firms. In this thesis, the World Bank’s
definition of SME in Ghana is applied for three reasons. While the World Bank’s study
included all firms, in this study those employing more than 100 employees (large firms)
are excluded because the study is limited to SMEs.
First, the thesis draws on the secondary data set by the World Bank in order to maintain
consistency, and also in order to compare the results of this study to others that build on
a similar data set in sub-Saharan Africa. Second, this is the most recent definition of
SMEs in Ghana and is widely used by researchers, and the thesis thereby continues the
current trend with regard to SME definition in Ghana. Third, the World Bank’s
definition compares favourably with the definition of SMEs in similar countries in subSaharan Africa. Definitions of other key concepts which form the core themes of the
thesis are clarified and presented in Appendix Q. Among these key concepts are:
154

CONCLUDING REMARKS

This chapter presented the general summary of the specific methodologies applied in the
empirical chapters of the thesis. The chapter examined the main procedures followed to
address the research question of the thesis. In all, four phases and ten steps were
followed as shown above. The analytical techniques for the empirical chapters were
introduced. The chapter further considered the population and sample utilised in the
thesis. In addition, the chapter describes how reliability and validity standards coupled
with consideration of ethical issues were used to ensure the integrity of the thesis. The
chapter ends by clarifying how the concept of ‘SME’ is operationalised in the thesis.

155

CHAPTER FIVE

THE DETERMINANTS OF EXPORT PROPENSITY:
EMPIRICAL RESEARCH PART 1

5.1

INTRODUCTION

This chapter addresses the first research question of the thesis and seeks to achieve the
second objective of the study. In this chapter, the main variables that are examined with
regard to export propensity (the dependent variable) are: (1) firm size, (2) firm age, (3)
aggregate full-time workers’ education, (4) foreign ownership and (5) sector. These
variables are drawn from the literature review (Chapter 2, Sections 2.8 - 2.8.5).
Moreover, the variables are selected because, whilst they seem theoretically and
intuitively plausible to positively influence the export behaviour of the SMEs, their
empirical findings remain inconclusive in the literature. Therefore, on the basis of the
theoretical and intuitive reasoning from the proposed integrated framework that informs
the thesis they are hypothesised as follows.
Following the first research question above, the hypotheses that this study seeks to
examine will include the following:
H1: Domestic SMEs that have foreign ownership will be more likely to respond
positively to export stimuli and change their strategy from engaging wholly in domestic
business and enter the export market than their counterparts that are wholly
domestically owned.
156

H2: The larger the size of the firm, the more the likelihood that it will respond positively
to export stimuli and change its strategy from engaging wholly in domestic business to
enter the export market.
H3: The older the firm, the more the likelihood that it would react positively to export
stimuli and change its strategy from engaging wholly in domestic business and enter the
export market and become an international business firm.
H4: The aggregate full-time workers’ education level in a firm will be positively related
to the export propensity of the firm.
H5: There will be a positive association between the sector and/or industry’s influence
and the export propensity of a firm.

5.2

DATA AND ESTIMATION METHOD

As already presented in the methodology chapter (Chapter 4, Section 4.3), the data for
this portion of the thesis follows the World Bank’s secondary data set (i.e. the RPED).
The World Bank’s study originally aimed to identify the business knowledge and policy
advice which would be useful for the development of private sector manufacturing in
sub-Saharan Africa (Frazer, 2005). According to Frazer, the World Bank first
recommended and sponsored the Structural Adjustment and Economic Recovery
Programmes (SAP & ERP) in the early 1980s in most sub-Saharan African countries,
which the majority of governments in the sub-region implemented. Frazer (2005) and
Biggs & Srivastava (1996) maintained that the World Bank, having realised that the
macro-level economic reforms it implemented in the 1980s were not a pre-condition for
157

private sector growth and development in Africa, initiated the RPED study to enhance
its understanding of firm level factors that could enhance manufacturing private sector
growth in sub-Saharan Africa.
The various themes which influenced the RPED study included the firm’s capacity,
labour relations, financial market characteristics, conflict resolution, infrastructure,
regulations and the uses of business support services by firms in sub-Saharan Africa.
The World Bank designed the study in three phases in each sampled country: these
included: (1) a panel survey of SME to large organisations; (2) case studies of selected
firms in each country, and (3) cross-country studies on the themes above (Biggs &
Srivastava, 1996). Ethiopia, Mauritius, Zambia, Cameroon, Côte d’Ivoire, Ghana,
Kenya and Zimbabwe were among the countries sampled for the study (Rankin et al.,
2002, Abor et al., 2008).
The World Bank study covers eight manufacturing sectors, each consisting of exporting
and non-exporting firms. These sectors were (1) garments; (2) textiles; (3) furniture; (4)
metal; (5) bakery; (6) wood; (7) machines and (8) chemicals. Harding et al. (2004)
contended that the World Bank selected these sectors because they accounted for
approximately 70% of the manufacturing employment generation and value added
outputs in the respective countries. De Toni & Nassimbeni (2001) confirmed this
assertion, reporting that in Italy, small organisations active in export businesses are
mostly found in similar sectors.
The samples were randomly drawn in each country. Teal (2002) and Van Biesebroeck
(2005) reported that in Ghana specifically, the sample was drawn randomly from a list
of firms contained in Ghana’s Census of Manufacturing Activities, this being a broader

158

industry directory. Questionnaires and interviews were the main instruments used in the
various World Bank’s study and, according to Teal (2002); the questionnaires were
designed and structured by a research team from the World Bank. The broader RPED
study uses the multiple informants’ techniques, using the owner-managers, senior
managers and workers as the main respondents for the study (Harding et al., 2004).

5.3. DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS OF THE DATA SET FOR THE THIS STUDY

In line with regression models by Hall & Tu (2003; 2004) and Hall & Cook (2009), this
study is based on a cross-sectional model. Although the World Bank’s data set (the
RPED) is a panel study, in the current study the cross-sectional model is based on the
2002 responses (data). The 2002 data set is used in this study because it was the most
recent data set publically available as at the time the data set was retrieved. In addition it
was the data set with less missing data 9. As indicated in Chapter 4, Section 4.7, while
the RPED data set covers micro to large enterprises, only SMEs are used because the
focus of the study is to understand the exporting and non-exporting behaviour of this
group of firms.
In addition, SMEs are used because it has been established that Ghanaian firms are
mostly SMEs and so it is the target of the government to promote this cluster of firms to
reduce poverty (Abor & Quartey, 2010). In all, 110 SMEs were analysed from the 2002
data set for this chapter. The 110 firms consisted of 17 exporters and 93 non-exporters
(i.e., 15% exporters and 85% non-exporters). This total sample compares favourably

From Table 5.1, it can be seen that the average age of an exporting firm is two years
greater than that of a non-exporting counterpart, and while the average aggregate
educational level of a full-time employee in the exporting firms is 11, for the nonexporting firm it is 9 years. Moreover, despite the differences in the continuous

162

variables, the categorical variables also show different features between the two groups.
Table 5.2 indicates that micro firms do not export in Ghana and while medium-sized
firms account for 94% of exporters, small firms amount to only 6%. As regards
ownership characteristics, 59% of the exporting firms consist of firms with foreign
ownership, while only 11% of the non-exporting firms have foreign ownership. In terms
of sector category, the largest percentage (60%) of the exporting firms is involved in the
wood sector, followed by garments and textiles and the metal sub-sector.
Notwithstanding this, a high proportion of firms that do not export are also in the
garment and textile sub-sector, accounting for 32% of such firms, while the wood sector
is the least represented among the non-exporting firms, making up only 1%.

5.4

MODEL SPECIFICATION

The matter of whether or not a firm will export can be modelled by employing the linear
probability model (i.e. the probability of event happening), where Ordinary Least
Square (OLS) and/or linear probability model can be applied in a straightforward
manner. According to Gujarati (1999), if the linear probability model is used, the
predicted values may lie outside the values 0 & 1. Consequently, such a challenge limits
the OLS regression for a dichotomous dependent variable. Therefore, when the response
variable is qualitative, using OLS and/or linear probability model leads to
misspecification of the model. Following Hall & Cook (2009), the Cumulative
Distribution Function (CDF) that takes a logistic distribution is used to transform the
model so that results of the dependent variable can produce a probability value in the

163

range of 0 and 1. Once the model is correctly specified via the logit function, it prevents
omitted variable bias which could affect the direction of the causality.

5.5

THE LOGIT ECONOMETRIC MODEL

In accordance with Gujarati (1999: 449), the following econometric logit model was
adapted:
In(Pi/1-Pi)

where Pi is the probability of an SME becoming an exporter and (1 – Pi) is the
probability of an SME not becoming an exporter. The ratio Pi/ (1-Pi), known as the
odds ratio, is simply the odds in favour of an SME becoming an exporter. The natural
log of this odds ratio is called the log odds, and therefore the above model is known the
logit model, which means the log of the odds ratio is a linear function of the explanatory
variables. Here

is the constrained model whilst the

is the error term. The acronyms

in the model above are explained as follows, but because the garment & textile subsector is used as a reference group it does not appear in the model.

164

5.5.1

Measurement of Variables

The manner in which the variables (i.e. the dependent and the independent variables)
are measured in this thesis is explained below.
5.5.1.1

Dependent Variable

In this study, the export propensity is used as the dependent variable. Therefore,
whether the firm will export takes the value = 1, and should it not export, this takes the
value = 0, consistent with previous studies (e.g. Obben and Magagular, 2003;
Mittelstaedt et al., 2003; Hall & Tu, 2004; Hall & Cook, 2009).
5.5.1.2

Independent Variables

In accordance with previous studies, the independent variables in this study are
measured as follows:
1) Firm size (mdSZ/msz) - measured as the total number of full-time employees
consistent with researchers (e.g., Zhao & Zou, 2002; Hall & Tu, 2004; Hall &
Cook, 2009);
2) Firm age (fmage/fge) - measured as the number of years a firm has existed since
starting the operation - consistent with researchers, (e.g., Toni & Nassimbeni,
2001; Hall & Tu, 2003; Hall & Cook, 2009);
3) Human capital (workersedu/wdu ) - measured as the aggregate education level
of the full-time employees. Here the levels of education are defined as: average
of up to 6 years – primary education in Ghana, average of up to 10 years middle education in Ghana, average of up to 17 years - secondary education and
the average of up to 21 years – university or degree education in Ghana,
consistent with how the World Bank defined average aggregate education levels
in its original study and similar to researchers (Abor et al., 2008);
4) Foreign ownership (fownership/fsp) - measured as the firms whose ownership is
partly foreign and partly local - consistent with researchers, (e.g., Aitken et al.,
1997; Cerrato & Piva, 2007); the last variable is the ‘sector’ of the firms, where
the firms that operate wholly in the garments & textiles sub-sector are used as
the reference group and the other categories include:
5) Wood (wood/wod) – measured as firms wholly in the wood sub-sector
6) Metal (metal/mtl) - measured as firms wholly in the metal sub-sector
7) Chemical (chem/cml) – measured as firms wholly in the chemical sub-sector
165

8) Furniture (furn/fnl) – measured as firms wholly in the furniture sub-sector.

5.6 ESTIMATION, ANALYSIS AND THE LOGIT REGRESSION RESULTS

Unlike previous studies (e.g. Zhao & Zou, 2002; Hall & Tu, 2004; Hall & Cook, 2009)
which use SPSS for their data analysis, the Stata Statistical Software Package (SSSP) is
the statistical software used to run all the analysis in this chapter.
5.6.1

Stata Listwise Deletion Method

In this study Stata listwise method became appropriate due to sample missing variables.
The listwise method in Stata is not appropriate in Statistical Programme for Social
Sciences (SPSS) and so Stata became helpful and was used. The listwise method in
Stata Statistical Software Package (SSSP) makes it works slightly different from the
Statistical Programme for Social Sciences (SPSS) with regard to how it performs its
analysis and presentation of outputs. Following Acock (2008), a key inbuilt mechanism
within (listwise deletion method) differentiates it from SPSS. What listwise deletion
means is that, for example, in a regression analysis if say there are 5 explanatory
variables, but a case/ subject and/or firm did not supply data for one of these 5
explanatory variables, by default, Stata will delete that case from the regression analysis
thereby reporting a sample size based on cases that provided full data for each
variable.Here the reported sample size will be lower compared to when missing data
was not taken care of in the regression output. In this study because SSSP is used
throughout, all regression outputs are based on the listwise deletion (also called casewise deletion). So, for instance, in Models 1 & 2, (Tables 5.3 & 5.5) of the sample size

166

110 firms used in the regression analysis, only 103 were reported on; what that meant
was that 7 firms did not submit data for some of the independent variables and as a
result Stata deleted them by default in performing the regression analysis. In addition, in
Table 5.6, Model 3, when different regressors were put in the model, the sample size
changes 107 out of 110 because of the listwise deletion method. Further, in all the
regression models (models 1 – 3) bakery and machinery sub-sectors did not appear; the
reason is that because from Table 5.2 none of the two sub-sectors submitted data for
exporting activities (i.e. they do not participate in export business), Stata deleted them
by default.
In addition, in all the regression analysis (Tables 5.3 – 5.6), the garments and textiles
sub-sector is used as the reference group and so as the reference group they do not
appear in the regression output, and the reaming sub-sectors in the model are interpreted
in reference to the garments & textile sub-sector. Besides the fact that due to statistical
purposes there must be one reference group with regard to categorical variables in a
regression equation, here the garments & textiles sub-sector in particular is used
because it is the focus of the study, as indicated in Chapter 1, Section 1.7, given the
country context (Chapter 3, sub-Section, 3.3.1).
5.6.2

Stata Stepwise Logistic Regression Process

Following Chen et al. (2003), like the SPSS, Stata uses the stepwise method to arrive at
the final model that best fits the observed data. Stata logistic regression is based on
maximum likelihood, which involves iterative procedures by comparing model to model
depending which one fits the data. The iteration is a step by step process, consisting of
listing of the log likelihoods at each iteration. The first iteration is called iteration zero;

167

in this iteration, Stata selects only the constant model to assess the fitness of the model
to data. This is called the log likelihood of the constrained model (the null or empty
model), that is, a model with no predictors. In the next iteration/step, after the
constrained model, the predictor(s) are put in the model. At each step and/or iteration,
the log likelihood increases because the aim is to maximise the log likelihood.
Here the Stata examines the variables in the model to ascertain whether or not one or
some should be removed via the likelihood ratio statistic (LR stats). For instance, if the
removal of the regressor (s) will make a significant variation with regard to how well
the model fits data then software maintains the regressor (s). When the variation
between successive iterations is very small, the model is said to have ‘converged’; then
the iteration is stopped and the results are produced. Here the likelihood ratio statistics
will be produced with its corresponding p-value. This p-value assesses the probability of
ascertaining the chi-square statistic, granting that the null hypothesis is the case. Here
the null hypothesis is that none of the variables is different from zero and/or the model
does not fit the data. Therefore, the p-value is compared to the chi-square statistic (LR
stats) to determine the fitness of the final model to the data. Finally, the p-value, is then
compared to a critical value (e.g. .05 or .01) in order to decide whether or not the final
model is statistically significant and the fitness of the final full model is judged. So, if
the critical value is below .05 the null hypothesis will be rejected. In this study, for
example, the likelihood ratio statistics of the models (Models 1-4) are statistically
significant, supporting the final model from each output.

168

5.6.3

The Z-Statistic in Stata Logit Regression Analysis

In a linear regression, the interest is not only in how the overall model fits the data, but
in addition, the contribution of the individual explanatory variables. In Ordinary Least
Square (OLS) linear regression, this can be identified through the t-statistic (i.e. the
estimated coefficient divided by their standard errors). In logistic regression, the statistic
is called ‘Wald statistic’ (Field, 2009). While SPSS software reports the Wald statistic,
Stata reports it as Z-statistic as can be seen in Models 1 – 4) (Acock, 2008). So here,
similar to the reporting of the Wald statistic (usually) by SPSS logistic output, Stata
reported the Z-statistic from its logistic regression outputs produced throughout the
study. Therefore the Z- value is used in Stata is based on 2-tailed hypothesis and an
associated p-value to assess the null hypothesis that none of the coefficients
(parameters) of regressors is different from zero similar to Wald statistic in SPSS.

5.6.4

Pseudo R-Squared from Stata Logistic Regression Output

The kind of pseudo-R2 that Stata statistical software produces is the one recommended
by McFadden (1973, cited in Kohler & Kreuter, 2005). The McFadden r-squared is seen
by some statisticians as better measure of fit for the logit model. For instance, the Cox
& Snell, r-squared cannot reach the maximum value of 1 as such and is not the best
measure of a good fit (Hair et al., 1998). Therefore the Stata logistic r-squared used in
the regression models in this study is based on McFadden’s (1973) pseudo r-squared.
Field (2009) argues that unlike OLS estimation, where R2 is normally employed in order
to ascertain the amount of variation based on the explanatory variables with regard to
the dependent, in logistic regression there is no such comparable or generally accepted
R2 measure of model fitness, and therefore, different authors have suggested different

169

statistics. While a common measure with regard to logistic regression is the pseudo-R2,
yet according to Kohler and Kreuter (2005), various types of pseudo-R2 can be found in
the literature, such as pseudo-R2 by Veall and Zimmermann & Long (1994 cited in
Kohler & Kreuter, 2005) and by McFadden (1973, cited in Kohler & Kreuter, 2005). So
the type of pseudo r-squared statistic used in the regression model is clearly specified.
In Tables 5.3 – 5.6 below, the regression outputs are presented.

The correlation analysis in Table 5.4 is used to assess the strength and direction of
linear relationship between two variables. The Pearson correlation coefficient (r) takes
the values from -1 to +1. -1 means perfect negative correlation while +1 implies perfect
positive correlation. A perfect correlation of either -1 and/or +1 means that the value of
one variable can be determined exactly, just by knowing the value of the other variable,
while zero correlation implies no relationship (Pallant, 2007). Following Pallant (2007)
positive correlation means as one variable increases so does the other and the reverse is
true with regard to negative correlation. In the table below, Model 2’s output is
presented.

business initiations for the SMEs in Ghana.

5.7 ROBUST AND DIAGNOSTIC CHECKS OF THE LOGIT REGRESSION
RESULTS

Other tests were also performed in order to confirm whether or not the regression results
above could be claimed to be robust. The first robust check is that the workers’
education variable was recorded as a dichotomous variable (though originally
continuous variable) in order to confirm the effect of the continuous variable on the
export propensity as found in the regression results in Model 2 above. As a result, the
workers’ education is coded as workers with secondary education and above = 1, or
otherwise (middle school and below) = 0 (Table 5.2). Therefore, the variable secondary
education and above (secedu&above) which equals one (1) and zero otherwise replaced
the workers’ education (workersedu) originally as in Model 2, and regressed in another
model called Model 4. The result of Model 4 (Table 5.7) is presented below. The results
in Table 5.7 show that when workers’ education was changed to a dummy variable,
firms which have workers with secondary education and above are more likely to enter
the export market compared to those that have workers with middle school education
and below. The results are shown in the table below.

The second robust check is that the likelihood ratio tests in Models 1 – 4 are all
significant, indicating that the models fit the data. Moreover, in order to further confirm
the results of the individual parameters in the models 1 – 4 above, the independent t-test
and chi-square test were performed on both the continuous and categorical variables
respectively. According to Pallant (2007), the chi-square test of independence ascertains
whether or not a relationship exists between two categorical variables, while the
independent sample t-test compares the means of normally distributed interval variables
for two groups. The t-tests and the chi-square tests are presented in Tables 5.8 and 5.9
respectively.
