5.8

DISCUSSION

From the regression results above, the following variables (i.e. firm age, firms from
furniture, metal and the chemical sub-sectors) became statistically non-significant. The
implications of these variables are that, from Models 1 and 2, the negative signs
177

associated with furniture and Chemical sub-sectors imply that firms from these subsectors have less likelihood of entering the export market, compared to those in the
garment and textile sub-sector, even though, the negative relationships were not
significant. In addition, the positive sign associated with the metal sub-sector implies
that firms from metal sub-sector have a likelihood of entering the export market; even
though, here the positive relationship was also not statistically significant. Moreover,
firm’s age did not predict any better the response variable (export propensity), which
contradicts one of the assumptions in the stage theory. The positive non-significant
effect of the age variable (Models 2, 3, & 4) in this study supports the arguments of the
born global researchers (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Zahra, 2005; Schulz et al., 2009)
who contend that as firm grow in years structure inertia causes them to be less
competitive and so H2 is not supported. The finding of firm age in this study confirms
what was found in Sweden (Svante et al., 2004), but contradicts what was found in
Canada (McNaughton, (2003).
With regard to the significant variables, in the light of the results above, it can be argued
that the positive effect of medium-sized firm compared to small firm predicts the
decision to initiate export business from Ghana. This confirms the importance of fixed
costs and the economies of scale hypotheses associated with export business (e.g. Hall
& Tu, 2004; Mittelstaedt et al., 2003, 2006; Hall & Cook, 2009). The fixed cost
hypothesis with regard to small business export behaviour implies that export business
is attached to fixed costs (e.g. sunk cost) which exporting firms cannot avoid. Large
firms will have the wherewithal to meet the fixed cost associated with export business
and will find it easier to respond positively to export stimuli and initiate export business
compared to SMEs.

178

In addition, because of the same fixed cost issue associated with export business, large
firms that enjoy economies of scale advantages will be less affected by the burden of the
fixed cost compared to the smaller size firms. Most researchers (e.g. Hall & Tu, 2004;
Mittelstaedt et al., 2003, 2006; Hall & Cook, 2009) use these two factors as a significant
justification for the importance of size on export decisions.
Based on this finding, the assumptions of the RBV and the stage models can be
supported as plausible theoretical frameworks for understanding SMEs’ export
decisions. This result also confirms the findings of Hall & Tu (2004) in the UK, the
USA (Mittelstaedt et al., 2003 & 2006) and in Jamaica (Williams, 2008). Over all, H1 is
supported.
The positive effect of having a partly foreign ownership compared to having a wholly
domestic owned firm to facilitate export initiation is also supported, which confirms the
importance of the export spillover hypothesis (Aitken et al., 1997; Greenaway et al.,
2004; Cerrato & Piva, 2007). Export spillover hypothesis assumes that formation of
alliances with foreign business by indigenous firms facilitates their (the indigenous
firms) export initiation. Furthermore, this finding consolidates the assumption of
network theory (Literature Review sub-Section 3.4.3.3) concerning human and other
capital resources, whereby alliances and/or partnerships with foreign partners contribute
to the resource capacity of the indigenous firms (Johanson & Mattson, 1988). In this
way, H3 is supported.
Another significant positive result is that the aggregate average full-time worker
education facilitates the firm’s transition from a wholly domestic business firm to an
international one, and theoretically the tenets of the RBV and the stage theory are

179

supported. Empirically, this finding confirms what Alvarex (2006) found in Chile, as
well as what Cerrato (2007) discovered in Italy. Therefore, H4 is supported. The
positive effect of the sector variable supports researchers who argue that the external
environment exerts weight on whether or not the small business will initiate export
business and not solely the influence from a firm’s internal resource capacity. In this
study, firms in the wood sector are found to be more likely to export compared to firms
in the garment and textile industry. On the basis of this finding, the tenets of the
contingency theory (e.g. industry’s influence on small business propensity to export) are
supported. This finding contradicts Javalgi et al. (2000) as regards the importance of the
wood sector and export propensity. In the study of Javalgi et al. (2000), it was
discovered that firms involved in production of electrical and electronic machinery,
equipment and supplies as well as rubber and miscellaneous plastic products are more
likely to change from wholly domestic business to initiating export business, whereas
those in the lumber and wood products industries are the least likely to export. Robert &
Tybout (1997) also discovered that, in Colombia, in comparison to firms in the wood
industry, firms involved in textiles and garments as well as those in the chemical
industry were more likely to change from a wholly domestically focused to an
internationally focused strategy. Overall, the findings indicate that external uncontrolled
factors play an important role in the SMEs’ decision concerning whether or not to
initiate export business (Zou & Stan, 1998; Sousa et al., 2008).
5.8.1

Conclusions

First of all, with regard to the logit regression output there is more likelihood that
medium-sized firm, those with an ‘educated’ workforce, those that have foreign
ownership structure, coupled with those that operate in the wood sector, will respond
180

positively to export stimuli and enter the export business. However, the significant
results from the logit model imply that the majority of non-exporting SMEs in Ghana
are small in size, have workers with low levels of formal education, do not have foreign
ownership and operate in sectors which are not export oriented. Following from these
findings, there is research evidence (e.g. Weasthead et al., 2004; Ibeh, 2005; Ibeh &
Wheeler, 2005; Ruzzier et al., 2006; Williams, 2008; Lages et al., 2009) to support the
argument that the export success of SMEs with regard to its decisions whether or not to
export resides highly within its internal capacity. While this is important, it must be
noted that the influence of the external environment plays an important role in
explaining the export behaviour of the SME in detail. This supports the argument of
researchers (Zou & Stan, 1998; Zhao & Zou, 2002; Sousa et al., 2008) who contend that
the phenomenon of the SME’s export behaviour cannot be fully understood if the
influence of the external environment is not considered together with the internal
environment.
Due to the positive significant effect of the firm size (the stage theory and the RBV),
worker’s education (the stage theory and the RBV), foreign ownership (the network
theory and RBV) and the sector/ and industry influence (the contingency theory), it can
be argued that the external validity of the proposed integrated theoretical framework
behind the thesis is supported. In addition, these findings support the argument that
because the export behaviour of the SME is a complex event, a single theoretical
framework is inadequate to shed full light on the phenomenon (e.g. Coviello & Martin,
1999; Crick & Spence, 2005; Coviello & Martin, 2006; Rialp et al., 2006). As a result,
the integrated theoretical approach may offer a more robust understanding of the SME
export behaviour than researchers (e.g. Bell, 1995; Coviello & Munro, 1995; Reuber &

181

Fischer, 1997; Westhead et al., 2001; Moen & Servais, 2002; Ibeh, 2005; Williams,
2008) who use the single theoretical framework to address the same topic.
As foreign ownership became statistically significant with regard to the export
propensity, this indicates that the internationalisation process may not be as gradual as
assumed in the stage theory, but that through access to the network partners’ resources
(among those having a foreign partner) the pace can be faster. This finding supports the
assumption of the network theory (Johanson & Mattsson, 1988; Gorman & Evers, 2008)
as incorporated in the proposed integrated framework.

5.9

CONCLUDING REMARKS

Following the logistic regression model, the variables which discriminate best between
exporting and non-exporting small and medium-sized owner-managed firms from
Ghana are: firm size, aggregate full-time workers’ education level, sector and foreign
ownership structure. Firm size relates to the stage theory and RBV, aggregate full-time
workers’ education level relates to the RBV and the stage theory, the influence of sector
relates to the contingency theory, while foreign ownership relates to the RBV and the
network theory. This suggests that the integrated theoretical framework explains SMEs
export business in more detail than a single theoretical framework. Furthermore, while a
combination of various types of resources is critical in enhancing a firm’s ability to
enter the export market, the strength of these findings is that they are visible, accessible
and measurable. As a result, it offers policy makers a clear picture of the type of firms
they need to target with regard to stimulating export business.
182

This chapter along with the literature review (Chapter 2), which examined the stimuli
that could stimulate the decision to enter export markets, indicates that to initiate export
business, factors within the SME’s

environment (external environment and/or

contingency factors) as well as its internal resource capacity are crucial in driving
export behaviour. Both chapters have offered insights into why some firms choose to
enter the export market while similar others facing similar market conditions choose to
focus on the domestic market. While the World Bank data set used in this chapter is
based on five small and medium-sized manufacturing sub-sectors (i.e. garment &
textile, furniture, metal, chemical and wood), the two remaining empirical chapters
(Chapters 6 & 7) are based on the garment & textile sub-sectors, as it is the main focus
of the thesis.

CHAPTER SIX
CRITICAL INCIDENTS WHICH TRIGGER EXPORT
INITIATION: EVIDENCE FROM THE GARMENT &

IN

EMPIRICAL

RESEARCH PART 2
6.1

INTRODUCTION

This chapter addresses research question two and seeks to achieve research objective
three of the thesis. As set in Chapter 1, Section 1.7 and Chapter 3, sub-Section 3.3.1, the
whole of the interview portion of the thesis (this chapter and Chapter 7) is focused on
the garment & textile sub-sector. This is also in line with Chapter 5 where the garment
and textile sub-sector is used in the regression models as the main reference group,
unlike the furniture sub-sector or any other sub-sector. The chapter uses the case study
interview method to explore the critical incidents that trigger export initiation and the
factors that facilitate the capacity of SMEs to meet their export orders.
In these interviews, all the owner-managers interviewed were with the case-studies at
the inception of the businesses and when the critical incidents took place. In addition,
the data collected with regard to the firm size (sub-Section 6.4.2.2) indicate the data on
the date of the interviews rather than the date of the critical incidents took place.
Following the assumptions of the individual theoretical frameworks integrated in the
literature review, in this chapter, it is hypothesised that the following factors will

184

emerge as triggers of export initiation during the interviews (by way of critical
incidents). Of the variables from the integrated theoretical framework that inform the
thesis, the following are hypothesised as critical incidents in line with the definition of
critical incidents in the study (sub-section 6.2.5.1). First of all, among the factors to be
explored from the stage theory are: (1) psychic distance and (2) being in receipt of
unsolicited order. Second, the factors to be explored from the network theory include
both formal (e.g. networking relations with other businesses, foreign partners) and
informal (e.g. networking relations with family and friends) relations.
Third, factors to be explored from the international entrepreneurship theory included:
(1) previous residency and work abroad and (2) previous overseas travel (i.e.
international orientation). Finally, government action is also selected from the
contingency theory to be explored in the interview. Besides these factors, it is further
hypothesised that the following factors will act as influences to facilitate the capacity of
the firms to supply their export orders. First, the factors to be examined from the stage
theory are: (1) firm size, (2) firm age, and (3) psychic distance. Second, the factors from
the network theory include both formal (e.g. networking relations with other businesses,
industrial associations, foreign partners/foreign ownership) and informal (e.g.
networking relations with family and friends) relations. Third, the factors from the
international entrepreneurship theory are: (1) alertness to profitable international
opportunities, (2) international orientation, (3) international business vision and (4)
international risk-taking behaviour.

185

The RBV factors are: (1) financial capital, (2) amount of full-time employees, and (3)
stock of equipments and machineries. Lastly, the influence of sector is the contingency
variable selected for examination. The themes selected for exploration had emerged as
being influential in the literature review.
In Table 6.1 below, the frequency of non-exporting among each sub-sector is presented.
The table is based only on non-exporting firms from exporting sectors and so the bakery
and machinery sub-sectors that do not participate in exporting business are excluded.

textile sub-sector is the focus of the thesis (Chapter 1, Section 1.7 and Chapter 3, subSection 3.3.1), the interviews are entirely based on this sub-sector. This flows through
from Tables 5.3 to 5.6 in Chapter 5 where the garment & textile sub-sector is used as
the reference group in the regression models.

6.2

BRIEF BACKGROUND OF THE GARMENT & TEXTILE SUBSECTOR IN GHANA

The garment and textile sub-sector is one of the dominant sectors of Ghana’s
Manufacturing Sector (Quartey, 2006; Kuffour, 2008). The sub-sector is comprised
mainly of SMEs, with a few large firms, namely the Ghana Textile Manufacturing
Company (GTMC), Akosombo Textile Limited (ATL), Ghana Textile Products (GTP)
and Printex Ghana Ltd. Firms in the sub-sector produce for individual consumption and
organisations such as schools, corporate bodies as well as other government institutions,
including the police, the army and hospitals (among others), both at home and abroad.
Indigenous firms dominate the industry, and only 5% are partly foreign and partly
locally-owned, while the rest (95 percent) are wholly under local ownership (Quartey,
2006).
African prints, (e.g. wax, java (typical in Ghana)), fancy clothes (also common in
Ghana), bed sheets, and school uniforms, as well as household fabrics (e.g. curtain
materials, kitchen napkins, diapers and towels) are among the sector’s products. Others
include knitted blouses and socks, and bleached cotton fabrics, known as tie and dye or
batik clothes, constitute another major product of the sector. Tie and dye is a product
187

where firms use handmade designs. Traditional or indigenous textiles called Kente
clothes (e.g. traditional woven fabric), Adinkra clothes (e.g. traditional hand-printed
fabrics) and other types of woven fabrics (such as smocks) are also popular products.

6.3

METHODOLOGY

This methodology addresses the procedures applied to explore the themes in Section 6.1
above.

6.3.1

Industry Selection

In line with the discussion of Table 6.1 coupled with Chapter 1, Section 1.7 and Chapter
3, sub-Section 3.3.1, this section of the thesis is limited to the garment and textile subsector. In addition to the policy reasons for selecting the garment & textile sub-sector as
the main sub-sector of the thesis, as outlined in Chapter 3, researchers (e.g. Coviello &
Munro, 1997; Coviello & Martin, 1999; Chetty, 1999) argue that using one sector
minimises the impact of inter-industry variance.
6.3.2

The Sample Frame & Sample Selection

The sample frame used for this portion of the thesis was compiled from data sources
from three institutions in Ghana. The Association of Ghana Industries (AGI), the Ghana
Statistical Service (GSS) and the Ministry of Trade & Industry (MoTI) were the main
institutions which provided separate sample frames used in this study. The separate

188

sample frames from each of these institutions were compiled into one sample frame and
applied in the study. The features of the sample frame consisted of:
•

the name of the firms (from small to large firms);

•

the name of the owner-manager or manageress, including his or her contact
number(s)

•

the locations and the addresses of the firms;

•

the sector of the firms;

61 garment and textile exporting firms were identified from the sample frame. Of these
47 firms became useable. The reason for this is that two companies’ owner-managers
were found to have passed away prior to the interviews, while another 12 firms were
confirmed to have ceased trading. Of the 47 firms left, 36 were interviewed because
they met the screening criteria behind the interviews, as set out in sub-Section 6.3.4.
The 36 firms interviewed were all continuous exporters.
6.3.3

Criteria for Selecting the Companies

Before the interviews started, telephone calls were made to the firms wherein the
researcher introduced himself and the study (see Appendix M for the detailed procedure
of the interview process and some of the problems encountered). In the course of the
initial telephone call, prospective interviewees were made aware that for them to be part
of the study; they must meet the criteria as set out below. As a result, every firm that
participated in the study met the following criteria:
•

The firm must be small or medium-sized in order to adhere to the definition of
SMEs as put forward in the World Bank’s study on Ghana (Chapter 4, Section

189

4.7). In this analysis, only small and medium-sized firms were selected because
the data set showed that the micro small firms in Ghana do not participate in
export business (Chapter 5, Tables 5.2);
•

The firm must be engaged only in the garment and textile sub-sector (because it
is the only sector of interest) and located in Accra (Accra is the most industryconcentrated area in Ghana (Abor et al., 2008). Here the study is limited to
Accra because Ghana is all about Accra.

•

The exporting firm must have a minimum of two years of export experience,
excluding domestic market experience;

•

The firm should be independent and indigenous, or partly foreign and partly
local (foreign ownership is defined in this thesis in Chapter 5, sub-Section
5.5.3), but it should not be a subsidiary of a larger domestic or international
firm, in order to avoid influence from the potential availability of resources on
its export decision-making.

6.3.4

The Data Collection Instrument and Method

Case study interviews were the main data collection instrument used to collect the
critical incidents from the perspectives of the owner-managers. The interviews followed
a semi-structured interview guide, which is presented in Appendix L. In the interview
process, the owner-managers of SMEs were interviewed because the intention was to
use the key informant technique. The key informant technique (e.g., SMEs research that
targets mostly owner-managers with regard to the data collection process) is mostly
used in the SMEs’ research field. For example, researchers (e.g. Chetty, 1999; Coviello
& Martin, 1999; Evangelista, 2005; Coviello & Cox, 2006; Williams, 2008) have
argued that because almost everything concerning the SME rests with its owner-

190

manager: in contrast with large multinational firms, adequate and reliable information
about this business is mostly obtained from the owner-managers. However, in one case
study (case firm 8), the marketing manager was interviewed because the owner was
unable to give time for the interview. With this one exception, in all other firms, the
owner was interviewed.
The interview guide used in the interview process imposed order and structure on the
interview process. Sample statements in the interview guide include: (1) tell me a story
by describing when, how and why you formed this firm, (2) how many workers do you
have (3) describe when, how and why your firm has been involved in export business up
until now. In accordance with Coviello & Cox (2006), words such as ‘what’, ‘why’,
‘when’ and ‘how’ were included as the tools to probe for details in order to facilitate
richness and depth of data. Such interjections also encouraged the informant to talk
more. For example, one owner-manageress (case study 4) declared she would only have
30 minutes for me when she agreed to be part of the study, but by using these probes,
we were able to converse for one hour and 26 minutes.
The interview process took an average of 130 minutes. Every interview was digitally
recorded, and was simultaneously supplemented by handwritten notes. The handwritten
notes were included because this allowed the triangulation of all the information given
by interviewees for analysis. As a result, there was reliability of data generation. Whilst
the digital recorder enabled every detail of the interview conversations to be captured, it
also enabled the researcher to handwrite only the key incidents as well as facilitating
each interview in a relaxed manner.

191

In accordance with McAuley (1999), all the handwritten interviews were read and reread at the close of each interview day and where a lack of clarity was detected, the
digital recording was replayed. Where clarity could not be achieved by such
triangulation, the interviewee was contacted first thing the next day by mobile telephone
for clarification. The rigour of the handwritten notes provided the researcher with a
prior opportunity to understand the critical events that triggered the export initiation of
the firms, including the variables that influenced the ability of firms to meet the export
orders, before the interviews were transcribed verbatim. Face-to-face interviews were
used throughout and the interview process took three months (July – September 2009).
The interviews ran from Monday to Saturday with a maximum of 2 interviews per day
and a minimum of 1 per day.
Two interviews (i.e. case studies 6 and 24) took place outside the premises of the firms.
In each of these two case firms, the owners had missed their first appointments because
of their changing commitments, and therefore the interviews were rescheduled. All the
other interviews took place on the premises of the firms in the owner-managers’ offices.
Moreover, except for one interview, all the interviews were conducted in the English
language. The reason for this is that as Ghana used to be a British colony, English has
been Ghana’s official language ever since; hence, it is commonly spoken in business
dealings. The owner-manageress (case study 23) who could not express herself in
English spoke her mother tongue, ‘Twi’ (a common dialect spoken by about three
quarters of the Ghanaian population). Her story was also recorded and written
concurrently, and then transcribed into English.
Some of the interviewees switched off their mobile phones voluntarily during the
interview process while others did not because they claimed that if they did, they might
192

miss an important business call. As a result, random telephone calls to interviewees
threatened to disruption to the data creation process. Consequently, when an interviewee
received a call during the interview process, the theme (e.g. the main phrase) which she
or he was commenting on before the phone call would be quickly written down and the
digital recorder was turned off. When the interviewee finished answering the call, he or
she would be reminded of the phrase he or she was commenting on before the call
came. In accordance with this device, the interviewing process went smoothly, even
with respondents who did not switch their phones off during the interview.
6.3.5

The Analytical Method

As indicated in the methodology section of the thesis (Chapter 4, Sections 4.4.1 - 4.4.2)
critical incident and content analysis were the main analytical techniques used to guide
the data collection and analysis of the interview transcripts.
6.3.5.1

Criteria for Identification of Critical Incidents in the Study

Before arriving at the main findings with regard to the critical incidents, each incident
was screened in accordance with the criteria set out in the critical incident method. The
critical incident technique involves a set of prescribed criteria, originally suggested by
Flanagan (1954), which are often adapted by researchers to suit their various studies
(e.g. Chell, 2004; Neupert et al., 2006; Zineldin, 2007) . Therefore, drawing from the
examples of these researchers, the themes (i.e. critical incidents and facilitating factors)
which emerged from the interview transcripts were required to meet the following
criteria:

193

•

First, the incident had to be ‘critical’. What constitutes a critical incident is
debatable as it varies from study to study. For instance, in the study of Chell
(2004), the author mentioned that if a particular incident led to the demise of the
firm being investigated, the event is considered as critical. In addition, in the
study undertaken by Zineldin (2007), the author argued that if an event led
directly to the establishment of a Swedish company in Vietnam, this was
considered critical.
Moreover, in the work of Neupert et al. (2006), the authors argued that if the
respondent could establish that an incident was the worst possible thing that
could happen or an enormous challenge to conducting international business,
this was also considered critical. The pattern from these studies clearly implies
what constitutes a critical event, and the factors are informed by the research
question and the purpose of the study. In this study, an incident as well as a
factor (influence) can be considered critical on two grounds. First, from the
perspective of the respondent, an incident should directly trigger export
initiation (e.g. being in receipt of unsolicited order); in addition, the factor
and/or the influence (e.g. adequate financial resources), from the perspective of
the interviewee should facilitate the firm’s capacity to deliver the export order.
Second, a critical incident leading to export business initiation should be
different from an ‘influence’ and/or ‘facilitating factor’. This implies that a
critical incident should relate to an ‘occurrence’ which the exporter encountered
(e.g. contact established due to prior international travel), while factors that aid
the firm’s capacity to supply the export order should be in the form of an
influence (e.g. having sufficient factory hands);

194

•

The interviewee should be able to remember the incident with the help of the
probes incorporated into the interview process (examples of the probes include:
‘You said you export, but have you exported to the UK, US, Italy before?’; ‘You
said your first export destination was in the Netherlands, but how about Canada,
Nigeria, and Norway?; ‘You said the year of your first export was in ….. (e.g.
1998). Did you also export in 1999, 2000, 2001… 2009; ‘How and what was the
outcome?’). Such probing strategies clearly triggered memories easily and
quickly and steered the interview smoothly;

•

The incident as well as the influence must relate directly to export initiation and
influence the capacity of the firms specifically to meet their export orders , even
where the firm serves both the export and domestic markets;

•

There must be sufficient detail for both the interviewer and the interviewee to
understand information regarding, for example, the ‘What? ‘When?’ and ‘How?’
of things.

Based on the above criteria, any themes and/or factors which did not meet the above
standards were excluded from the analysis. In addition, it is important to indicate that
not everything the interviewees articulated was included in the analysis. As a result,
responses about what motivated them to form their businesses and the processes
involved were not analysed because some particular questions were asked (included in
the interview guide) with the sole aim of encouraging the interviewees to talk more and
to make the discussion sequential and interesting. In some cases, some new themes
emerged out of the conversational interviews as critical incidents. Although these were
not among those initially selected to be explored from the literature review, as they met
the above criteria (sub-Section 6.3.5.1), they have been included in the analysis.

195

For instance, contacts established at trade fairs (critical incident triggering export
initiation), membership of many industrial associations, frequent interactions with
industrial associations, having a human resource incentive package, having skilled
production workers, having prior domestic market experience (facilitating capacity to
meet export orders), tying the long-term business growth goal to export market and
having a positive perception of the export market

were among the factors which

emerged out of the interviews as critical incidents and influential factors which
triggered the export initiation and all of these facilitated the capacity of the firm to meet
their export orders.

6.4

RESULTS & DATA ANALYSIS

The findings and analyses of the interview transcripts consist of three parts, namely:
•
•
•

Profile of the case firms
Results on critical incidents
Results on factors that influenced that capacity of the firms’ to supply their
export orders

.
6.4.1

Analysis of the Profile of the Case Studies

The number of firms (36 firms) interviewed does not allow the opportunity to describe
the profile of each case company, unlike in other studies, which have sampled a few
firms and were able to describe the profile of each (e.g. Oviatt & McDougall, 1995;
Arenius, 2005; Nummela et al., 2006). This study is in accordance with other
researchers (e.g. Coviello & Munro, 1995; MacDonald & Cook, 1998; McAuley, 1999;
196

Chetty & Campbell-Hunt, 2003; Bell et al., 2004; Gemser et al., 2004; Evangelista,
2005; Hutchinson & Quinn, 2005; Hutchinson et al., 2006; Chetty & Agndal, 2007),
who sampled many firms and therefore summarised and combined the profile of their
case studies. In a similar manner, the table below summarises the profile of the firms
interviewed in the research.

199

In Table 6.2, the mean exporting experience of the firms was 8 years (see Appendix N,
page 3 for the calculations). Here, the mean age of exporting experience of Ghana’s
firms varies from, for example, what Neupert et al. (2006) found in Idaho (USA) and
Vietnam. In that study, the researchers indicated that the mean exporting experience of
the firms in the USA and Vietnam was 13 and 10 years respectively. It can be argued
that the variations may be due to differences in economic development among these
countries; the US and Vietnam are more advanced economically compared to Ghana,
and so it is plausible to find variety in the mean age of firms in terms of their exporting
experience. Furthermore, the average age (from formation until the time of the
interview) of the case study firm was 14 years whilst the average size of the workforce
was 28. This workforce size implies that garment and textile firms in Ghana are
generally small and may partly explain why most firms in the sub-sector choose to
concentrate on the domestic market. This supports the regression results in Chapter 5,
since in the logistic regression results it was found that the medium-sized firms from
Ghana will be more likely to export compared to small firms. As a result, the firm size
issue may partly explain the low propensity to export among the garment and textile
sector in Ghana.
Moreover, the average length of domestic operating experience before the
commencement of export business is six years. This figure deviates from the average of
three years, which Coviello & Munro (1997) found among New Zealand’s firms when
they commenced export business. This disparity with regard to average domestic market
experience before the time of commencing export business may be due to inequalities in
socio-economic development between New Zealand and Ghana. Furthermore, the
variation could be due to the differences in the sample populations. While this study

200

uses the sample from the garment and textile sub-sector, Coviello and Munro sampled
software firms. Previous studies (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Oviatt & McDougall,
1994; Rialp et al., 2005; Hall & Cook, 2009) have established that software firms begin
to export sooner after their formation than firms in other sub-sectors.
From the profile of the case studies in Table 6.2, two interviews in the study are
considered outliers in terms of the mean length of domestic market experience before
commencing export business. The two firms were able to start exporting two years after
their formation; these are firms 2 and 11. When their interview transcripts were looked
at critically, it was found that the owner of case study firm 2 is a business woman who
has lived in the US for most of her life. She stated that she moved to Ghana to establish
her export business in order to maximise the AGOA initiative. She revealed that she
made all the contacts about her buyers before leaving for Ghana to start the business.
Her great propensity to export supports the positive relationship between the
entrepreneur’s international orientation (e.g. previous residency abroad and/or previous
work experience abroad) and export propensity knowledge. For example, her story also
confirms the findings of Hall & Cook (2009) in the UK and Ruzzier et al. (2007) in
Slovenia concerning the positive relationship between the entrepreneur’s international
orientation and the export proclivity of the UK and Slovenian firms respectively.
The second outlier (case 11) is a foreign owned firm (i.e. partly foreign-owned and
partly locally owned). The Ghanaian partner here indicated that their speed in entering
the export market can be explained by human capital resources and the network contacts
of his two foreign partners (an American and a Chinese man). This evidence also
supports the significant positive effect of foreign ownership and the likelihood of export
(as found in the quantitative analysis - Chapter 5). In the quantitative analysis, both the
201

logistic regression and the chi-square results confirm the significant positive
relationship between foreign ownership and export propensity. As a result, the case
study evidence in Ghana confirms what has been found in other countries regarding the
positive effect of foreign ownership and the propensity to export. Examples of countries
where this positive effect has been found include: (1) the UK (Greenaway et al., 2004),
(2) Italy (Cerrato & Piva, 2007), and (3) China (Zhang & Song, 2000). Only one firm
had part foreign and part local ownership (foreign ownership) among the 36 firms
interviewed. Therefore, like the small average size of the garment and textile firms in
Ghana, the fact that very few are under foreign ownership may also account for the
sector’s low export involvement in general. This suggestion is corroborated by the
secondary data analysis finding that firms with foreign ownership are more likely to
export compared to their counterparts that are wholly local.
Approximately 72 % of the owners of the firms are females. This supports the finding
by Kuffour (2008) that 90% of those employed in the garment and textile sub-sector in
Ghana are women, representing both workers and owner-entrepreneurs. Overall, the
case firms can be classified into four groups, namely fashion firms, garment firms,
textile firms and fabric firms, although all of them fall under the umbrella of the
‘garment and textile sub-sector in Ghana’. Examples of the products the case firms
produce include smocks, ladies wear, menswear, batik or tie and dye, fabric, kante,
napkins, cushion covers, bedspreads, chair backs, curtains and toys among others,
similar to the information already presented in Section 6.2 above. South Africa, the US,
the UK, Switzerland, Holland and Belgium are among the export destinations
mentioned by case firms.

202

6.4.2

Critical Incident Results & Analysis

This analysis concerns the factors which were selected from the literature review to be
explored in the interview process and which met the criteria in sub-section 6.3.6.1. In an
approach consistent with other research (e.g. Coviello & Munro, 1997; MacDonald &
Cook, 1998; McAuley, 1999; Coviello & Martin, 1999; Huggins, 2000; Hutchinson et
al., 2006), selected direct quotes from the interviewees which indicate how a particular
theme was crucial in triggering export initiations are used to aid the analysis. Also in
line with other similar studies (e.g. Coviello & Muro, 1997; Hutchinson et al., 2006),
the original names of the firms have not been used, but are instead replaced with
numbers (e.g. case studies 1, 2, 3).

203

6.4.2.1

Summary of Findings – Critical Incidents & Critical Influence

It can be stated that the hypothesis with regard to the critical incidents stated at the
outset of the chapter (Section 6.1) was only partially supported, as from the 2 stage
theory factors specified in Section 6.1, only receipt of an unsolicited order triggered
export initiation (supported). Contacts and recommendations gained via having foreign
ownership as well as having friends and family abroad were the two main network
factors which triggered export initiation among the 4 network theory factors initially
selected. Consequently, among the 2 international entrepreneurship theory factors only
previous residency and work abroad (international orientation) triggered export
initiation. Winning government awards (i.e. government action) was fully supported, as
hypothesised, as a contingency theory factor in triggering export initiation among the
firms. Contacts established via trade fair participation constituted the remaining
incident. Overall six main critical incidents were found.
These critical incidents are categorised using Leonidou et al.’s (2007) dimensions
concerning triggers of export behaviour of firms. According to Leonidou et al., triggers
of export initiation are classified on the basis of whether or not they fall under a firm’s:
(1) proactive internal behaviour, (2) proactive external behaviour, (3) reactive internal
behaviour and (4) reactive external behaviour. The six critical incidents are categorised
based on Leonidou et al.’s dimensions in the figure below.

3

Figure 6.1 indicates that overall the incidents which initiate export business are
triggered by an external stimulus. With regard to their relative differences, contacts
established via participation in trade shows represent the leading sparks of export
initiation among the garment and textile firms. Contacts established as a result of having
international orientation represent the next most significant trigger of export initiation,
while contacts and recommendations gained via having family and friends abroad
followed next. The fourth ranked incident was winning a government award, whilst the
receipt of unsolicited orders ranked as the fifth most critical incident. Contacts and
recommendations gained via having foreign ownership is the lowest ranked factor
among the critical incidents. Appendix N, Tables 2 & 2.1 contains the synthesis of the
critical incidents identified in the study.
6.4.2.2

Summary of Factors which Influenced the Capacity of the Firms to Supply
their Export Orders

First of all it must be pointed out that the detailed analysis and synthesis of these factors
is also presented in Appendix N, Tables 3- 6. Again not all the factors hypothesised in
Section 6.1 facilitated the capacity of firms to deliver their export orders, thereby only
partially supporting the second hypothesis (Section 6.1). The influential factors are
categorised according to the various theoretical frameworks used in the thesis, and are
summarised as follows. First, all three RBV factors specified (available financial
capital, large number of full-time workers – firm size, availability of a stock of
machinery and equipment) were found to significantly influence the capacity of the
firms to meet their orders, in addition to three further factors which were: available
human capital resources of the foreign partners (foreign ownership), having trained
production workers, and having a human resources incentive policy for workers.
206

Among the RBV factors, having a stock of machinery and equipment and the stock of
the financial capital were ranked equally as the overriding facilitating factors, while the
possession of skilled production workers followed.
Second, three factors (i.e. having export vision at the commencement of the business
and having international risk taking behaviour and having international orientation)
from the four international entrepreneurship theory factors hypothesised in Section 6.1
were found to influence the capacity of the firm to supply their export orders. Two
additional factors, namely: (1) tying the long-term business growth to export market
entry, (2) having a positive perception of the export market emerged in the interviews as
equally facilitating the firms’ capacity towards their export delivery. Of these factors,
the owner’s positive perception of the export market and his or her export vision at the
commencement of the business were both rated the most influential factors, while
international risk taking behaviour came next.
Third, only one stage theory factor (the influence of firm size) was supported among the
three factors hypothesised above. An additional factor which emerged as equally
significant in terms of the influence of stage theory factors related to initial domestic
market experience. Of these two factors, the influence of initial domestic operating
experience was rated the highest. Finally, all the four network theory factors (i.e. (1)
networking with industrial associations, (2) networking with fellow business people, (3)
networking with family and friends abroad) hypothesised (Section 6.1) were supported.
Two additional network factors that emerged as equally significant were: (1) a large
amount of networking ties with more than one industrial association and (2) maintaining
frequent interaction and/or contacts with industrial associations. Of these factors, having
network ties with an industrial association was rated as the leading influence, followed
207

by the extent of network ties with different industrial associations and the frequency of
interactions a firm maintains with industrial associations. However, the impact on the
sector from the contingency theory, also hypothesised at the beginning of this chapter,
did not receive support.

6.5

DICUSSION OF FINDINGS

The next sub-section sets out the context for a discussion of the results in relation to
research question two of the thesis and the literature. The section also comprises two
parts: the first section focuses on the critical incidents that triggered export initiation,
while the second section focuses on the factors which influenced the capacity of the
firms to meet their export orders.
6.5.1

Discussion of Critical Incidents which Trigger Export Initiation

As already mentioned, contacts established via participation in a trade fair event
represents the top event among the incidents that cause the firms to begin export
business, and all the exporting firms passionately attested to this (Figure 6.1). From
Figure 6.1, trade fair events are classified under the proactive external behaviour of the
firm since this constitutes an external stimulus, as in this case the firm adopted a
proactive initiative and maximised the event. The trade fair events, from the perspective
of the respondents, represented grand occasions that occasionally take place in Ghana
(although not at regular intervals according to the firms) or abroad (most frequently).
According to the case studies, the event brings a variety of buyers and investors (both

208

local and foreign) together at one point. According to the case firms, while instant credit
and cash sales are made during the fairs, most arrangements concerning subsequent
export orders are made through the contacts with foreign buyers during the fair. For
instance, one owner-manageress noted:
My entry into the Netherlands’ market took place during a trade fair in the UK.
You know that country is closer to the UK, OK [okay]. This buyer approached
me, examined the samples and said she liked the design and the price. She
followed up after the fair with the first order and since then she been reordering and so I am always motivated to seek new markets during such events,
though the investment is huge, it works [pays off].
(Case 8)
Although this evidence stresses the importance of the role of trade shows as a
significant occurrence of export initiation, it is evident from the above quote that other
moderating factors interact to trigger the main incident. For example, part of the quote
above relates to investment in trade shows, and the willingness to commit resources to
such events. What is implied here is that a firm must vigorously explore trade fair
information in terms of its nature, time and venue, and must be prepared to commit
resources to it. The case studies revealed that as well as obtaining information about the
fair, a firm must register and purchase a stand (e.g. a place to showcase one’s products
and/or samples during the fair).
In addition, the case companies indicated that there is a need for a firm to arrange for
visa acquisition (especially if the fair is taking place abroad) and it must also be
prepared to pay the cost of board and lodging at the destination of the trade fair. As a
result, although trade shows appear to be the most important event for export initiation
among the firms, as has been demonstrated by the case firms, the management’s (the

209

entrepreneur’s) attitude, desire, willingness and capacity to appreciate the moderating
factors (e.g. the time and the investment) are the crucial antecedents to its success.
This finding confirms what McAuley (1999) found in Scotland and Nigeria (Ibeh &
Young, 2001; Ibeh, 2004). Here, the assumptions of the contingency theory with regard
to export initiation by the SME are supported (Lawrence & Lorsch, 1967; Kuada and
Sorensen, 2000; Ibeh, 2003b; Crick & Spence, 2005; Li et al., 2004). The contingency
theory argues in this respect that a firm’s ability to adapt to events in the external
environment (e.g. responding positively to trade shows) contributes to its export
success. In addition, as the incidents that triggered export initiation at the trade fairs
were connected to the contacts established at the fairs, it can be argued that the network
theory interacts with the contingency theory to produce this behaviour of the firm.
Consequently, the finding also supports the assumptions of the network theory
(Johanson & Mattson, 1988; Gorman & Evers, 2008). In this light, the theoretical
integration assumption that informs the thesis is supported.
A moderating factor relating to the capacity of the trade fair event to trigger export
initiation is a firm’s membership with industrial associations, as became widely evident
in the individual interview transcripts. According to the case companies, the activities of
an industrial association offer considerable trade fair opportunities because the
associations act as trade promotion organisations for their members. Among the various
industrial associations operating in Ghana, those cited by the case studies included: the
Ghana Chamber of Commerce and Industry, the Ghana Association of Women
Entrepreneurs, the Association of Ghana Industries, the Federation of Ghanaian
Exporters, the Spinnet Textiles & Garments Cluster, the Ghana Tailors and Dress
Makers Association, and the Ghana Association of Fashion Designers. Almost every
210

firm related its opportunity to participate in trade show to its membership (i.e. the
assistance it received) of one industrial association or another.
According to the experiences of the case study firms, because access to the internet at
business premises is not a common phenomenon among SMEs in Ghana, coupled with
the fact that unlike in advanced countries, postal services and telecommunications are
not well developed, the industrial associations serve as a major source of information on
export business development in Ghana. The benefits offered by the industrial
associations, as cited by the case firms, include detailed information about a trade fair
event (e.g. the time, venue and criteria for participation). One entrepreneur declared:
If you do not belong to an association, you cannot do anything. You see because
we are from a poor country, if you decide to go alone, it will take you days
[long] before you arrive [the export market] and you cannot bear the cost. Let
me ask you, can one firm or two organise trade fairs here in Ghana? [This
indicates that while the associations facilitate members’ access to fairs abroad,
they do extremely well by collaborating to host some of the fairs in Ghana,
occasionally]. Even our visa acquisition from the various embassies before you
attend the fairs overseas, I tell you if you apply the visa as an individual firm,
you will not get it [less likely to get], but if the association presents us as a
group, it is easy, even in seeking a stand [venue to showcase one goods at trade
fairs] abroad.
(Case 21)

While this quote supports the important role of industry associations with regard to
export initiation, it also implies that access to an international trade fair stand and/or
venue is easier if the firms are represented as members of an association abroad. It was
indicated in one interview that whenever a firm encountered a problem abroad, either
with customs or immigration abroad, while attending a trade show, it was easier to
obtain assistance from either Ghana’s mission abroad or the host country’s security
211

agencies when they were represented as a group, rather than when presenting
themselves as individual businessman or business woman from Ghana (Case 32).
Contacts established as a result of having international orientation represent the next
highest ranked incident (Figure 6.1). In terms of the interviews, foreign orientation
implies contacts established as a result of an owner-manager’s previous residency and
work abroad. When the individuals who have lived and worked abroad later leave the
overseas country and establish a business in their home country, they are able to contact
their extant acquaintances, which can lead to export initiation. According to the
interviews, the firms in the export destination countries feel secure in transacting
business with firms whose owners demonstrate international orientation, compared to
owner-managers that have not benefited from such prior opportunities. The case studies
with international orientation advantage indicated that such an advantage also alleviates
the normal uncertainties associated with export business, which similar owner-managers
who never had any international exposure may experience.
For example, exposure gained from previous work and residency in the US propelled
the setting up of one particular business in Ghana when AGOA was enacted (Case study
2). According to the owner-manageress, she used her foreign contacts to generate export
business when she commenced her business activities in Ghana.
She further stated that she received export orders through from contacts abroad, after
she had sent samples of her products to these acquaintances. According to her,
exporting to the US market did not present a problem because of her advantage via
foreign orientation. It must be noted that while the above forms of international
orientation represented a good platform for export business opportunity, the owner-

212

manageress stressed that they needed to be managed through frequent communication
and establishment of trust (Case study 2). Another owner-manageress commented:
Yes, although I have said most of my orders come from Belgium because of my
previous residency in that country initially, I must tell you that at least every two
years I visit Belgium to strengthen the ties I have with the shops with whom I do
[transact] business.
(Case 4).

This last quote also stresses the importance of efforts on the part of the export firms to
exploit export business opportunities through international orientation, because in this
case the advantaged firms have acted in a proactive manner, even though international
orientation can be considered an external stimulus. This finding confirms those from
Canada (Reuber & Fisher, 1997), Nigeria (Ibeh, 2004) and Slovenia (Ruzzier et al.,
2007) with regard to the link between propensity to initiate export business and
international

orientation

advantage.

Theoretically,

therefore,

international

entrepreneurship literature is supported because capitalising on prior international
orientation to trigger export business represents a clear entrepreneurial action
(McDougall et al., 1994; Ibeh, 2004). In addition, the resource-based view framework –
which considers the behaviour of the SME owner-manager in understanding its export
behaviour (unlike the stage theory that focuses only the firm) – is also supported
(Barney, 1991; Westhead et al., 2001). For instance, the fact that the interviewees
themselves in a way initiate the move to maximise their prior international orientation
advantage, when they felt confident, was real evidence of a combination of RBV and
international entrepreneurship theory factors. In addition, with regard to this finding, the
influence of network theory is also demonstrated here through the triggering of export

213

initiation as a result of the contacts gained during the owner-manager’s previous
residency abroad.
Contacts established and recommendations gained via having family and friends abroad
represent the next cited incident; according to the interviewees, the impact of the
network relationship with family members and friends abroad on export initiation takes
two forms. First, some of the businessmen and women in Ghana have family and
friends who reside abroad (e.g. the USA, UK, Holland, Belgium) and who introduce
them to firms (foreign businessmen and women), churches and shops when they pay
them informal visits abroad, which later leads to the receipt of export orders. These
export orders come about as a result of contacts they make while on a visit aboard. The
case studies indicated that before they embark on such international visits, they always
have the idea of promoting their businesses whilst abroad. As a result, they go on such
visits with samples of their finished products, often in several large suitcases. For
instance, one owner-manageress commented:
The visits play a big role! I visited my brother-in-law in Atlanta in 1998 and I
made lots of sales and since that time I still receive orders from shops because
of that visit. In my case I made my brother-in-law and my sister aware that I was
coming to do business in addition to the visit. So on the Sunday of the first week
of my arrival they took me to a large church consisting of different nationalities
and by the pastor’s permission, they introduced me to the church about what I
do and I showed some samples of the clothes and, my brother that was the
beginning of greater things. So now most of us are using that strategy as well,
because if you use one strategy your business will not move [grow].
(Case study 32)

214

The second form of networking with family and friends abroad was where the friends
and family members abroad recommended the Ghanaian businesses to overseas
businesses and individuals. This occurs even when the Ghanaian exporter does not go
abroad himself or herself, but sends samples to businesses abroad through existing
acquaintances (family and friends), and in this way the export order takes place (see
case studies 13, 18, 32, etc., Appendix N). This finding on export initiation, involving
family and friends abroad, also confirms what has been found in: New Zealand
(Coviello & Munro, 1995; Chetty & Holm, 2000), Nigeria (Ibeh & Young, 2001),
Tanzania (Rutashobya & Jaensson, 2004) and Ireland (Gorman & Evers, 2008).
Therefore, the basic assumption of network theory applied in this thesis is supported
(Johanson & Mattsson, 1988; Gorman & Evers, 2008).
Another major incident indicated by some interviewees (e.g. 4, 5, and 9) was that of
winning a government award. This confirms or augments the export-led promotion
programmes being implemented in Ghana by the government. According to the case
studies, the government in collaboration with some industrial associations (e.g. the
Association of Ghana Industries, the Ghana Chamber of Commerce, the Association of
Women Entrepreneurs), coupled with the assistance of the Ghana Export Promotion
Council, purposively samples particular firms in each sub-sector and evaluates them
based on particular criteria (e.g. the firm’s product quality, employment size, the
number of years of operation). According to the case studies, firms which are selected
or emerged as winners are given an award (e.g. medal,) as a symbol of good
performance. Such firms will then receive free and full sponsorship to travel abroad and
represent Ghana by showcasing their businesses at top business shows.

215

From the interviews, following the opportunity of showcasing Ghana abroad, the
beneficiary firms then make sales abroad while showcasing their business on behalf of
Ghana, and receive export orders after these trips. The firms stated that the programme
is conducted periodically, except in particular cases where a change of government
delays it. They also indicated that they receive privileges (e.g. direct assistance from the
Ghana mission abroad) while showcasing abroad as they are representing by the
government of Ghana. Although firms winning the government awards are supported by
representing the government of Ghana abroad, they indicated that all the benefits go to
their business directly. Case studies 4, 5, 9, 20, 21 were among these firms. The
evidence below is taken from one of the firms.
But it is hard work, you see I told you even the premises I am using are rented
from the government because I have been promoting the country. Look on the
shelves, I received all these three gold and wooden awards [medals] from the
government in different years and so if they directed you here it is hard work
paa [a Ghanaian word which means – indeed or truly/greatly] but the direct
benefit is that is what has made me a big exporter, because [although] I go to
represent the country, the chunk of orders I have been doing [receiving] come
from that opportunity.
(Case 4)
This part of the interview transcripts implies that a firm’s own readiness to activate
export business opportunities is a critical export success factor. Considering this event,
in this instance, the product, the right size of a firm, relevant domestic market operating
experience, management desire and commitments were in place. Theoretically, the
contingency theory’s assumption (e.g. the role of government) coupled with
entrepreneurial proactive behaviour, act together to trigger the export initiation.
The receipt of an unsolicited order is another event which sparks export initiation.
According to the case studies, an advertisement led to one unsolicited order. Two of the
216

firms among the case studies (i.e. 3 & 13) indicated that they had developed a simple
book profiling their businesses (e.g. case study 13’s version is called African fashion
clothes). According to these firms, the books were deposited at selected international
hotels in Accra, Ghana (Crystal Royal Hotel, LaPalm Beach, Golden Tulip, Accra &
Novotel) while others were also sent abroad through their family and friends. From the
accounts of the case firms, this creative step (advertisement) resulted in unsolicited
orders from the UK, Spain, Italy and Canada. Of the four case firms which specified
unsolicited orders, the other two firms (Case studies 5 & 32) attributed the incident to
their firms’ websites. In the case of Ghana, an unsolicited order does not represent the
most important trigger of export initiation (being fifth in the ranking, see Figure 6.1).
On one hand, this finding runs counter to earlier studies which reported that unsolicited
orders signify the most important incidents in triggering the decision to initiate export
business among SMEs (Tesar & Tarleton, 1982; Ghauri & Kumar, 1989, cited in
Williams, 2008). Nonetheless, the finding (receipt of unsolicited orders) in this study
confirms others from Cyprus (Leonidou, 1995b), Scotland (McAuley, 1999), the UK
(Crick & Spence, 2005) and Jamaica (Williams, 2008). Theoretically, the assumptions
of contingency theory are supported.
The next and least critical incident that emerged was contacts and recommendations
gained due to having a foreign ownership business structure. According to case study
11, most of the export orders have come through the influence of its foreign partners
abroad. The owner-manager commented:

217

On that question, I can say my foreign partners did everything; they look for the
export order using their connections out there. Even all the machines you saw
there were brought from China, which was facilitated by the Chinese partner. As
a result, our capital finance is stable compared with the other people [other
firms] those are being and interviewed; our only problem is the bottlenecks in
the system which the new government promises to ease.
(Case 11).
The quote above implies that having foreign ownership (foreign partner(s)) not only
triggers export initiation, but also brings about the flow of resources into the host firm
(e.g. ‘…. Even, all the machines you saw there were brought from China, which was
facilitated by the Chinese partner. As a result our capital finance is stable….’). From
this case firm, it can be seen that two partners abroad (especially the one in the USA)
introduced the firm to potential buyers abroad. This firm is involved in mass production,
including school uniforms. However, only one firm (Case study 11) enthusiastically
described foreign ownership as catapulting it into export initiation.
As the theme was further explored in the interview process with other case firms, most
of them (i.e. 1, 6, 10, 15, 19, 25 etc.) indicated that they would have preferred a
partnership with a foreign business in order to grow their businesses through export
market penetration but they did not have this possibility. This implies that opportunities
for the indigenous firms in the garment and textile sector in Ghana to form partnerships
with foreign businesses in order to activate export business are scarce. The lack of
potential foreign partners to align with Ghanaian businesses confirms the finding of
Asiedu (2002). In that study, the author found that while from 1990 into the 2000s the
total inflow of foreign direct investment (FDI) into developing countries increased, the
sub-Saharan African share became marginally lower.

218

The finding also supports findings from Slovenia (Ruzzier et al., 2007), challenges
others from Ireland (Ruane & Sutherland, 2005), while confirming findings of similar
studies in China (Zhang & Song, 2000); the UK (Greenaway et al., 2004) and Italy
(Carrato & Piva, 2007). Theoretically, the role of foreign ownership and the export
propensity of the host country’s firm confirm the assumptions of network theory
(Johnson & Mattson, 1988).
6.5.2

Discussion of Factors that enable the Firms to meet their Export Orders

The specific factors from the proposed integrated theoretical framework that influenced
the capacity of the case studies to meet their export orders are discussed below.
6.5.2.1 Stage Theory Factors
Although the stage model has been criticised (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994), it continues
to receive empirical support from researchers (e.g. see Williams, 2008; Hall & Cook,
2009). Among the stage theory variables selected in (Section 6.1.), only the firm’s size
and prior domestic market experience were accorded significance in the interviews with
regard to their influence on the firm’s ability to meet its export orders. In terms of size
(the total number of full-time employees), the case studies pointed out that garment and
textile manufacturing involves mass production with different production lines involved
in the manufacturing process. For instance, the case studies (e.g. cases 2, 4, 8, 14)
strongly emphasised that for a firm to meet one job order (supply one export order) by a
stipulated time, many factory hands are needed simultaneously, especially because
labour intensive production methods are used among the firms in Ghana. As a result, it
has become evident that to supply the international market, large numbers of workers

219

are required. All the firms concurred that their reliable and sizeable workforces were a
critical success factor in supplying export orders. For example, one interviewee noted:
Yes that one [number of workers] is very important for our business here. You
know we do not have the required up-to-date machines which our counterparts
in countries like Sri Lanka, China & Pakistan have, and so most of the work we
do here is labour intensive, and so if you do not have the right number of
workers, you cannot do export business from this country. You see when I took
you round even the workers you saw are only the full-time team, when I get
more orders I have temporal workers that I call.
(Case study 2)
This quote contains two major points. For example, in the literature, one of the
explanations used to affirm the positive relationship between the firm size and the
propensity to export is the fixed cost component associated with export business (Hall
& Tu, 2003; 2004; Mittelstaedt & Ward 2006). Another study (William, 2008) argues
that a firm’s size is a proxy for the overall productive capacity of the firm. This
argument too is plausible because different workers will put different skills at the
disposal of the firm. However, further to this, the results in this portion of the thesis
imply that mass production, coupled with labour intensive export manufacturing,
confirms the positive relationship between the size of the firm and its propensity to
export. This finding confirms what has been found in the UK (Hall & Tu, 2004) and the
USA (Brush et al., 2002). Theoretically, therefore, the basic assumptions of the
resource-based view and stage theory have been confirmed with regard to firm size.
Prior domestic market experience became a dominant influence on the ability of firms
to meet their export orders – 49% (Figure 13 below) of the firms in the case studies
strongly affirmed that their long-standing domestic market experience enabled them to
understand how the Ghanaian business environment works. As a result, they have a

220

competitive edge (e.g. familiarity and credibility with government agencies, the
Customs Exercise and the Preventive Service (CEPS), and the port and regulatory
institutions) over firms that do not have these prior domestic industry experiences and
connections.
According to these case firms (e.g. case studies 4, 5, 6, 15, 35, 36), whenever they have
an export order to deliver and they encounter any problems they find solutions more
easily compared to competitors who do not have such domestic market experience.
Some of them strongly argued that they receive financial assistance to work on their
export orders from financial institutions, mainly because of their long practice in the
domestic market. The benefit of having prior domestic market experience concurs with
the assumptions of stage theory. In contrast, no significant influence was accorded to
psychic distance, the firm’s age or the age of the owner of the firm.
For instance, it was understood from the interview transcripts that the choice of export
market by the case study respondents was driven by a search for ready buyers (existence
of markets, mostly based on network contacts) as opposed to similarities between the
export market (the importing country) and the exporting country (i.e. psychic distance).
The relative importance and the difference between these two variables were content
analysed as shown in the table below.

221

Figure 6.2: Stage Theory Factors in Ranked Order

From Figure 6.2, it can be seen that influence of firm size takes precedence over prior
domestic market experience with regard to the export development of the case firms.
The next sub-section discusses the key RBV factors that according to the interview
transcripts influenced delivery of export orders.
6.5.2.2

Resource-Based View Factors

The RBV factors that emerged as facilitating the capacity of the firms to meet their
export orders include: having a large number of workers, foreign ownership, having
adequate factory buildings, having a stock of equipment and machinery, having
adequate financial capital, having skilled production workers, and a human resources
incentive policy. Figure 6.3 depicts their relative importance and their differences.

222

Figure 6.3: Influential Resource-based view Variables in Ranked order

From Figure 6.3 it can be seen that the availability of financial capital, the stock of
equipment and machinery as well as a large workforce were ranked highest among the
RBV factors. Some of the case firms (e.g. case studies 1, 2, 4, 10) revealed that their
international buyers rarely pre-finance the export orders they place, but where they do
they pay only 25 percent of the total cost. Consequently, it was the responsibility of the
owner to top up, and hence finance was of key importance in getting export orders
completed and delivered.
In addition, availability and good condition of machinery and equipment (e.g. sewing,
embroidery, knitting and dyeing machines) were indicated as other essential
requirements for delivery of export orders on time. Availability of a large number of
full-time workers was accorded much importance when the interview turned to RBV
factors; however, the influence of firm size on the case study firms’ ability to meet their
export orders is similar to that discussed under the stage theory factors (Figure 6.2). The
223

positive influence of firm size on the capacity to deliver export orders confirms findings
from the USA (Bloodgood et al., 1996; Brush et al., 2002), but contradicts what Svente
et al.(2004) found in Sweden. Although firm size is one of the strongest arguments in
stage theory, at the same time it is plausible to argue that it is also a resource-based
view variable since it captures the internal motivation ability of the firm. Theoretically,
therefore, the assumption of the RBV and the stage theory is confirmed.
Having skilled production workers is the fourth highest ranking category. According to
the firms, these workers not only carry out repetitive tasks, but they are knowledgeable
about the entire production system and therefore they act as supervisors and team
leaders in the production departments. Some of the case study respondents (e.g. firms 2,
14, 21, 34) indicated that a proportion of their production workers are expatriates (e.g.
from Sri Lanka, Pakistan & Mauritius), while others (e.g. those in the case studies, 1,
11, 13), indicated that they have trained some of their indigenous employees to master
the production process and they have therefore acquired a skilled production workforce.
The third ranking RBV critical influence is the availability and adequacy of factory
buildings (factory space and/or premises). Some firms in the case studies (e.g. 8, 11, 24)
stressed that availability of spacious factory buildings plays an important role in
enabling them to meet export orders. According to them, assets such as a separate
production area, a packaging section and a separate warehouse enabled them to deliver
orders and to work on many orders simultaneously. An owner-manageress noted:

224

My factory buildings [factory space] are a major boost to me so that I do more
orders. I think as you have been going round you saw it. Some people are using
part of their homes as their plant site [factory and/or work place]. They are
doing well, but I think they are restricted as to how many orders they can accept
and how they can deliver. So that is why those with real factory facilities are
ahead.
(Case 24)
The importance of having factory facilities is reinforced from the quote above, with
regard to the capacity of the firm to meet its export order. One pattern indicated in the
interview transcripts was that almost all those who stressed the importance of factory
facilities were medium-sized firms, which further emphasises the importance of firm
size and its influence on the capacity to export. Another RBV factor relates to human
resource incentive policies for workers. Some selected case study companies (e.g. 3, 4,
10, 11) have policies which take care of their workers when they encounter life crises.
Examples of these are (1) when an employee wants to relocate their spouse from a
village to a city; (2) ill-health and (3) bereavement among the workers.
According to these case firms, such incentives encourage employees to show extra
commitment when working on orders because some of the incentives are directly linked
with working on export orders which they consider important. The worker incentives
motivate workers to work on export orders, which support findings in Italy (Toni &
Nassimbeni, 2001). Furthermore, foreign ownership also emerged as a significant RBV
influence. One firm (case study 11) highlighted the role of its foreign partners (foreignownership) in enhancing the firm’s ability to meet its export orders. According to case
study 11, human capital, financial support and the international network resources of its
foreign partners served as a buffer for their firm whenever they faced a crisis in

225

delivering an export order. Here the assumption of the network theory is also supported
because its moderates the RBV.
6.5.2.3

International Entrepreneurship Factors

The international entrepreneurship factors which emerged as a critical influence in
facilitating the case companies’ capacity to meet their export orders include the
following: (1) tying the long-term business growth of the firm to export market entry;
(2) having export business vision at the commencement of the business; (3) having
international orientation; (4) having international risk taking behaviour, and (5) having a
positive perception of the export market. The relative importance and the differences
among these factors are also content analysed and presented in the figure below.
Figure 6.4: Influential International Entrepreneurship Factors in Ranked Order
