Among the five themes in Figure 6.4, having an export vision at the commencement of
one’s business, having a positive perception of the export market and possessing

226

international risk-taking behaviour shared the highest ranking. With regard to the export
vision at the commencement of the business, every case study respondent specified that
from the outset (from the inception of their business) the vision to enter the international
market was one of their goals and so, according to their accounts, their strong
internationalisation vision drives them whenever they receive export orders. However,
none of the firms had a formal strategic plan where this vision was expressly written
down, although it was possible to understand intuitively that internationalisation vision
was a characteristic of the firms from inception. From the accounts of the interviewees
the goal and/or the export vision implied a long-term business growth target envisaged
from the start of their respective businesses. One owner-manageress indicated that from
her school days (in the polytechnic) when she was sewing for friends, the customers
confirmed that she was good at her work and she conceived the idea of setting up her
business and taking it abroad from that moment. She stated: “I am in the international
market, isn’t that wonderful” (28). This supports findings in the USA (McDougall et al.,
1994; Oviatt & McDougall, 1995; McAuley, 1999; Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki, 2003;
Ruzzier et al., 2006).
All the firms in the case studies complained about the risks they face concerning their
export business transactions. They indicated that because of the negative perceptions
about Africa, most international buyers are reluctant to pre-finance part of the cost of
the export order (as already stated). They therefore identified this problem as one of
their challenges because it pressures them to seek external funds in order to meet the
order. An owner-manageress complained and bemoaned:

227

To do this export business here [Ghana] you must possess a high capacity to
take and absorb risk. You need funds to work on the orders and pay your
workers every month and so it is not easy. There are too many challenges; that
is why some have shut down. There was a time that my order was returned from
the US, because it got delayed. I hope case study 6 [actual name withheld], the
lady you said you just interviewed this morning before coming here, has told
you, because she has faced the same challenge last month [June, 2009], but she
re-organised herself immediately, because it is common with our business. My
brother, there are too many bottlenecks in the country; you see this small port
[harbour] before your goods can be sent abroad eh; [in fact] it is a headache
[difficult task]. But because I want to prove to my customers out there that I am
well able, I go all out to satisfy them. My husband has been telling me to shut the
business and come back to the States, but I think this is my country and therefore
I need to do something.
(Case 2)
The evidence above implies that the risks associated with the Ghanaian export business
are multifarious. These range from a lack of funds to pay staff wages, a lack of funds to
pre-finance orders and, generally, bottlenecks in the Ghanaian business environment.
What can be implied here is that an entrepreneur must possess risk taking behaviour
when doing export business in Ghana and s/he must be prepared to show a higher
amount of risk-taking behaviour beyond the borders of Ghana. Consequently, their
willingness and capacity to take risks in the international market influences them
positively, regardless of the challenges which go with meeting that order (e.g. … “but I
think this is my country and therefore I need to do something” (case company 2) – from
the preceding quote). The findings concerning the attitudes towards international risktaking support what was found in China (Zheng et al., 2009) and Vietnam (Thai &
Chang, 2008). In addition, this confirms the findings of Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki
(2003) concerning the antecedents of international entrepreneurship behaviour as
reviewed in Chapter 2.

228

In terms of positive perceptions of the international market, every firm in the case
studies demonstrated from their conversation an inordinate belief that they would be
able to expand and achieve success in the international market compared to the
domestic market. Some firms (e.g. case studies 3, 6, 7, 10, 22, 23) also mentioned
indicated financial gain, business growth and survival in the export market. Others (e.g.
case studies 1, 22, 26, 28, 29) described benefits such as goal achievement, a feeling of
well being and a platform for social networking. For example, one owner-manageress
commented:
the fact is if you want to make it big, you must join the associations
[industry associations) and export. Once you are prepared to accept the
challenges, you will make it. You see, through this business I have built
my house, my two daughters are schooling in Canada, all from this
business. Now my husband is sick because he is old, but I am the one
taking care of him. And it is nice, my brother, as well as the money, you
meet lots of people and it is good name too for you and your business.
(Case study 22)

It is interesting to note that businesses have multiple goals when entering the
international market, which are different to the profit motive (as can be seen in the
above quotation). Another example of this is the owner-manageress of a firm in a case
study who simply takes delight in meeting people through international business.
Tying the long-term business growth goal of the firm to export market entry (fourth
highest ranked factor), drives the firms to do everything in order to meet their export
orders. Most of the companies in the case studies believed that the only way they could
achieve long-term business growth was by entering the international market. Factors
which they considered as indicative of ‘long-term business growth’ included: (1) having

229

a large amount of money in a firm’s bank account, (2) having many workers,
departments, ample equipment and factory space and (3) having many branches. The
majority of them (e.g. 1, 3, 6, 20, 26, 29, 30, 31, 32, 33, 34) held strongly that Ghana’s
domestic market was too small, hence their endeavours to realise their long-term
business growth through entry into the international market. They also maintained that
this strong belief helped them invest all their time, money and energy whenever they
received an order; by putting so much time and effort in, they aimed to keep their
customers and to receive repeat business and referrals.
The lowest ranked factor among international entrepreneurship behavioural factors is
the entrepreneur’s international orientation. While this factor forms part of the triggers
for export initiation (as already discussed under the triggers of export initiation/Figure
6.1), according to some of the firms it also served as a crucial consideration in meeting
their export orders. Two entrepreneurs (Cases 2 & 18) indicated that their previous
experience of living and working in the US and Belgium had impacted on their export
business because they had no problems with their buyers when they sent their export
orders. These case study firms indicated that they knew how western business culture
works before coming to settle in Ghana. Product quality, the speed of delivery, trust and
repeated communication were part of the western business way of life and they claimed
that they knew about this and that it facilitated their dealings with buyers abroad. These
key qualities developed and built through one’s international orientation and their
impacts on export business support previous findings (McDougall et al., 1994) in the
USA (Oviatt & McDougall, 1995), Scotland (McAuley, 1999), Vietnam (Thai & Chang
(2008) and the UK (Hutchinson et al., 2006).

230

6.5.2.4

Network Theory Factors

The main network theory factors which emerged include: (1) having network relations
with industrial associations, (2) network relations with other business people, (3) the
extent of a firm’s network relations with industrial associations, (4) the frequency of a
firm’s networking interaction with industrial associations and (5) networks with family
and friends. Figure 6.5 below shows the relative differences and ranking among these
themes.

From Figure 6.5, among the network factors, networking with industrial associations
ranked the highest because every firm attested to it. In terms of how this facilitates the
firms’ capacity to meet their export orders, the following is a sample of the comments
cited by the case study firms:

231

•

Access to funds: the firms in the case studies indicated they were able to source
funds to work on their export orders through their membership of industry
associations (e.g. some mentioned the World Bank’s assistance through the
Business Development Fund established in Ghana in collaboration with the
government of Ghana) and from other banking and non-banking financial
institutions. According to these case firms, an individual firm may find it
difficult to access such sources of funds unless they apply through the various
industrial associations in Ghana;

•

Capacity building: most of the case studies (e.g. 2, 4, 6, 7, 29, 34) strongly
emphasised various capacity building programmes (e.g. financial planning skills,
marketing skills and business communication skills) which they receive
periodically through their membership of industrial associations. They strongly
acknowledged that the skills they receive from such training enhance their
business processes and therefore they are well equipped to meet the expectations
of international buyers.

The impact of network relations with industrial associations and the capacity to export
confirms what has been found in Scotland (McAuley, 1999) and in Tanzania
(Rutashobya & Jaensson, 2004). This finding supports Gorman & Evers’ (2008)
network structure.
The next consideration was having membership of more than one industrial association.
This factor was ranked the second highest among the network factors. As already
mentioned under this discussion section, the case studies indicated that in some cases
the activities of industrial associations overlap, while in other cases the proportion of
232

benefit they offer, the time and contents differ. As a result, some firms (e.g. 10, 14, 16)
stressed that certain particular industrial associations are more resourced and have more
connections with international and local bodies (e.g. government ministries and foreign
commissions in Ghana) than others. Therefore, it is advantageous for firms to work with
more than one industrial association. The owner-manager of case study 10 pointed out
that it was through his second registration with another industrial association (i.e. Ghana
Chamber of Commerce) that he received training in commercial law. Implicitly, the
amount of support the case firms receive from industrial associations varies in content
and degree and so the more the memberships it has of industrial associations, the greater
the capacity of the firm to meet its export orders.
The frequency of interactions with one or more industrial associations is another critical
influence (i.e. third ranked influence). According to some of the case studies (e.g. firms
19, 20, 23, 24), each industrial association has periodic meeting days and others days
for other activities. Some of the case study respondents recounted that those who
attended meetings and participated in all the activities of one association or another
received greater benefits than their counterparts who, although members, did not
demonstrate much commitment towards the associations’ activities. They indicated that
although the benefits are the same as those discussed under the first point (membership
of industrial associations), this form of interaction (frequent interactions with the
industrial association (s)) is seen as offering more opportunities. One owner-manager
commented:

233

Whether you will do well or not also depends on you the person [entrepreneur].
Let me tell you, we have a lot of associations and, my brother, they help us a lot.
All that is required of you is commitment and you have to be faithful in paying
your dues. Me, I have registered with most of them. Unless I do not hear that
this association is here or there, massa [Ghana term which means – master) I
will go for it and once they know that you are serious, any opportunity that
comes you will be among the first that they will give priority to. You know most
of us do not have access to the internet at our job places and so, if you do not
attend meetings you will be left behind. The other day my friend said I am
selfish, but look he always stays home and does not attend meetings, but he
expects me to call him each time we go for meetings. How?
(Case 9)

The quotation indicates that while membership of associations builds a firm’s capacity,
individual businesses must be prepared to show commitment through repeated
interactions. The next critical factor was the networks with family and friends (the
fourth ranked influence). Some case study respondents indicated more than once that
they occasionally receive substantial financial support from friends and family, both in
Ghana and abroad, which they use to work on their orders. In some cases (e.g. cases 4,
11, 33, 34), such financial assistance was interest-free, in contrast to offerings from
financial institutions. The findings on benefits of network relations with family and
friends on the SME’s capacity to meet export orders confirm findings in the UK
(Boojihawon, 2007; Hutchinson et al., 2006), Ireland (Gorman & Evers, 2008) and
Tanzania (Rutashobya & Jaensson, 2004).
Networking with other business people is another influence (the lowest ranked). Some
of the people in the case studies (e.g. cases 20, 24, 28) indicated that their strong ties
with other exporters in the sub-sector (in Ghana) augmented their capacity. Some of the
interviewees stated they had received financial assistance from their fellow business

234

associates when they urgently needed money to work on their export orders (see case
studies 2, 4, 8, 20). In another incident, the interviewee in case study 2 indicated that
there was a time when she received more orders than she could cope with. She stated
that because of her good relationship with another firm (i.e. case study 4), she delivered
the orders on time without any problems (i.e. the two firms shared the orders and both
worked on them). These results confirm what has been found in the USA (Oviatt &
McDougall, 1995), the Netherlands (Gemser et al., 2004), New Zealand (Coviello &
Munro, 1995) and the UK (Hutchinson et al., 2006). This confirmation of network
theory factors in the study counteracts the weakness of the stage theory (Johanson &
Vahlne, 1977) and the RBV theory (Barney, 1991) identified in the literature review
with regard to ascertaining whether or not a firm’s capacity to export is the result of its
own efforts.
Although, most of the business owners were females, it was noted from the interviews
that they actively sought network opportunities for export business and seemed
successful at it. These women were proactive in their interaction with network contacts,
possessed a dense network of contacts, mixed with both men and women in their
networking, and accessed network information from both commercial people and
family. This result contradicts Ibarra (1997), who found that gender differences existed
with regard to business networking, but supports those researchers (Cromie & Birley,
1992; Aldrich et al. (1997) who found otherwise.

235

6.6

CONCLUSION

6.6.1

Triggers of Export Initiation (Critical Incidents)

With regard to the five theoretical frameworks integrated to explore the main critical
incidents of export initiations, it can be concluded that contingency theory became the
most important. The findings indicate that the main triggers of export initiation among
the garment and textile firms were largely based on external stimuli (Figure 6.1). Six
main themes emerged as the critical incidents, with contacts established at a trade fairs
the most important incident, followed by the contacts attained via international
orientation and contacts and recommendations gained via network relations with friends
and family abroad the third most important incident. These incidents support the
assumptions of the network theory (Johanson & Mattsson, 1988; McAuley, 1999;
Coviello & Martin, 1999; Gorman & Evers, 2008). Winning government awards was
the fourth ranked critical incident. However, the incidents based on receipt of
unsolicited orders and having foreign ownership were not significant. The finding on
the unsolicited order provides some support for the stage theory (Johanson & Valhne,
1997), but here the firms were not entirely passive players as stage theory suggests.
In most cases, the owners acted in a sufficiently entrepreneurial manner to identify and
maximise the incident which catapulted them into export initiation. With regard to most
of the critical incidents, the networking contacts established played the crucial role. For
example, contacts gained via trade shows, foreign orientation, having foreign ownership
and via friends and families abroad were the crucial incidents. In addition, the owner’s
international orientation also provoked export initiation. Consequently, the assumptions
of the international entrepreneurship theory are supported (MaDougall et al., 1994;

236

Oviatt & McDougall, 1995; McAuley, 1999; Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki, 2003;
Ruzzier et al., 2006). In addition, winning a government award and unsolicited orders
also triggered export initiation. Whereas most of the incidents seemed mainly
spontaneous, serendipitous, episodic, fortuitous

and accidental (e.g. receipts of

unsolicited orders), some case companies took full advantage of them and exploited
them. The act of maximising episodic events and being able to initiate export business
support both the international entrepreneurship and the contingency theories’
assumptions (Lawrence & Lorsch, 1967; McDougall et al, 1994; Kuada and Sorensen,
2000; Ibeh, 2003; Li et al., 2004; Crick & Spence, 2005).
The dominant impact of external stimuli on initiation of export activity among the
Ghanaian case companies contradicts some common findings on SMEs’ export
behaviour in developed countries. For example, according to the synthesis of Leonidou
et al. (2007) based on 32 empirical studies about export stimulating factors (between
1974 & 2005) in SMEs in the developed countries and parts of Asia, the internal
proactive factors appeared to be the leading sparks of export initiation among all the
SMEs. In addition, findings in Jamaica (Williams, 2008) based on 44 small exporting
firms and in the UK (Hutchinson et al., 2007) supported the findings of Leonidou et al.
(2007) on the influence of internal proactive factors in triggering export initiation
among SMEs. The dominant influence of internal factors in causing export initiation by
small firms in the developed world may be a reflection of these firms’ strong and rich
internal resource capacity, whereas SMEs in developing countries (e.g. Ghana) are
more constrained in this respect.

237

Therefore, it can be argued that the variation in terms of this finding (external proactive
stimuli export behaviour of Ghanaian SMEs versus the internal proactive stimuli/export
behaviour of developed countries’ SMEs) can be attributed to the differences in
resource stocks among the firms. This consequently supports the argument that the
resource-based view is one of the most useful frameworks in explaining the
internationalisation behaviour of SMEs (Barney, 1991; Westhead et al., 2001).
Although SMEs in general are universally known to be resource constrained, the
problem is most pronounced among African firms. Consequently, the finding that the
case studies in Ghana manifest proactive external export behaviour also confirms what
has been found in Nigeria (Ibeh & Young, 2001). Overall, the assumption of the
proposed integrated theoretical framework behind the thesis is supported in terms of the
findings on critical incidents that trigger export initiation. The findings described above
provide evidence that the influences of contingency theory, network theory,
international entrepreneurship and stage theory have all interacted to reproduce the
single behaviour of the firms (i.e. the export initiation).
6.6.2

Factors which enabled the Firms to Meet the Export Orders

The main factors from the proposed integrated framework which influenced the
capacity of the firms to meet their export orders are summarised as follows. Among the
variables the RBV factors which emerged included: (1) large number of factory hands,
(2) adequate equipment and machinery and (3) adequate financial capital were found to
be the most significant factors, (4) followed by a skilled production workforce. Having
foreign ownership and a human resource incentive package were the least significant
influences. Stage theory factors which emerged as significant included: (1) firm size and
(2) having adequate prior domestic market operating experience. Of the two factors,
238

firm size appeared to be the most significant. In addition, network theory factors that
emerged included: (1) networking with industrial associations, (2) networking with
other business people, (3) networking with family and friends abroad, (4) having many
networking ties with industrial associations, and (5) the frequency of a firm’s interaction
with its industrial associations. Of these factors, networking with industrial associations
was rated the most influential, followed by the amount of networking ties a firm
maintains with industrial associations and then the frequency of interaction with
industrial associations.
Networking relationships with friends and family and those with business associates
emerged as the least influential factors. Furthermore, international entrepreneurship
factors which were accorded significant influence were: (1) tying the long-term business
growth goal to export market entry, (2) having an international orientation, (3) having
export vision at the start of the business, (4) having an international risk taking
behaviour, and (5) having a positive perception of the export market. Among these
factors, the last three were rated as the greatest facilitators, whilst the first two were the
least significant. Considering in combination the critical incidents and the factors that
enabled the firms to meet their export orders, the following conclusions can be drawn.
Considering as a whole in the light of the variables from the proposed integrated
framework, first, contingency factors played an important role, implying the importance
of external factors and their influence on export propensity and export success of the
small firm. However, while the entrepreneurs of the firms proactively capitalised on
external stimuli (e.g. contacts made at trade fairs, government selection & unsolicited
orders), it can be argued that internally the firms had the capacity to meet export orders
in terms of their products, foreign ownership (human capital resources of the foreign
239

partner), factory buildings, stock of equipment and machinery, available financial
capital, skilled production workers, incentives for workers and prior domestic industry
experience.
This aspect of internal readiness supports the assumptions of RBV and stage theories
integrated in this thesis. It also implies that, while contingency factors are essential, the
internal capacity factors are crucial to the firms’ export success. For example to think
about taking advantage of a critical incidents and initiating export business a firms be
ready in terms of export product. However, it must be noted that though it is argued
here that in some sense the case companies were internally ready (e.g. as export
contingency factors came their way, they were able to maximise them), it can be
implied clearly from the interviews that the individual resource capacities of the
respective case companies were inadequate to meet the export orders, and as a result
they made use of the resource capacity of their network relations (e.g. networking with
the various industrial associations, networking with business associates, networking
with foreign partners). Therefore, internal readiness in the context of Ghanaian firms
must be understood in the context of their capacity to receive support from their
networking associates. This makes the assumption of network theory with regard to
export success of SMEs from Ghana crucial.
With regard to accessing resources from their various networking relations, it is worth
stating that the owners’ entrepreneurial qualities facilitated the process greatly. For
example, the case companies’ ability to link up with different types of network
associates (e.g. business associates, families and friends, industrial associations),
coupled with the frequency of their interaction with these associates, can be construed
as evidence of actual entrepreneurialism. For instance, a firm’s ability to identify its
240

liabilities (resource constraints) and to spot alternative suppliers of these resources (e.g.
from industrial associations, foreign partners) and seize such opportunities to achieve its
export objective is a real international entrepreneurial action.
Following on from the literature review, this study set out to explore whether or not a
single theoretical framework could fully account for the internationalisation behaviour
of the SME. From the evidence in this study, it can be concluded that no one single
theoretical framework can fully explain the phenomenon. Both the triggers of export
initiation and the factors which enabled the case companies to deliver the export orders
appear to be complex, dynamic and interactive as some manifestations of each of the
five theoretical frameworks are reflected in the behaviour of the firms. Further, on the
evidence of the interviews, the event can be argued as consisting of a mixture of rational
(planned) and unplanned (serendipitous) events. For instance, the evidence in this study
on chance incidents (unsolicited orders), capitalisation on previous residency in the
export destination country, making the most of network relationships established via
trade fairs to create subsequent export orders are indications unplanned and/or
entrepreneurialism coupled with having a ready export product (planned action) .
Together the findings confirm the assumptions behind applying an integrated theoretical
framework to address the phenomenon.
Specifically, the influence of the network and the contingency theories behaviour of the
case companies resolve and counteract the weaknesses of the RBV and stage theories,
as identified from the literature review. For example, the resource-based view and stage
theory imply that the decision of the firm about whether or not to export and develop its
export business depends mainly on its own efforts. In addition, stage theory excludes
the role of the entrepreneur owner-manager in explaining the export behaviour of the
241

SME. Furthermore, these two theoretical frameworks ignore the influence of
environmental factors (e.g. chance event and serendipity) on SME export behaviour. On
one hand, the findings here support researchers who argue that no one theoretical
framework can fully account for the events, and who therefore use combined theoretical
models. Samples of these studies can be found in New Zealand (Coviello & Munro,
1997; Coviello & Martin, 1999; Coviello & Cox, 2006), the UK (McAuley, 1999; Crick
& Spence, 2005; Hall & Cook, 2009) and Sweden (Jansson & Sandberg, 2008).
However, the findings deviate from those of researchers (e.g. Reuber & Fischer, 1997;
Westhead et al., 2001; Manolova et al., 2002; Brush et al., 2002; Ibeh, 2003; 2004; Hall
& Tu, 2004; Hutchinson et al., 2006) who apply a single theoretical framework and at
least partly exclude the consideration of other theoretical influences on the topic.
Finally, the intention of understanding and testing the predictive power of each of the
theories integrated into the theoretical framework of the thesis was not to establish the
best theory, but to understand how all four theoretical frameworks coupled with the
contingency theory would be significant in fully explaining and understanding the
export behaviour of small businesses in Ghana. The evidence in the interview
transcripts indicates that in the case of Ghana, network theory, international
entrepreneurship theory and contingency theory are more dominant with regard to
influencing the SMEs to respond positively to export stimuli compared to the RBV and
the stage theory.

As already indicated, the network theory is dominant with regard to the critical
incidents. This occurs through making use of contacts and recommendations gained via:

242

(1) international orientations, (2) trade fair participation, (3) via foreign ownership and
(4) friends and families abroad, which are all network factors. Also, taking advantage of
the owner’s international orientation (e.g. his/her previous residency, working abroad)
to trigger export initiation constitutes the interplay of networking influence (network
theory) and international entrepreneurial qualities. In addition, taking advantage of
contacts established at trade fairs to bring about subsequent export business amounts to
a mixture of network theory, contingency factors and international entrepreneurial
factors, whilst the ability to spot opportunities in the environment (e.g. during trade fair
events) and seizing them to initiate export business, can be considered as
entrepreneurial actions.
In addition, concerning the factors which influenced the capacity of the firms to meet
the export orders, the utilisation of networking relationships through industrial
associations played an important role as already discussed above. They also signify that
a combination of networking and entrepreneurial powers is in play. Consequently, the
original model as crafted from the literature review is revised to reflect the Ghanaian
experience as follows.
The revised integrated framework indicates that among the five theoretical frameworks,
network theory, international entrepreneurship theory and contingency theory are more
influential in explaining the export behaviour of the small business in Ghana. The
revised framework is presented in Figure 6.6 below.

243

Figure 6.6: Revised Integrated Theoretical Framework of SME Internationalisation based on Ghanaian SMEs’ Export Behaviour

244

Figure 6.6 implies that in deciding to enter the export market from Ghana, the small and
medium-sized firm already possess some of the necessary resources itself, as implied by
stage theory and the resource-based view theory (e.g. factory premises and export
products). These two sources of capacities are augmented by the business owner’s
personality factors (e.g. his drive, ambition – the international entrepreneurship theory
factors). However, the three composite resources (i.e. the RBV, stage theory and the
entrepreneurship factors of the owner-manager) are still inadequate. As a result, the
firm, through the owner-manager’s entrepreneurial qualities, accesses network relation
resources to augment its capacity. It is the unique entrepreneurial qualities of the ownermanager that maximise network relation resources into the focal firm. Hence, with
regard to the firm’s capacity, a double sided arrow connects (Figure 6.6) the network
relation resources through to the entrepreneur (the owner) and then to the firm (i.e.
making entrepreneurial qualities very crucial and central in accessing of networking
relation resources by the firm). Here, the implication is that the network relations’ stock
of resources, which flows into the focal firm, does so by means of the owner-manager’s
unique entrepreneurial qualities.
As a result, because the network resources flow from the environment into the firm,
there is an arrow from the environment through the network to the owner- entrepreneur
and the firm. Therefore, due to the fact that networking seems to occupy a critical role
and be very dominant with regard to the firm’s export success, the figure for the
network relations’ stock of resources is literally enlarged in the figure above. In
addition, because it is the owner-manager’s entrepreneurial behaviour that connects
with and brings the network relations’ resources into the firm, coupled with whether or
245

not the firms will maximise chance events or not (e.g. winning government awards,
unsolicited orders – contingency theory factors) that (the entrepreneurial role) too is
literally enlarged. Again, according to the interviews, the critical incidents were mainly
triggered by external stimuli and so the influences of the contingency factors are
deemed crucial in explaining the export behaviour of the SMEs in Ghana. So the figure
which represents the external factors’ impact on the firm’s export success is also
enlarged in the figure above.
Consequently, the figures for the network relations’ stock of resources and the owner’s
personality/entrepreneurial factors and the contingency factors are written larger than
those of the RBV and the stage theory factors in order to, and clearly show their
importance, as the crucial role they play in terms of obtaining an export order and being
able to supply it is extremely important in Ghana’s context. Finally, through the impact
of these three key frameworks, the firm’s export decision and/or success is shown to be
vibrant and positive as opposed to negative in remaining focused on the domestic
market. Finally, the export involvement figure is also drawn considerably larger than
the non-exporting figure.

6.7

CONCLUDING REMARKS

The chapter set out to explore selected variables (Section 6.1) from the proposed
integrated theoretical framework applied in the study (Chapter 2, Literature Review).
The thinking behind this was to explore their significance in influencing SMEs in
Ghana to initiate export business, as well as influence on the firms’ capacity to meet
246

their export orders. The findings are that, first of all, not all the factors which trigger the
export initiation of the SMEs simultaneously facilitate their capacity to meet their
export orders. Secondly, the findings indicate that the triggers of export initiation by
firms in Ghana are based on proactive external stimuli. This implies that for the
Ghanaian context it is important that SMEs align their strategy (planned or unplanned)
to the changing trends of the external environment.
Whilst the influence of the external environment appears important, the firm must
develop their internal capacity appropriately (e.g. readiness of export product and
financial capital to work on the export order). Overall, the interaction of the key factors
appears complex, dynamic and interactive and this supports the application of an
integrated framework, as opposed to a single theoretical framework. Evidence in the
interview transcripts indicates that of the five theoretical frameworks the influence of
the network theory, the international entrepreneurship theory, and the contingency
theory appear to be significant in the Ghanaian context. The average size of firms from
the garment and textile sub-sector in Ghana is small, which implies low internal
resource capacity to enter the export market by its own internal efforts.

247

CHAPTER SEVEN

UNDERSTANDING NON-EXPORTERS’ PERCEPTIONS
OF THE CRITICAL INCIDENTS WHICH TRIGGER
EXPORT INITIATION: EMPIRICAL RESEARCH PART 3

7.1

INTRODUCTION

Having identified the incidents which trigger export initiation and which influence the
capacity of the SMEs to meet their export orders in Chapter 6, this chapter addresses
research question 3 of the thesis and seeks to achieve objective 4 of the thesis. It
examines the perceptions and experiences of non-exporting SMEs in the same garment
and textile industry concerning the critical incidents identified in Chapter 6. In Chapter
6, six main incidents were found to be critical concerning the export initiation among
the firms. These six incidents are: (1) contacts established via trade fair participation,
(2) contacts established via having international orientation, (3) contacts and
recommendations established via having fiends and families abroad, (4) winning
government awards, (5) being in receipt of unsolicited orders and (6) contacts and
recommendation established via having foreign ownership. On the basis of these
findings, it is hypothesised in this chapter that non-exporting firms from the same sector
may have encountered these incidents in their operating lives, but due to one problem or
another they could not capitalise on them sufficiently to initiate export business.

248

The literature review offered classification of non-exporters into two groups, namely the
SMEs that have exported before, but have withdrawn from the export business, and
SMEs which have not exported before (e.g. Leonidou, 1995a; Williams, 2006).
However, the non-exporters interviewed in the current study belong to the second
group. For example, among the non-exporting studies reviewed in Chapter 2, the study
of Leonidou (1995c) in particular examines the perception of non-exporters as regards
the factors that trigger export initiation. The contribution this study makes to such a
body of research is that it not only seeks to explore the perceptions of the non-exporters
about the occurrences that activate export initiation, but it ascertains whether or not
some or all of the non-exporters have experienced those critical incidents during their
operating lives.
To address the research question behind this chapter, the rest of the chapter is subdivided as follows. The methodology for the study is discussed in the next section, and
this is followed by the presentation of the results of the data analysis; these being subdivided into: (1) a description of the case companies’ profiles; (2) the analysis of
general reasons for non-exporting among all the sampled firms and analysis the case
firms which experienced the critical incidents of the exporting firms as well as the main
barriers which prevented them from acting upon those stimuli and becoming exporters.
The chapter ends with discussion and conclusions of the study.

249

7.2

METHODOLOGY

This section sets out the procedure used to ascertain the perceptions of the nonexporters concerning the critical incidents. The procedure consisted of the sample
selection, the data collection method and instrument as well as the analytical method.

7.2.1

The Sample Selection

66 non-exporting firms were identified from the sampling frame used in Chapter 6 (subSection 6.2.2). Of these, 12 were no longer operating, leaving a usable sample of 54
firms. Of these 54 firms, only 33 met the pre-interview screening criteria (as outlined in
sub-section 7.2.2 below) and participated in the study.
7.2.2

The Pre-interview Screening and the Selection Criteria

As with the exporting firms in Chapter 6, before the actual interview, each firm was
contacted in advance by personal mobile phone. This call sought to introduce the study
to the firms, and the following criteria were also thoroughly explained.
•

Each firm was required to be either small or medium-sized, consistent with the
World Bank definition of SMEs in Ghana applied throughout the thesis (Chapter
4, Section 4.7);

•

Each firm had to be operating in the garments and textiles sub-sector and to be
located in Accra;

•

Each firm was required to have a minimum of 5 years of domestic operating
experience. This criterion was motivated by Coviello & Munro (1997), who
250

argued that the average length of domestic market operating experience for a
small New Zealand exporting firm before exporting was three years. In
extending this argument to Ghana, since New Zealand is long way ahead of
Ghana in terms of socio-economic development, it was decided that an average
SME in Ghana should have 5 years of domestic operating experience before
contemplating moving into the export business. Therefore, if after 5 years, a firm
had not considered going into the export business and had not closed down, then
it would be considered worthwhile investigating why it had not considered such
activities when other similar firms had done so;
•

Each firm had to be independent, indigenous or partly foreign and partly local.
None was to be a subsidiary of a larger domestic and/or an international firm ;
and

•

7.2.3

None of the firms was to be a wholly international local firm.

Data Collection Method and Instrument

Similarly to the interviews with exporting firms, a semi-structured interview guide was
the main data collection instrument used (see Appendix O for the structure of the nonexporters’ interview guide). Moreover, as with the exporting firms, each interview was
hand-written and digitally recorded. The interviews were face-to-face encounters and all
the interviewees were the owners of the respective SMEs. An average interview with
these non-exporters took 120 minutes. The interviews with the exporters were purposely
planned to precede those of the non-exporters because the intention was first to
understand the critical incidents from the perspective of the exporters in order to fuse
this information into the non-exporting study. Every interview took place on the
251

premises of the non-exporting firms (i.e. at the owners’ offices). Additionally, unlike
the exporting interviews where one woman chose to communicate in a language other
than English, every non-exporting firm freely agreed to communicate in English.
7.2.4

The Analytical Method

As with the analytical technique applied in the interviews with the exporting firms in
Chapter 6, in this portion of the study the responses from the non-exporting firms were
also content analysed using frequencies and rankings.

7.3 RESULTS, ANALYSIS & DISCUSSION

Appendix P contains the synthesis of results concerning this chapter. Consistent with
Chapter 6, in some instances, some of the direct quotations of the interviewees were
included to support the discussion, in line with MacDonald & Cook (1998).
7.3.1

Profile of the Non-Exporting Firms

Firstly of all, the average age of the non-exporting firm was 10 years. This deviates
from the average age of 14 found among the exporting counterparts, as analysed in
Chapter 6. It may be argued that the difference in the average firm age supports the
assumption of stage theory, which suggests that a firm should be longer established
before it starts to export (Johanson & Vahlne, 1977). Furthermore, none of the nonexporting firms had foreign ownership, compared to 3% of the exporting firms as
indicated in Chapter 6. Overall, about 70% of the entrepreneurs were females, similar to
the proportion among the exporting firms.

252

7.3.2

Results on Perceived Barriers & Reasons for Non-Exporting

Nine main perceived barriers emerged among the 33 non-exporting firms in relation to
why they still choose to focus on the domestic market. The main findings were: (1)
having a small-sized firm, (2) the lack of finance to support export business, (3) having
a negative perception about the influence of industrial associations with regard to the
export business, (4) the lack of appropriate machinery & equipment, (5) having a
negative perception of the export market, (6) the availability of business in the domestic
market to fulfil the firm’s goals, (7) the lack of ambition for long-term business growth,
(8) a lack of government support, and (9) a lack of skilled and reliable workers (see the
synthesis in Appendix P, Table 4 & 4.1). Among these reasons, (1) having a smallbusiness firm, (2) lack of finance to fund export business and (3) the lack of appropriate
machinery and equipment to support an export production were the most influential
problems perceived by the non-exporting firms, while (1) perceiving sufficient business
in the domestic market to fulfil the firm’s goals and (2) the lack of concern for a longterm international business growth motive were the least perceived problems. The
relative strengths of these factors are presented below.

7.3.3

Results on Critical Incidents Experienced by Non-Exporters

Of the six main critical incidents identified among the exporting firms in Chapter 6,
three were found to have been experienced by 14 out of the 33 non-exporting firms. Of
these 3 critical incidents, only one was an actual critical incident (as defined in subSection 6.3.6.1, Chapter 6) while the other 2 were in the form of influences. The one
critical incident was being in receipt of unsolicited order. The other two influences came
from two critical incidents, namely: (1) contacts established via having international

254

orientation (i.e. international travel) and (2) contacts and recommendations established
via having friends and families abroad. For instance, with regard to ‘contacts
established via having international orientation’, some of the non-exporters agreed that
they had travelled abroad before to visit family members (i.e. they have had
international orientation experience), but in this case, they could not establish contacts
during their visits which might have led to receipt of export orders as experienced in the
case of the exporting firms (Chapter 6).
In addition, concerning the incident of ‘contacts and recommendations gained via
having friends and families abroad’, some non-exporters affirmed that they have friends
and families living abroad, but here again, they have not thought of using those
acquaintances to trigger export initiations. Compared to the exporting firms, these nonexporters have not have discussed their business with their acquaintances abroad (e.g.
via sending them samples) to prompt their acquaintances to make contacts and
recommendations on their behalf to trigger export initiation. With regard to the relative
importance of the one incident and the two influences, the most influential among them
was the influence from having acquaintances that reside abroad, followed by the
influence from having experienced international orientation. The least influential was
the receipt of unsolicited orders. The relative importance of these themes is presented in
the table below.

7.4 RESULTS, ANALYSIS & DISCUSSION

This section is sub-divided into two main parts. Part one discusses the main perceived
barriers among the non-exporting firms as a whole concerning non-entry into the export
market. Part two concerns the actual barriers and reasons affecting the 14 non-exporters
who experienced the one critical incident and the two influences and yet could not use
them to create export business.
7.4.1

Perceived Barriers to Exporting

Of the nine perceived barriers (Table 7.1), as already indicated, the three most
influential are (1) small size of business, (2) the lack of funds to finance the export
business, and (3) the lack of machinery and equipment. Among these three factors,
every non-exporter perceived that they had too small a firm which had nothing to with
export business. On the one hand, this perception supports the assumptions of the stage

256

and RBV theories (Johanson & Vahlne, 1977 & Barney, 1991; Westhead et al., 2001
respectively) because these frameworks predict that firms must overcome all barriers to
export market entry before they can decide to export. However, a mere perception that
one’s business is small and, therefore, does not merit export business can be argued as a
lack of entrepreneurialism among these owner-managers. Hence, following the
antecedents about the international entrepreneurship phenomenon reviewed in the
literature (e.g. McDougall et al., 1994; Oviatt and McDougall, 1994; McAuley, 1999;
Dimitratos & Plakoyiannaki, 2003), it can be construed that the non-exporters in this
study lack international entrepreneurial skills.
The lack of funds to finance the export business, including the lack of working capital,
is another significant perceived barrier which, according to the cases studies prevents
them from contemplating entering the export market. Some of the case companies (e.g.
cases 7, 12, 13 & 16) mentioned a lack of funds to meet the day-to-day needs of their
firms, including funds for undertaking improvements in their plants. For instance, most
of the firms mentioned that they lacked the funds to buy new machines and/or repair
existing ones (1, 3, 10, 11, 12). According to the firms, the replacement and
maintenance costs of the machines they use (e.g. the machines for spinning, weaving,
knitting, finishing, dyeing, and embroidery) are exorbitant, and, in addition, machinery
and spare parts have to be imported. One woman lamented:
Our greatest challenge is the day-to-day money [working capital] to meet our
bills. You see as I am talking to you now I have such a large electricity bill
outstanding; they [the electricity company of Ghana] have threatened to cut off
my power on three occasions. Even sometimes some of my workers fall sick and
even the money to pay the hospital is a problem. If you go to the bank, they say
you do not have a track record….”
(Case-company 12).

257

The perception of financial barriers supports what has been found in Cyprus (Kaleka &
Katsikeas (1995), United Kingdom (Fillis, 2002), New Zealand (Shaw & Darroch,
2004) and Singapore (Keng & Jiuan, 1989). In addition, it is also one of the export
barriers among SMEs in the developed countries found by Leonidou (2004) from
among the 32 empirical studies he reviewed. However, in Leonidou’s (2004) review,
whereas financial barriers were significant, they never formed part of the high impact
factors. Conversely, with the SMEs in Ghana, the perceived financial barriers represent
one of the high impact factors. The reason may be because of the differences between
the social economic development of Ghana and developed countries (e.g. North
America and the Europe). Moreover, the finding also supports what has found in
Indonesia, India, the USA and the UK (Table 2.13, Chapter 2).
Another barrier perceived as having a high impact is the lack of appropriate machinery
and equipment to produce export products. This barrier is linked to the financial
barriers. As already seen, the serious lack of machinery the case studies mentioned
included spinning, weaving, knitting, finishing, dyeing, printing and embroidery
machinery. This particular barrier was not often evident among the export barriers
examined in the literature review in this thesis. This may be partly because most of the
studies reviewed are based on firms in the developed economies who (in themselves)
are also the producers of capital goods. As a result, SMEs in the developed countries
may not have as big a problem with capital equipment as their counterparts in Ghana.

258

The negative perceptions concerning the prospects of the export market represent
another problem perceived as having high impact. Most of the non-exporting firms have
too many reservations about venturing into the export market. Among the major
negative perceptions sampled were:

(1) limited information to locate potential

importers, (2) keen competition in the export market, (3) the huge cost of investment in
the export business, (4) too much corruption in Ghana’s ports to export goods abroad,
(5) the excessive bureaucracy in the Ghanaian business environment would hinder the
initiation of export business, and (6) perceived competitive nature of the export
business. Of these, the huge investment costs and the competitive nature of the export
business were found to be the most significant negative perceptions. The following
statement was captured directly from the interview transcripts:
To do what in the export market? To voluntarily add on more stress on myself?”
(case-company 28); “… but even here in the country look at the corruption
among the people at the CEPS [Ghana’s Customs Exercise & Preventive
Service), so with the bureaucracies and the corruption at the port do you think
one can profit by going into exporting coupled with all the bribes you will be
paying before you export a single consignment abroad?
(Case-company 17).

These perceived negative perceptions among the SMEs owners confirm the arguments
of some of the studies (e.g. Lloyd-Reason & Mughan, 2002; Westhead et al., 2004;
Ruzzier et al., 2006) reviewed in Chapter 2. These studies argue that for an SME to
export successfully, one of the most crucial variables is the influence of the ownermanager’s subjective characteristics.

259

The next perceived barrier to be discussed is the negative perception about the influence
of industrial associations on the export business. The significance of membership of
industrial associations in Ghana was specifically explored among the non-exporters
because this incident was directly linked to the trade fair events, which catapulted most
of the exporting firms into the export market (Chapter 6). According to the interview
transcripts, the average non-exporting firm in the garment and textile sub-sector holds
that: (1) industrial associations are meant for big firms, (2) there is considerable
corruption associated with the activities of industrial associations in Ghana (e.g.
nepotism & favouritism), and (3) it is a waste of time. For instance, one owner (casefirm 27) who stated she registered with the Ghana Association of Women Entrepreneurs
in 2003 (through her friend’s recommendation) confirmed that she had never attended
their meetings and nor has she since paid any membership dues, apart from on the day
when she first registered.
Overall, it can be inferred that a lack of interest in the activities of industrial
associations among the non-exporters is one of the root causes of the lack of export
business participation. The reason for this is that trade fair participation, coupled with
the membership of various industrial associations in Ghana (e.g. membership of the
Association of Ghanaian Industry, the Ghana Association of Women Entrepreneurs, the
Spinnet Textiles Garment Cluster, etc), became one of the significant influences which
catapulted the exporting firms into critical incidents that led them to export initiation
(sub-Section 6.5.1 of Chapter 6). The case firms also perceived that trade fair events are
meant for large firms that have the money to invest in them.

260

The lack of government role/incentives represents another perceived barrier. According
to some of the case companies (e.g. firms 1, 2, 3, 4, 21, 22, 27), the government fails to
support them in most areas (e.g. in finance, machinery and equipment, training) in
developing their businesses to the point of being capable of entering the export market.
While this contradicts the motive of export-led programmes outlined in Chapter 6
(Section 6.2), some of the firms (e.g. case studies 1, 11, 13, 22 & 27) maintained that
government export-led programmes in Ghana are based on political party lines (i.e. a
person has to be a member of the ruling party). One entrepreneur noted:
Oh are you asking about that one [government export-led programmes] it purely
depends on whom you know. If you do not know anybody in the government then
forget it! I have a friend who said when AGOA was announced in 2002 she
submitted her name, but until today you are interviewing me that was the end.
She did not hear anything about it so what do you think about the government
role in promoting export business in Ghana?
(Case 11)

As already indicated, it can be argued that, in general, the non-exporting firms had
negative perceptions of the role of the government of Ghana in encouraging them to
export, despite the numerous export-led government programmes reviewed in this thesis
(e.g. see Chapter 1, Section 1.1, Chapter 3, Section 3.6 & Chapter 6, and Section 6.2).
However, this apparent contradiction seems to suggest that: (1) although there may be
many on-going export-led programmes, a perception of corruption may prevent most
small firms from accessing these promotional packages; (2) to a certain extent, past and
on-going government export-led programmes are only targeted at existing exporters and
exclude non-exporters; or (3) the programmes are not always effective.

261

The lack of skilled workers is a further barrier. The case companies indicated that they
do not have skilled workers to make them confident in bidding for export orders. One
owner-manager lamented:
But one of the problems is also with our workers. In this country they say we
have polytechnics and the University Science and Technology (one of the public
universities in Ghana), but what do they do? They are producing school leavers
that can only read and write. Now they all want to work with banks when they
leave school.
(Case study 13).
It can be understood from the preceding quote that failings of the education system are a
partial cause of the under-industrialisation of Ghana. Another owner-manageress also
lamented:
You see apart from the skilled workers we need to operate the machines that are
used in this industry, our workers here are very poor at finishing skills [e.g.
putting on buttons, flaps and open stitches on clothes] which is also the critical
thing in producing clothing, especially when you want to send it abroad.
(Case-firm 22).

This lack of skilled, full-time time workers to work on export orders supports the
findings in Leonidou’s (2004) review of 32 empirical papers (Appendix I). Consistent
with what was found by Fillis (2002) in the UK and Ireland, some of the firms (e.g.
case-firms 1, 7 & 14) maintained that there is sufficient business for them in the
domestic market and consequently, they are not interested in entering the export market.
Some of the owner-entrepreneurs (e.g. case-firms 8, 11, 13) indicated that they had not
thought about the export business because they established their businesses just to
support their household, and therefore, business growth that transcends national borders
is not a priority. A common characteristic of this group of non-exporters (e.g. case-firms

262

8, 11, 13) was the ‘lack of a sense of seriousness’ in what they did. This barrier also
links to what Hutchinson et al. (2009) found from the UK sample, namely a lack of
export business vision. It can also be concluded that the barrier is connected with a lack
of entrepreneurial qualities among these owner-entrepreneurs in Ghana, and again
supports the importance of entrepreneurial qualities as proposed by the international
entrepreneurship literature (McDougall et al., 1994).
7.4.2

Actual Barriers Preventing Use of Critical Incidents to Initiate Export

The barriers that actually prevented the 14 out of the 33 firms that encountered the one
critical incident and two influences from exporting are discussed as follows. In Table
7.2, one of the influences that some of the non-exporters said they had experienced was
the ‘international orientation’ phenomenon. Four firms (i.e. firms 1, 7, 12 & 27) referred
to previous international travel as the main influence from their previous experiences.
From these four non-exporters (i.e. case studies 1, 7, 12 & 27), it was found that their
separate experiences were informal visits paid to families and friends overseas. Each of
the case firms indicated that they had had the opportunity to travel abroad once up until
the time of the interview. In terms of why they did not maximise the visits to initiate
export business, the owner-manager of case company 1 indicated he had paid a visit to
Amsterdam in 2001 to visit his brother in-law. According to this owner-manager, before
he travelled to Amsterdam, he knew it was an opportunity to promote his business
(because his business associate had told him he could use the occasion to promote his
business over there), but he refused.
Evidently, what prevented him was that it was his first visit to his brother-in-law and his
sister, and so he thought it would be impolite to seize the opportunity to promote his

263

business. When he was asked whether or not he had talked about making a second visit
to his family in the Netherlands in order to promote his business, he answered ‘no’
because his business had been slowing down up until the time of the interview. The
barrier to this firm can therefore be interpreted as a lack of entrepreneurial spirit to
identify profitable export business since it was apparent from the results on the
exporting firms that he could still have done business in the Netherlands through his
hosts without travelling there directly (e.g. by sending samples to businesses and
churches). In addition, the owner of case company 27 revealed that she visited the
United Kingdom, but she went for personal reasons and not for business motives. She
observed:
For me the thought of trying to promote my business in the UK was not my main
intention and to be frank with you, the idea that I could use the visit to become
an exporter never struck my mind. In fact, I went to ‘abrotwere’ (i.e. a Ghanaian
terminology which is used to refer to every western country) for personal
ambition and for personal satisfaction and not because of my business. As you
know over here [Ghana], if you have travelled to ‘abrotwere’ before, you
command a lot of respect. So I took the opportunity to participate in my
brother’s graduation in Manchester in 2002 just to see the beauty of United
Kingdom because lots of people have said many things about the place. In fact,
when I came back, my family was very happy and proud!
(Case study 27)

It could possibly be argued that the quote above reflects a lack of the necessary
entrepreneurialism to spot export business opportunities and/or lack of export business
vision. When the woman visited the UK she did not have her business in mind, although
she could indeed have formed alliances and used the African community there to
promote her business. In addition, she stated that she had never discussed with her host
if she could make a second visit, this time to promote her business, or whether there

264

could be an opportunity to send samples. Furthermore, the other two case studies (cases
7 and 12) among the four firms mentioned above also maintained that they had made
trips abroad, visiting the USA and the UK respectively over Christmas.
Neither were these visits for business purposes, but undertaken for personal reasons.
The owners of case companies 7 and 12 indicated that before they travelled abroad, they
too had never thought of taking their firms into the international market, because in their
view their firms seemed too small to achieve anything on the international market. From
these two case firms, having too small a business was the main ‘mental’ blockage
inhibiting their firms’ opportunity for growth. Having a small firm that did not merit
export business was among the perceptions of non-exporters discussed in the review of
export barrier literature and those presented in Appendices H & I. For instance, this
finding confirms what is found in the UK & the Republic of Ireland (Fillis, 2002) as
well as in New Zealand (Shaw & Darroach, 2004). Finally, what prevented these SMEs
from using their international visits, both during and after their time abroad, also
provides some evidence of the lack of entrepreneurial spirit and international business
vision on the part of these owner-entrepreneurs, coupled with the mental picture of
having a small-sized firm. The presence of the barrier concerning lack international
business vision among these non-exporting case companies supports similar findings
from the UK (Hutchinson et al., 2009).
Secondly, receipt of unsolicited orders is the one main critical incident two of the nonexporters (i.e. case studies 10 & 13) encountered. According to the stage theory, an
unsolicited order can be defined as an approach to supply an export order which hitherto
a firm was not interested in doing, and/or not preparing for. First of all, the owner of
case-company 10 indicated that her unsolicited order was received in Ghana through an
265

exporting firm (i.e. this was case-firm 4 among the exporting firms interviewed in
Chapter 6). According to this non-exporter, the exporting firm received a huge order
through the AGOA in 2003 (from the USA) and through her husband she had the
opportunity to co-produce the order. According to this owner-manageress, the two main
barriers that prevented her from working on the order were: (1) a lack of skilled and
reliable workers and (2) a lack of appropriate machinery and equipment. With regard to
the former, the owner-manageress indicated that she had nine full-time workers in
addition to herself, but the quality of work among her workers was insufficient to fulfil
an international order. She lamented:

You see the finishing skills of my workers are very poor; meanwhile this area is
the most critical and crucial with every garment to be manufactured, especially
those which are meant to be sold abroad.
(Case company 10)
According to her, in order not to cause a problem for the firm that gave her the
opportunity to be involved in exporting, she declined to work on the order. In addition,
the owner also indicated that she had a serious problem with absenteeism among her
workers which also made her unwilling to accept the order because she did not want to
spoil her relationship with her business associates (the exporting firm that gave her the
unsolicited order). Further, the owner of case firm 10 indicated that since most of her
machines were old (especially her embroidery and knitting machinery), she doubted
they could deliver the order on time and up to standard. The barrier of lack of skilled
workers to work on export orders, coupled with the barrier of a lack of appropriate
machinery and equipments, confirms the presence of the barriers to export business
reviewed in Chapter 2. In particular, it confirms what was found among similar small
businesses in Detroit, the USA (Yaprak, 1985), Northern Ireland and the UK (Fillis,
266

2002). It can be confirmed that these barriers do hinder export initiation, just as, in their
absence, contrasting influences facilitated the capacities of the exporting counterparts to
deliver export orders (Chapter 6, sub-Section 6.5.2.2). With regard to unsolicited orders,
the owner of case company 13 said he had received an order in 1999 to deliver school
uniforms to France through his sister who is married to a French man. He identifies the
problems that prevented him from seizing that critical incident and becoming an
exporter as follows.

Well I did not set up this business actually to take it to the export market, but
when I had this order, I was so happy; however, main barriers were that: (1) I
did not have money to pre-finance the order as the importer too wanted it so
because she said she was dealing with me for the first time, that was her
condition and (2) my sewing machines were all old and I did not have knitting
and finishing machines at that time and there was no pre-financed money to
fund them. Eventually, I thought of collaborating with some other businesses,
but because of misunderstanding over sharing the work and the profit that
would arise, it did not work. So when the importer saw that I was delaying he
called me and stopped the whole deal.
(Case firm 13)

Besides the lack of funds, machinery and equipment, the preceding quotation also
shows that lack of trust/business collaboration and/or partnership among businesses in
Ghana has great potential to stifle export business initiations. The barrier of lack of
finance to fund export business appeared to be a common finding among researchers
(e.g. Fillis, 2002; Leonidou, 2004; Tesfom & Lutz, 2006; Hutchinson et al., 2009).
The other influence that some of the non-exporting firms experienced arose from the
fact that they have friends and family that reside abroad. Eight case firms (i.e. case
studies 2, 3, 4, 9, 15, 13, 21 & 28) maintained that they had friends and families residing
abroad. Of these eight firms none have ever visited their acquaintances abroad, nor had

267

they discussed exporting opportunities with these acquaintances overseas up until the
time of the interview. Overall, all these firms (e.g. case firms 3, 4, 9, 15, 13, 21) stated
that their businesses seemed too small for them to think about export business, and they
had consequently not felt motivated to send samples of their products to solicit for
export orders.
The main reason why these firms never used these influences to initiate export business
is due to their perception of having too small a firm. However, this barrier can also be
interpreted as being linked to the lack of the entrepreneurial skills needed to spot
profitable export business opportunities and/or the lack of export business vision. For
example, having acquaintances abroad and not knowing that he or she can be used to
trigger export business shows a real lack of entrepreneurialism. This lack of
entrepreneurial skills supports findings by McDougall et al. (1994), while the
perception of having too small a business supports findings from the UK and the
Republic of Ireland (Hutchinson et al., 2009). The barriers that prevented the 14 firms
from converting the one critical incident and two influences they experienced into
initiation of export business are summarised in the table below.

268
