7.5 CONCLUSION

Nine main perceived barriers emerged as being responsible for non-exporters choosing
to remain focused on the domestic market. They are summarised in Table 7.4 below.

269

Table 7.4: Perceived Reasons for Non-Exporting and their Sources
Nature of Impact

Type of Barrier
(1) Being too small a firm(ib)

Very significant impact (3)

(2) Lack of finance (ib)
(3) Lack of machinery and equipment
(ib)
(4) Negative perception about
industrial associations (eb)

Significant impact (3)

(5) Negative perception about the
export market (ib)
(6) Lack of skilled workers (ib)
(7) Lack of business support from
government (eb)

Least significant impact (3)

(8) Sufficient business in the
domestic market (eb)
(9) Lack of concern for long-term
international business growth (ib)
Source: reconfigured from Table 7.1
Keys: ib = the source is an internal barrier & eb= source is an external barrier

Table 7.4 indicates that the barriers perceived by the non-exporting firms derive mainly
from internal weaknesses. Surprisingly, lack of incentives from the government is one
of the lowest perceived barriers. Barriers and/or reasons (1), (2), (3) & (6) support the
assumptions of the RBV and stage theories about the determinants of SME export
decisions. In addition, barriers (5), (4) (7) & (8) support the assumptions of contingency
theories concerning how firms’ strategies (planned or emergent or both) are shaped

270

directly or indirectly by the influence of the operating environment. Moreover, barrier
(9) also supports the assumptions of the international entrepreneurship literature
concerning the behaviour that catapults firms into the export business. These results
support the assumptions that the export behaviour of the SME is indeed a complex
event which cannot be fully understood by a single theoretical framework. The results
also imply that external environmental factors are equally important in fully explaining
the export behaviour of the small firm.
Second, with regard to the main barriers and/or reasons which prevented some of the
non-exporters from exploiting the one critical incident as well as the two influences
which came their way, the following can be concluded. Of the six critical incidents
found in Chapter 6, only one incident (i.e. the receipt of unsolicited orders) and two
influences (i.e. (1) having international orientation – previous overseas travel - and (2)
having friends and family residing abroad) emerged as events that had been experienced
by the non-exporting firms interviewed, and then only by 14 of the 33 firms. Of these
three themes the influence from friends and family residing abroad (i.e. networking with
friends and family abroad) was the most significant, followed by the influence of having
international orientation. The receipt of unsolicited orders was the least significant
influence.
The main barriers which prevented the firms from converting the influence they
encountered into critical incidents which could have triggered export initiation were: (1)
having a very small business and (2) a lack of the entrepreneurialism needed to spot
profitable export business opportunity and/or lack of export vision. Of these two
barriers, the perception of having a very small firm was the most influential. Concerning
the receipt of unsolicited orders, the main barriers were: (1) lack of skilled workers to
271

work on the orders, (2) lack of appropriate machinery and equipment and (3) lack of
funding to pre-finance the order. Among these factors, lack of having appropriate
equipment and machinery had the greatest influence, followed by a lack of funding and
skilled workers.
Overall, it can be concluded that both actual and perceived barriers were mostly
internal, which also supports the argument of the importance of SMEs’ internal resource
capacity to any subsequent export success (e.g. Ibeh, 2004). In addition, having the
perception that a firm is too small and cannot go into the export business until it
achieves a certain size supports the assumptions of stage theory (Johanson & Valhne,
1977). Furthermore, the lack of entrepreneurial vision on the part of the owners of the
firms to spot the opportunities (e.g. the receipt of unsolicited orders) to initiate export
business strongly supports the assumptions of the international entrepreneur theory
(McDougall et al., 1994). The lack of skilled workers, appropriate machinery and
equipment confirms the assumptions of the RBV theory. Overall, the manifestation of a
mixture of more than one theoretical framework in these findings supports the
assumptions of the integrated framework underlying this thesis.
Finally, these findings (i.e. the constraints on the non-exporting firms’ participation in
export business) can be compared and contrasted effectively with the findings from their
exporting counterparts in Chapter 6 (e.g. Chapter 6, sub-Sections 6.5.2.1, 6.5.2.2 &
6.5.2.3) on factors that influence the capacity of the exporters to meet their export
orders. The reason for this is because the factors which facilitated the capacity of
exporting firms (Chapter 6) to respond positively to export critical incidents are the
same factors non-export lacked and therefore, constraint their export involvement. In
addition, just as was found in Chapter 6, this implies that, regardless of the benefits of
272

contingency factors, a certain amount of internal readiness, and/or preparedness (e.g. the
right product) on the part of the non-exporters toward entering the export market is
essential.

7.6

CONCLUDING REMARKS

It was hypothesised that non-exporters in the garment & textile industries in Ghana
experienced the same critical incidents which catapulted their counterpart exporting
firms into the export business. The hypothesis was partially supported, with 14 out of
the 33 non-exporters affirming some experience of one critical incident and two
influences from the 6 critical incidents found among the exporting firms. Overall, nonexporters reject export stimuli and remain concentrated on the domestic market for
reasons that are mostly of internal origin. As a result of limited resource capacity, these
companies reject the export stimuli because of fears that they would not be able to
control the barriers in the export market. The strongest barriers seem to include the
perception that the firm is too small; export business is the preserve of the large firms,
negative perceptions about prospects in the export market as well as lack of
entrepreneurialism. The results indicate that, regardless of firm size, the reasons for
non-exporting show a similar pattern. Therefore, these empirical findings lend some
support to the non-exporting literature (Section, 2.10, Chapter 2).

273

CHAPTER EIGHT

SUMMARY OF FINDINGS, CONCLUSIONS AND
IMPLICATIONS

8.1

INTRODUCTION

This chapter responds to objective 5 of the thesis; the main motivation for this thesis
was to understand the exporting behaviour of SMEs from the garment and textile
manufacturing sub-sector in Ghana. As a result, the thesis sought to answer the main
question: why is the export involvement of manufacturing (SMEs) in Ghana so low,
with the majority of the firms remaining focused on the domestic market? The literature
review established that this problem can be understood through focusing on the
internationalisation of the firm and/or the export behaviour of the firm. Most theoretical
frameworks (e.g. stage theory, international entrepreneurship theory, and the resourcebased view) in the field offer various assumptions with regard to answering the problem
above. Although empirical studies have been conducted to explore the assumptions of
these theoretical frameworks (i.e. the literature review/Chapter 2 & other studies Appendices A to I of the thesis), the theoretical frameworks used were based on the
export behaviour of firms in developed countries, and the empirical studies were mainly
based on small firms from developed countries (e.g. the North America & Europe).

274

Therefore, because the socio-economic contexts of the developed countries differ from
those of developing countries, there has been little exploration of the export behaviour
of SMEs from a developing country like Ghana in the literature. As a result, the extant
frameworks in the field and the corresponding empirical findings cannot be merely
externally generalised to Ghana. Four theoretical frameworks (i.e. the resource-based
view, stage theory, network theory and international entrepreneurship) were identified
from the literature review to address the theme of the thesis in detail. While the
contributions of extant studies by previous researchers in the field are worthy of
appreciation, some studies (e.g. Bell, 1995; Coviello & Munro, 1995; Westhead et al.,
2001; Moen & Servais, 2002) apply a single theoretical framework to analyse the
phenomenon.
However, another group of researchers (e.g. Coviello & Martin, 1999; Crick & Spence,
2005) contend that SME export behaviour represents a complex and interconnected
phenomenon that cannot be fully understood through application of a single theoretical
framework. As a result, these researchers apply more than one theoretical framework to
the topic. Hence, in order to contribute to the latter argument, the four frameworks
above were integrated and applied in the thesis, taking into account the most influential
variables (e.g. firm size, firm age, foreign ownership, possession of adequate financial
capital, receipt of unsolicited orders, contacts based on prior foreign residency and work
abroad) in terms of inducing the firm to export.
In addition, because previous frameworks have not fully accounted for the influence of
the external environment on SME export behaviour, the contingency theory was added
to the four frameworks above in order to shed greater light on the phenomenon. As a
result, sector and/or industry influence was selected in addition to the variables above in
275

order to account for the influence from the external environment. The assumption
behind the proposed integrated framework is that its in-depth examination of the
combination of variables offers a deeper understanding of why one small and/or
medium-sized firm will export from Ghana, while other similar firms will not, even
though they may all be operating in the same location and face the same market
conditions. Another inspiration behind the integrated framework is that by empirically
testing this framework in Ghana, researchers and practitioners can be offered a fresh
understanding of the topic in an African developing country in detail. As a result, the
major thrust of this research rests on empirically validating the proposed integrated
theoretical framework (i.e. achieved in Chapters 5, 6 & 7).
The remainder of this chapter is organised as follows. First, the research questions
behind the thesis are presented sequentially. This is followed by a summary of the major
findings of each research question together with the conclusions drawn. Next, the clear
contributions from the research are presented, followed by the implications of the
findings for public policy, private practice and future research. The chapter then focuses
on the limitations of the study approach before finally providing concluding remarks.

8.2

PRESENTATION OF THE RESEARCH QUESTIONS

8.2.1

Research Question 1

In research question 1 the thesis sought to test whether or not, in the light of the
theoretical assumptions of the integrated theoretical framework, there is likelihood that
firm size and age, average aggregate educational level of the workers, foreign

276

ownership, and sector could predict whether or not a small business in Ghana would
enter the export market.
It was found that in relation to export propensity, the size of a firm predicts the decision
to initiate export business, whereas firm age was not found to be helpful in this respect.
Furthermore, having foreign ownership (i.e. being partly foreign-owned and partly
locally owned) predicts the likelihood of entering the export market and the aggregate
workers’ educational level (i.e. having secondary education and above) also facilitates
the firm’s transition from a wholly domestic to an international business firm. In
addition, the influence of the sector and/or industry also predicts the decision to export.
From these findings, the conclusions drawn are as follows. First, concerning the low
export propensity of SMEs from Ghana, it can be concluded that small firm size, the
low level of formal education of full-time workers, a lack of foreign ownership and
being in an inappropriate sector (e.g. sectors other than the wood sector), are
significantly associated with the majority of non-exporting SMEs in Ghana, thereby
suggesting the main reasons for low export involvement. Second, relating to the low
export involvement of SMEs from Ghana, it can also be argued that not being in the
appropriate sector (e.g. the wood industry), as well as being unable to learn and apply
the export behaviour of firms in the wood sector, contribute to the low export
involvement among Ghanaian firms.
Overall, the importance of the firm size (stage theory and RBV variable), aggregate
workers’ education level (RBV variable), foreign ownership, (the network theory
variable & RBV) and sector influence (contingency theory variable), interacting to
produce a single outcome (SMEs export behaviour), validate and reinforce the

277

integrated theoretical framework argument that informs the thesis. Evidence from the
network and contingency theories addresses and counteracts the weaknesses of the RBV
and stage theories identified in the literature review (Section 2.5, Literature Review).
For instance, the significant influence of industry on export propensity implies that
external factors cannot be excluded if a deeper understanding of exporting and nonexporting SMEs behaviour is to be achieved.
8.2.2

Research Question 2

In this research question, the thesis sought to identify the main critical incidents which
catapult export initiation among SMEs in Ghana and the main influences which enable
the firms to meet their export orders. Here the aim was to ascertain the main incidents
and the influences from the firm owners’ perceptions. In this chapter the main findings
were: (1) being in receipt of unsolicited orders, (2) contacts established through trade
fair participation, (3) contacts established via having foreign orientation, (4) contacts
and recommendation established via having friends and family residing abroad, (5)
winning government awards and (6) contacts and recommendations via having foreign
ownership. Of these six incidents, contacts established via trade fair participation,
contacts established via having international orientation, contacts and recommendation
gained via having friends and family abroad, as well as winning government’s awards,
ranked the highest in terms of impact (Figure 6.1, Chapter 6).
The main factors in influencing the firms’ capacity to meet their export orders are as
follows: of the stage theory variables, having a large number of full-time workers and
possessing adequate prior domestic operating experience were the most significant
factors (Figure 6.2, Chapter 6). Four variables from the RBV were found to be

278

influential, these were: having a large number of full-time workers, holding available
financial capital, possessing a stock of appropriate equipment and machinery and an
adequate supply of skilled production workers. Among these factors, the first three were
ranked equally in terms of their relative importance, followed by skilled production
workers (Figure 6.3, Chapter 6).
Three international entrepreneurship factors were also found to be influential: having
export vision at the commencement of business, international risk taking behaviour, and
a positive perception of the export market. These three factors were ranked equally with
regard to their relative importance (Figure 6.4, Chapter 6). Finally, the most influential
network theory factors, in order of importance, were found to be: networking with
industrial associations, networking with a large number of industrial associations, and
maintaining frequent contact with industrial associations (Figure 6.5, Chapter 6).
From these findings, the conclusions drawn are as follows. The results on critical
incidents that trigger export initiation suggest that few SMEs in Ghana (e.g. from the
garment & textile sector) are able to participate in trade shows. Similarly, few of them
have had an opportunity to develop international orientation (i.e. experience of living
and working abroad). In addition, the majority of them lack the advantage of having
friends and family members that reside abroad. Moreover, few of them have the
capacity to win government awards. Therefore, these critical incidents also partly
explain the reasons why the export involvement of SMEs from Ghana is low.
The findings on whether or not a firm would be in the position to deliver any export
orders they might receive suggest that most non-exporting firms in Ghana are of a small
size, which confirms the results from the research question one. As a result, those firms

279

might be offered an export business opportunity, but might not have the internal
resources (i.e. sufficient workers, including skilled production workers) to meet the
order. Second, the findings suggest that most non-exporting SMEs in Ghana (e.g. from
the garment and textile sector) lack adequate financial capital and appropriate stocks of
equipment and machinery; as a result of this they may not be able to meet export orders
even if they have the opportunity to export.
Furthermore, the findings reveal that most of the non-exporting firms lack an
international entrepreneurial outlook. In addition, the majority do not have: export
vision, possession of international risk taking behaviour, and do not possess a positive
perception regarding the prospects of the export market. The results also suggest that
non-exporting SMEs from Ghana do not possess networking relations with industrial
associations; and those that do, do not show commitment to the associations’ activities.
This failure among non-exporters to form networking relations with industrial
associations in Ghana limits their access to export business opportunities as well as the
opportunity to participate in trade fairs.
Here again, the findings show that the export behaviour of the SME is a function of a
multiplicity of complex factors, confirming the thesis argument that no single
theoretical framework can fully explain the process. Moreover, the findings from the
critical incidents indicate that the decision to export among the firms is partly caused by
external stimuli. This significantly supports the assumptions of contingency theory in
terms of the export behaviour of the small firm.
Through the application of the five theoretical frameworks to the thesis, experiences of
the case-companies demonstrated that three of these frameworks (i.e. the network

280

framework, the international entrepreneurship and the contingency framework) appear
to have a significant influence on the export success of SMEs from Ghana. This is
explained by the fact that almost all of the critical incidents that activated export
initiation by the firms consisted of forms of interaction from within the network,
international entrepreneurship and contingency theories. For instance, four out of the six
critical incidents were all associated with networking factors (i.e. contacts established
via trade fair, contacts established via international orientation, contacts and
recommendations

gained

via

having

foreign

ownership

and

contacts

and

recommendations gained via having friends and family that resided abroad), thereby
affirming the importance of the network framework in the Ghanaian context.
In terms of meeting their export orders, it was again apparent that the internal capacity
of the exporting firms was inadequate and that they therefore benefited from access to
networking associates’ resource stocks (e.g. networking with industrial associations). In
most of these incidents, the ‘entrepreneurialism’ of the owner-managers enabled the
firms to seize external contingency factors (e.g. establishing solid contacts at trade
shows) as well as accessing the resource capacity factors of their networking associates
(e.g. keeping in frequent touch with industrial associations) . On the basis of this
evidence in the interview transcripts, the original integrated theoretical framework was
revised (see Chapter 6, Figure 6.6) to reflect the actual exporting behaviour of the firms
from Ghana.
The revised integrated framework predicts that while the average size of an exporting
firm from Ghana (e.g. from the garments and textiles sub-sector) is small, the
entrepreneurial qualities of the owner are crucial in enabling the firms to take full
advantage of contingency factors. The revised framework further implies that
281

networking relations with associates (e.g. industrial associations) augment internal
resource capacity, enabling firms to respond positively to export stimuli and initiate
export business. Overall, the revised framework predicts that Ghanaian non-exporting
SMEs are on average too small to respond positively to export stimuli, but enhanced
entrepreneurial qualities (e.g. of the owner-managers risk taking behaviour) coupled
with access to the resource stocks of networking associates) the export success of SMEs
from Ghana can be facilitated.
8.2.2.1 Comparison of the Regression Results & the Outcome of Interviews
As it was not possible to prove causality from the regression analysis in Chapter 5, the
purpose of the interviews (Chapter 6) was to explore the critical incidents that cause
manufacturing SMEs from Ghana (e.g. the garment & textile sub-sector) to initiate
export business and to find out whether or not the factors that enable one firm to take
advantage of a critical incident and initiate export business were the same as those that
emerged as significant from regression results and the general factors arising from the
proposed integrated theoretical framework (Figure 1.1, Chapter 1 & Figure 2.6, Chapter
2).
However, the results from the interviews simply suggest variables (e.g. firm size,
importance of workforce education level, networking and human capital resources of
foreign partners) which emerged as statistically significant from the regression analysis,
were strongly confirmed in the interviews (see sub-sections 6.4.2.1 & 6.4.2.2). For
instance, concerning variables relating to networking resources of foreign partners, the
case company 8 indicated that the financial and the human capital resources brought by
the foreign partners put them at an advantage compared to others in relation to taking

282

advantage of a critical incident and initiating export business. Taken in tandem, the
results of the interviews coupled with the regression analysis confirm the interacted
variables from the various theoretical frameworks that inform the thesis.
8.2.3

Research Question 3

In this research question, the thesis sought to examine the extent to which the nonexporting SMEs in the garments & textiles sector experience the same ‘critical
incidents’ which enable their counterpart exporting firms to initiate export business.
Here the intention was to identify the general reasons for non-exporting among the
firms, including actual barriers constraining them from acting upon critical incidents
and becoming exporters. The results of this research question indicate that the
significant barriers to exporting were: (1) having a small-sized business firm, (2) lack of
funds to finance export business, (3) lack of appropriate machinery and equipment, (4)
having a negative perception about export market opportunities and (5) having negative
perceptions about export market opportunities. Of these five factors, the first three were
ranked equally as being of most importance, while the last two were both ranked next in
importance. The firm’s perception of the adequacy of the business in the domestic
market to fulfil its goals, the lack of ambition for long-term business growth (including
taking the export business to export market), and a lack of government support were
least perceived as barriers (Chapter 7, Table 7.1).
In addition, concerning the hypothesis that non-exporters experience similar critical
incidents to the exporters, the main findings were that 14 out of the 33 non-exporting
firms interviewed maintained that they had some experience of one critical incident and
two influences (from two critical incidents). These were: (1) receipt of unsolicited
orders (i.e. the one critical incident); (2) international orientation (an influence) (i.e.
283

prior international travel) and (3) having friends and family that reside abroad (the
second influence). Among these 3 factors, having friends and families residing abroad
emerged as the most influential factor (Table 7.2, Chapter 7).
From the interviews, three main barriers were found to constrain the capacity of the 14
non-exporters to maximise unsolicited orders and become exporters; these were: lack of
finance, lack of skilled and reliable workers, as well as lack of appropriate machinery
and equipment. Of these three, lack of appropriate machinery and equipment was the
most significant barrier (Chapter 7, Table 7.3). Of the firms that experienced influences
from the two critical incidents, the main reasons that prevented them from converting
the influences into export critical incidents and becoming exporters were: (1) the
perception of having too small a firm, and (2) a lack of the entrepreneurial spirit to spot
export opportunities and/or lack of export business vision. Across these two factors, the
perception of having too small a firm was the most significant.
The conclusions that can be drawn for research question 3 are as follows. The actual and
perceived barriers that prevent non-exporters from venturing into export business
confirm the revised framework developed from the interviews with the exporters
(Figure 6.6, Chapter 6). The reason for this is that the factors from the three theoretical
frameworks that significantly explained the export success of exporting firms from
Chapter 6 were the main areas in which the non-exporting firms showed evidence of
weaknesses.
In particular, the findings from the non-exporters imply that due to the lack of
entrepreneurial outlook and networking with industrial associations, non-exporting
firms do not respond to changing trends of the Ghanaian business environment. While

284

networking influenced the export success of the exporting firms, this was not the case
with the non-exporting firms. Here, it can be argued that the issue is not due to gender
difference, but differences in networking behaviour based on whether a firm is wholly a
domestic market business or exporting business. The explanation for this is that while in
both groups of firms (i.e. exporters and non-exporters) women owner-managers
represent about three quarters of the samples, non-exporting firms (wholly domestic
firms) appear to lack the density of networking contacts and degree of networking
interactions that facilitated the export success of the exporting firms. Therefore, the
variation is attributable to the distinction between a wholly domestic firm and an
exporting company and not to gender differences. This contradicts researchers (e.g.
Moore, 1990; Renzulli, 1998) who found gender to be relevant to differences in
business networking.
Over all, it can be concluded that non-exporters fail to spot and seize the contingency
factors which have the potential to propel them into the export market. Moreover, the
empirical findings on the perceptions of the non-exporting firms concerning the actual
and perceived barriers to exporting indicate that export success of the firms is connected
to both their internal and external behaviours, with internal proactivity seeming to be
particularly important. Finally, evidence on export barriers deriving from the RBV,
stage theory and the network theory (among others) supports the assumption behind
applying an integrated rather than a single theoretical framework to gain a deeper
understanding of such events.
Here, it must be also added that the results of the regression analysis (Chapter 5) lend
some supports to the perceptions on both perceived and actual barriers that prevented
some of the non-exporters from taking advantage of the critical incident which came
285

their way and initiating export business. In fact, the same factors (e.g. firm size,
educated and/or skilled workforce) which enabled the export firms to maximise the
critical incidents that came their way and initiate export business (which also emerged
as significant from the regression analysis) were found to be lacking among the nonexporters: in these cases preventing them from taking advantage of critical incidents and
initiating export business.

8.3

RESEARCH CONTRIBUTIONS

8.3.1

Contribution to Knowledge

The aim of the research was to understand the exporting behaviour of manufacturing
SMEs from the garment & textile sub-sector of Ghana. While export manufacturing in
particular has been hailed in Ghana as a significant driver for Ghana’s socio-economic
development, currently, local involvement in export business is low. As a result, the
research sought to examine why the majority of manufacturing SMEs in Ghana remain
focused on the domestic market. Although similar problems have been addressed in the
literature, the theoretical underpinnings of these studies are mostly based on the context
of developed countries (e.g. North America & Europe). Therefore, a research gap exists
with regard to the export behaviour of SMEs in Africa (e.g. Ghana). The problem here
is that not knowing how the behaviour of SMEs in Ghana relates to that of SMEs in the
developed economies has serious implications for practitioners and public policy.
The contribution of this thesis lies in the formulation of an integrated theoretical
framework set in the context of Ghana and its empirical validation using (1) the World

286

Bank’s secondary data set, (2) case study interviews with 36 exporting small businesses
from the garment & textile sub-sector, and (3) another sample of case study interviews
with 33 non-exporting SMEs from the same sector. The application of the integrative
framework in this thesis extended a line of research in the field (e.g. Coviello & Martin,
1999; Crick & Spence, 2005) which contends that the export behaviour of the small
owner-managed firm represents a complex, dynamic and interactive event which cannot
be fully understood using a single theoretical framework.
This is the first study to systematically analyse the export behaviour of SMEs in Ghana,
especially the exporting and the non-exporting behaviour of SMEs from the garments
and textiles sub-sector.

Previous studies (e.g. Quartey, 2006) on the sub-sector

examined the future prospects of all firms in the sector in terms of the challenges they
faced, without specifically focusing on the SMEs export potential of the sub-sector. The
reason for this is because development of SMEs export capabilities of the sub-sector has
been the focus of Ghana’s government agenda to develop manufacturing export due to
the potential export spillover effects from the garment and textile sub-sector to
manufacturing SMEs in other sub-sectors. In addition, whilst another study (Kuffour,
2008) has focused on the employment generating capacity of the firms from the subsector in terms of the previous government’s Presidential Special Initiative (PSI)
programme, this study did not consider the internationalisation capacity of firms from
the sub-sector. In addition, it has been established that Ghana’s market level is too low
to mall that it cannot foster growth of its firms (Rankin et al., 2000). Therefore,
focusing this study to this sub-sector with regard to promoting manufacturing export
competitiveness of SMEs from Ghana is appropriate because it informs the
government’s police framework current being implemented in Ghana.

287

This study is the most comprehensive so far on exporting and non-exporting SMEs
behaviour in Ghana in terms of the range of variables used to explain the theme of the
thesis. For instance, the integrated theoretical framework captures the major influential
factors discussed in the literature review (Chapter 2) (e.g. firm characteristics, the
entrepreneur owner-manager characteristics and

external factors). Even within the

field, previous studies (e.g. Westhead et al., 2001; Obben & Magagula, 2003) have dealt
with only a few selected factors, namely firm and managerial factors, and human capital
resource factors respectively. Furthermore, it must be stated that developing an
understanding of firms’ export behaviour necessitates exploration of the attitudes of
both exporting and non-exporting firms in a single study. However, very few studies in
the field have focused on non-exporting SMEs’ behaviour (see Section 2.10, Chapter 2).
As a result, Chapter 7 of the thesis makes a fresh contribution to non-exporting
literature in the field based on evidence from Ghana. The importance of the contribution
to the non-exporting literature derives from the exploration by the thesis of the extent to
which some non-exporting firms may have actually been hindered by barriers in their
attempt to become exporters. This approach deviates significantly from those adopted
by extant studies (e.g. Leonidou, 1995b; Fillis, 2002).

8.4

IMPLICATIONS OF THE STUDY

The research holds possesses implications for public policy, practitioners and future
researchers, some of which are summarised in the following section.

288

8.4.1

Contribution to SMEs &/or Practitioners

Due to the nature of export manufacturing in Ghana (e.g. labour intensive coupled with
mass production), non-exporting owner-managers who desire to enter the export market
must use part of their profits to attract more full-time workers, train and retain them. In
addition, SME owner-managers who wish to deepen their export involvement must also
increase the size of their firms and recruit full-time workers with higher levels of
education (e.g. workers with secondary education and above). The exporting firms, but
even more so the non-exporting firms must also vigorously explore opportunities to
form partnership with foreigners, since the findings from the studies indicated that such
partnerships would facilitate their export success. For example, the study indicated that
foreign partners have the potential to expose Ghanaian local firms to upstream and
downstream international networks and to thereby augment their internal resource
capacity. Moreover, non-exporting owner-managers from Ghana who desire to enter the
international market must understand that the phenomenon is a complex, dynamic and
interactive. They must therefore align their strategy (planned or emergent) to the
changing trends of the external environment, as both the results of the regression
analysis and interviews show that external stimuli appear crucial with regard to
promoting export success of Ghanaian firms.
For instance, specifically in terms of firms in the garments and textiles sub-sector, the
findings indicate that the export stimuli which drove the sub-sector were mainly
external pro-active stimuli, confirming the contingency theory findings from the
regression analysis. While the importance of the contingency factors has become
obvious, for Ghanaian firms to succeed in the export market they must constantly build
their internal resource capacity. Although various external stimuli (e.g. the availability

289

of trade fair events, government industrial programmes, and unsolicited orders) may
help to motivate entry into the international market, the firms must first prepare
internally (e.g. be ready with export product).
For example, findings from both the regression analysis (Chapter 5) and case study
interviews (Chapter 6) demonstrated that on gaining access to the resources of network
partners, the firms became fully ready internally (e.g. with appropriate firm size, foreign
ownership resources, a ready export product, appropriate equipment and machinery, and
adequate financial capital resources). On achieving this internal preparedness, there is
greater potential for non-exporters to maximise any contingency factors that might
come their way and subsequently to initiate export business.
In particular, those non-exporting manufacturing SMEs in Ghana should not remain
separated from the dynamics of the external environment. The findings from the nonexporting firms suggest that they were not aware of the external stimuli which trigger
export initiation among the exporting firms (i.e. the critical incidents). In addition,
according to the findings from the exporting firms (Chapter 6), one of the major ways
for the non-exporters to achieve growth in international market knowledge and capacity
that would boost their export market entry would be through involvement in networking
with industrial associations in Ghana. In relation to this, non-exporters must develop the
habit of discussing their businesses with friends and family abroad, because such
actions can open up export opportunities.

290

8.4.2

Contribution to Public Policy

A number of export-led programmes to encourage non-exporting SMEs in Ghana to
consider engaging in export business are already being implemented. However, the
findings from this study suggest that the perceptions of the users (i.e. non-exporters)
about the effectiveness of these export-led programmes are negative and their awareness
of the programmes is limited. As a result, the government should launch new
educational initiatives to publicise its export-led programme and develop new
programmes focusing on non-exporting firms and already exporting firms. Moreover, it
is crucial, in the light of the outcomes of this study, that any government programme to
target the non-exporting manufacturing firms and encourage them to enter the export
market must first deal with changing the perceptions of the owner-managers as regards
the prospects of the export business before focusing on the internal capacity constraints
on firms. The results of this study suggest that the majority of non-exporting SMEs in
Ghana possess negative perceptions concerning the prospects for export business in
Ghana.
It has been highlighted above that individual owner-manager from Ghana need to
become more motivated to seek international alliances with foreign partners. The
recommendation to the government of Ghana is that it should create a platform (e.g. by
sponsoring business development forums either home or abroad) where wholly local
businesses will have the opportunity to interact with foreign businesses to create
alliances for export success. Such a platform would enable the wholly indigenous firms
to explore opportunities for shared ownership with foreign partners. In addition, since it
has been found that foreign partners augment the resource base of indigenous firms, this

291

would make the firm more likely to respond positively to the on-going industrial exportled programmes of the government and to initiate export business.
Furthermore, in the field of policy, governments in developing countries must host a
variety of industry fairs and subsidise international entrepreneurs who are willing to
participate in such events. Especially by hosting the event locally, the cost will be low
to attracting non-exporting firms to participate. The reason for this is that, findings from
the study indicate that some of the non-exporting firms perceived that they did not have
the wherewithal to participate in trade fairs. Some also perceived attendance at trade
fairs as being appropriate only for large businesses. The government must encourage
such initiatives because it would open up opportunities for wholly indigenous firms,
enabling them to showcase what they do, to interact with foreign business people, and
to form various alliances. The importance of this recommendation is reinforced by the
six critical incidents which emerged from the exporters’ accounts as causing export
business initiation; contact established via trade fair participation was the highest ranked
of these. Currently, most of these trade shows take place overseas based on the
interviews. So the government of Ghana must plan to subsidise participation in trade
fair event by its own firms.
8.4.3

Contribution to Theory & Future Research

The findings from the study have stimulated discussion of some basic extant concepts in
this research field. For instance, the results do not wholly support the widely accepted
contention that the stage theory (Johanson & Vahlne, 1977, Chetty, 1999) of
internationalisation represents a fully valid predictor of the type of market the small
and/or medium-sized firm will enter once a decision is made to initiate export business.
For instance, in this study, ‘psychic distance’ did not appear to be significant (i.e.
292

neither the critical incidents nor the influential factors); rather, the firms exported to
where there were ready buyers and in some cases where a network party lived or where
foreign orientation had occurred. Therefore, future researchers must be careful when
applying this theory to explain the export behaviour of SMEs from different geographic
contexts (e.g. Ghana).
The study has also stimulated debate regarding the assumptions from both the RBV
(Barney, 1991) and the stage theory, which argue that the export development of a firm
is: (1) achieved by its own individual efforts (e.g. the process of resource mobilisation
and development for export) and (2) is also explained mainly by its internal capacity
factors. This confirms the need for future researchers to be cautious when applying
either stage theory or the RBV theory to an explanation of the small and medium-sized
export firm’s behaviour in a developing country like Ghana. The reason here is that
firms from this part of the world are heavily resource constrained, and any export
success they achieve can only be understood in detail only when it is considered in the
light of networking ties and contingency factors. This situation may differ from similar
firms in the advanced countries, which may feel confident in proactively seeking export
business because, in sharp contrast to their counterparts in Ghana, they may already
possess the necessary internal resource (Leonidou et al., 2007; Williams, 2008)
capacity.
As none of the theoretical frameworks used in the thesis is without weaknesses, coupled
with the fact that the event under study appeared complex, dynamic and interactive,
integrating the 5 theories offset the weaknesses inherent in one or the other. Moreover,
the proposed integrated framework arguably has more potential to explain this complex
phenomenon than a single framework. In particular, application of the network theory
293

and the contingency theory in the thesis resolves the weaknesses of the RBV and the
stage theory, making the examination provided by the thesis more robust.
With regard to extending the current thesis, the generalisation of the findings could be
widened if the study’s framework were to be replicated in similar SME manufacturing
sub-sectors in Ghana as well as in similar developing countries. In this case, the revised
framework (Chapter 6, Figure 6.6) could serve as a first step and as a theoretical basis
for such a study. On the basis of the qualitative responses, it might be suggested that the
relative differences and importance found among the factors could be tested in the
future studies using statistical tests. In addition, similar future work could repeat the
method applied here in other sectors (e.g. those of machinery, metal, furniture), in order
to ascertain whether the same critical incidents were evident.
8.4.4

Limitations of the Study

Notwithstanding the contribution of the thesis, the following limitations must be
highlighted. Although the variables used in some parts of the study (e.g. Chapter 6)
were operationalised in the light of previous studies, it should be indicated that in some
cases they do not measure the specific resource variable or the aptitude of a firm in
terms of facilitating export behaviour. These measures are proxies. For example, the
aggregate full-time workers’ education level measures the average level of the last
school attended. This proxy excludes other measures such as: ambition, motivation,
perseverance, enthusiasm and networking, which a person gains from formal education.
This must be taken into consideration when interpreting the results.
In addition, the results were based on SMEs from the manufacturing sectors, and so it is
not known whether similar firms from the service sectors in Ghana would manifest the

294

same behaviour. Therefore, this must also be taken into account when interpreting the
results. Furthermore, the critical incident studies were limited to the garments and
textiles sub-sector. As a result, given the circumstances of the case studies, the study
indicates the critical incidents the export behaviour of SMEs in this sub-sector in Ghana
even though other manufacturing SMEs from Ghana can be said to share similar
characteristics. Moreover, the data set upon which the study draws is based on the
context of one country.

8.5

CONCLUDING REMARKS

The major contribution of the thesis is the development and testing of an integrated
theoretical framework from the literature review to explain the export behaviour of
small owner-manager firms from Ghana. The use of evidence from Ghana (e.g. a nonUS or European context) to validate the proposed integrated theoretical framework
represents a significant contribution to the field by answering the call (e.g. Sousa et al.,
2008) to validate extant findings in the field using evidence from Africa. Consequently,
the results contribute to the literature on the export behaviour of SMEs in developing
countries. The study makes an additional contribution through shedding light not only
on export behaviour, which has already received some attention in the SME literature,
but simultaneously on non-exporting SMEs behaviour: an area in which there has been
very little research. In addition, the new insights from Ghana provided by this thesis on
the international entrepreneurship phenomenon (e.g. Chapter 6) will serve as important
guidelines in the current debate within the SME internationalisation literature.

firm size did not seem to
matter in early
internationalisation, but
desire for short-term
profit, favourable
foreign demand
conditions and industry
factors predicted the
early
internationalisation
events of the
Vietnamese firms.

Appendix J: Fieldwork Introductory Letter

Page 362

Appendix K: Exporters’ Interview Guide
Qualitative Interview Guide – the Exporters Section
Introduction
The study concerns your experiences of the critical incidents and factors that trigger your
export initiations and that facilitate your ability to deliver the export orders you receive. The
conversations and the stories are in two parts: the first focuses on critical incidents that
trigger export initiation in this firm and the second concerns the critical factors that facilitate
the delivery of the export orders you receive.

Part 1
(1) Tell me a story by describing when, how and why you formed this firm.
(2) How many workers do you have?
(3) Describe when, how and why your firm became involved in the export business up until
today.
(4) How many countries have you and/or do you export to, to date? (e.g., I have exported to China
before, I have exported to the USA, before, I have or do export to Holland etc).
(5) In which years did this occur? (e.g. in the USA, it was in 1995; in Holland in 2001 etc.).
(6) Why those specific countries and years, including this year? (e.g., because Holland is
close to Ghana, because that country shares similar psychological characteristics just like
Ghana, because the UK is closer to Ghana, or because my buyer lives there or because my
network partners reside there etc. etc. and also it was 1995 (2001, 1999) because my business
was doing well at that time). For example, you said you export, but have you exported to the
UK, US, Italy before?’; ‘You said your first export destination was in the Netherlands, but
how the about Canada, Nigeria, and Norway etc,?; ‘You said the year of your first export was
in ….. (e.g. 1998), but did you also export in 1999, 2000, 2001… 2009?; ‘How and what was
the outcome?
(7) Which specific incidents directly trigger the receipt of export order(s)? In this question, I
want us to explore the extent to which any, all and/or combination the following factors
became the most critical incidents:
The stage theory’s factors: (1) psychological distant (Psychic distant) – defined as events in
terms of the geographical and the psychological closeness between Ghana and the country of
your export destinations and (2) receipts of unsolicited orders – defined as event in terms of
being approach to supply an export order which previously the firm has not be prepared for.

Page 363

The network theory’s factors: (1) event as a result of the influence of formal network
relations (e.g. networking relations with other businesses, industrial associations, foreign
partners/foreign ownership) and (2) event as a result of informal network relations (e.g.
networking relations with family and friends) were explored.
The international entrepreneur theory’s factors: (1) event as a result of international
orientation – previous residence, work and travelling abroad.
Contingency theory’s factors: (1) event as a result of government action.
Part 2
After you have received export orders, to what extent can you recollect, affirm and
confidently describe the following factors as significantly facilitating the capacity of your
forms to deliver its export orders.
The resource-based view factors: (1) financial capital, (2) the quantity of available full-time
employees and the stock of equipment and machinery;

The network theory’s factors: (1) the influence of formal networking relations (e.g.
networking relations with other businesses, industrial associations, foreign partners/foreign
ownership) and (2) informal networking relations (e.g. networking relations with family and
friends);
The international entrepreneur theory’s factors: (1) excessive alertness to profitable
international opportunities, (2) international orientation, (3) international business vision and
(4) international risk-taking behaviour;
The stage theory’s factors: (1) the size of your firm, (2) the age of your firm, (3)
psychological distant and (4) and
Contingency theory’s factors: (1) the influence of sector.

Page 364

Appendix L: Procedure for the Interview
The procedure used in the interview process is outlined as follows. The first procedure
concerns advanced telephone calls to the prospective interviewees from the sample frame.
The calls offered the opportunity to introduce and explain the study in detail to them.
Following these calls, owner-managers who willingly accepted to be part of the study and
who also met the screening criteria set out in the study (Chapters 6 & 7) will give the
directions to their companies’ premises in detail for the interview to be conducted. These
directions then became the map followed to the precise location on the day of the interview.
Furthermore, apart from the introductory call, on the day of the interview, a prospective
interviewee would be called again as a reminder.

Page 365

Appendix N: Qualitative Interview Guide – the Non-Exporters’ Section of the Study
Introduction
The study forms part of a broader study which concerns entrepreneurs’ experiences of critical
incidents which trigger export initiations and the actual factors that facilitate the capacity of
the exporting firms to meet their export orders. Although you operate in the same sub-sector
and location, I understand that you do not participate in export business. As a result, I would
like know in this interview whether your business:
Part 1
(1) Has attempted to export before?
(2) Has been called upon by a government ministry, agency or department for a
nomination to be selected to represent Ghana’s garment and textile sub-sector abroad
in connection with export business?
(3) Has received an unsolicited export order before? If yes, in which year (s) and from
which country or countries?
(4) Has experienced or participated in trade fairs or shows, whether here in Ghana and/or
abroad, in connection with export business?
(5) Has advertised its profile/product(s) in any medium – for example, the internet or a
book - in an attempt to export? Or has received an unsolicited order before?
(6) Has had an offer of partnership by an international partner(s) as joint owners of the
firm in an attempt to enter the export market?
(7) And/or have you have lived, travelled and worked abroad, where these can impact on
your opportunity to enter the export market?
Part 2
In any of the above questions where you answer is in the affirmative, I would like you to
tell me how you reacted to any of those incidents. In specific, what made it difficult or
prevented you from seizing and maximising such events and becoming an exporter?

Could it be said that any of the challenges that prevented you from maximising such
opportunities and become an exporter were due to lack of and/or inadequate of:
The resource-based view factors: (1) financial capital, (2) the quantity of available full-time
employees and the stock of equipment and machinery;

The network theory’s factors: (1) the influence of formal networking relations (e.g.
networking relations with other businesses, industrial associations, foreign partners/foreign
ownership) and (2) informal networking relations (e.g. networking relations with family and
friends);

Page 385

The international entrepreneur theory’s factors: (1) excessive alertness to profitable
international opportunities, (2) international orientation, (3) international business vision and
(4) international risk-taking behaviour;
The stage theory’s factors: (1) the size of your firm, (2) the age of your firm, (3)
psychological distant and (4) and
Contingency theory’s factors: (1) the influence of sector.
Part 3: Reasons for non-exporting
Lastly I will also like you to tell me the reasons why you have not considered the export
market, especially after operating more than five years in this domestic market until now.

Page 386

APPENDIX P: DEFINITION OF OTHER KEY TERMS

1.1

INTRODUCTION TO THE DEFINITION OF A SMALL FIRM

Varied definitions of SMEs exist from both official and academic sources. Notable
official sources come from the USA, the UK and Europe (i.e. the European
Commission) in general. The official definitions of these countries are often cited
because they are the countries which are most researched, especially with regards to the
SME internationalisation research field and the small business research field in general
(Sousa et al., 2008).
1.2

THE US’ OFFICIAL DEFINITION

The Small Business Administration (SBA) in the USA uses two quantitative measures
to define SME, namely the number of employees and the annual sales revenue. These
two criteria are used to define SMEs in seven main sectors which include: construction,
manufacturing, mining, transportation, wholesale trade, the retail business and selected
services. Table 1 presents a sample of firms of those industries to which the definition
applies.

Page 397

Table 1: US’ Classification of Small Business
SIC
Code

1.3

BRITAIN’S OFFICIAL DEFINITION

Britain’s official definition is mostly drawn from the Bolton Committee’s definition.
The problem of defining a small business is an old challenge and goes as far back as
1971. In 1971, the Bolton Committee attempted to resolve the challenge and so
formulated two definitions, these being ‘economic’ and ‘statistical’ definitions. On one
hand, the ‘economic’ definition of a small business is one which meets the following
criteria:
•
•
•

A relatively small market share in the market place;
Management which is personalised and unstructured as well as being wholly run
by the owners or part owners;
Flexibility and independence since it does not form part of a large enterprise.

On the other hand, with regard to the statistical definition, a small business will be
defined as such based on the following factors:
•
•
•

The size of the small firm’s sector and contribution to GDP, employment,
exports, etc;
Whether or not the small firm sector’s contribution has changed over time;
The possible application of the statistical definition in a cross-country
comparison for the small firm’s economic contribution.

For example, following the statistical definition, the committee contended that small
firms in the manufacturing sector are those with 200 employees or less, and in the
construction sector – 25 employees or less; in the mining sector – this is 25 or less. In
addition to the number of employees criterion, other criterion are defined in terms of the
annual sales turnover, these being: the retail sector – with a turnover of £50,000 or less;
miscellaneous – with a turnover of £50,000 or less; a service – with a turnover of
£50,000 or less; the motor trade – with a turnover of £100,000 or less; wholesale trade –

Page 399

with a turnover of £200,000 or less. However, other measures include those in road
transportation – five vehicles or less - and in catering - excluding multiples and brewery
– managed houses (Bolton Committee, 1971).
Storey (1994) criticises the Bolton committee’s definition (see sub-Section 1.6 below).
As a result of the weaknesses associated with the Bolton’s committee’s definition (subSection 1.6), the UK’s companies Act 1989 issued another definition that matches the
reporting requirements of small and medium-sized firms. The Act also recommends the
following criteria: a firm is said to be a small business if, for the financial year and the
one immediately preceding it, two out of the following three conditions apply:
•
•
•

Turnover does not exceed £2 million;
The balance sheet total does not exceed £975,000;
The average weekly number of employees does not exceed 50.

The Act further separates small business from medium-sized firms, unlike the Bolton
committees’ definition, and also recommends that a firm may be defined as mediumsized, if for the financial year and the one immediately preceding it, two out of the
following three conditions apply:
•
•
•

1.4

Turnover does not exceed £8 million;
The balance sheet total does not exceed £3.9 million;
The average weekly number of employees does not exceed 250.

EUROPEAN COMMISSION’S DEFINITION

As a result of the challenges associated with the concept of ‘small business’, in 1996 the
European Commission (EC) coined the term SME and recommended a single
definition. The EC divided small firms into three components, namely the micro, the

Page 400

small, and the medium-sized enterprises, and applied the following definitions as shown
in Table 2 below.

1.5

THE UNIDO’S DEFINITION

The United Nations Industrial Development Organisation (UNIDO) also applies the
number of employees to define SMEs. The organisation distinguishes the definition of a
small business in industrialised countries from those in developing countries. According
to UNIDO, SMEs in industrialised countries consist of the following: large firms – with
500 workers or more; medium-sized firms – with 100 – 499 workers, and small firms
with 99 workers or less. However, for developing countries, SMEs are classified in the
following way: large firms have 100 workers or more; medium-sized firms have 20 – 99
workers, small firms have 5 – 19, and micro-firms have 5 workers or less.

Page 401

1.6

ASSESSMENT OF SMEs’ DEFINITION

Although, the above official definitions of SMEs vary, they provide the foundation for
extant definitions on policy, practical and theoretical research. However, these official
definitions are not without criticisms. As can be seen above, several variations exist; for
instance, the Small Business Administration in the US uses only quantitative criteria,
based on two different measures (i.e. the number of employees and annual sales
revenue). In addition, although, the Bolton’s report uses qualitative and quantitative
measures (i.e. economic and statistical), and has too many criteria for different sectors
in the same country. Storey (1994) criticised the Bolton’s (1971) definition because it
regards a small firm as a homogenous entity with no demarcations. In addition, Storey
(1994) further criticised the Bolton’s definition by arguing that the committee assumed
that the management of small firms will always be unstructured and personalised.
Following the weaknesses of the official definitions, coupled with the variations among
them, the ‘definition of the small business’ among academic researchers also vary
depending on a study’s purpose and the country of the study. A sample of how the small
business is defined among researchers is discussed below.
For example, Sousa et al. (2008) reported that from their review of 52 studies on the
export behaviour of SMEs (Table 1.3), it was identified that most researchers applied
different definitions. In this review, the researchers found that for studies in China,
SMEs were conceived as enterprises with less than 3,000 employees. This deviates from
a Swaziland study by Obben & Magagula (2003), who defined a small business as a
firm with 20 employees or less. Further to this, in an Australian study, Philip (1998)
used 10 employees or less to define the very small firm. Hence, form an objective
consideration of previous work; it can be argued that what constitutes a small business
Page 402

depends on a number of factors, including: (1) the purpose of the research, (2) the
country of the study, and (3) the industry where the study is conducted.
1.7

DEFINITION OF EXPORTING

Firms which intend to consider international business can do so using various strategies.
These strategies include: exporting, licensing and foreign direct investment (Hutchinson
et al., 2005; Petersen & Welch, 2002). Of the strategies that exist, SMEs are noted for
their export business strategies. By definition, the export business constitutes the
transfer of goods and services across national boundaries, either through direct or
indirect methods (Lee & Brasch, 1978; Hatten, 1997). According to these authors, the
indirect method involves export through representatives, whereas the direct method
involves export directly through foreign customers such as the buyers or the importers.
This study applies the above definition, but while it is specified above that the export
business can be composed of services or merchandise products, only firms that export
merchandise products across the national boundaries of Ghana are captured in this
thesis.
1.8

DEFINITION OF INTERNATIONALISATION

Like the concept of a ‘small business’, there are major variations regarding what
constitutes internationalisation in the literature, with a variety of writers in the field
presenting the concept from different perspectives. Initially, Johnson & Vahlne (1977),
two of the earliest proponents of the concept, defined internationalisation as an outward
movement of individual firms. These authors implied that a firm moves from operating
solely in the domestic market to expanding across its national border, supplying goods
or services to the foreign markets. Johnson & Vahlne’s (1977) idea of

Page 403

internationalisation is that it has a unidirectional approach, rather than being a
simultaneous act that involves both buying from and selling to international markets.
In addition, Elango (2000) perceives the internationalisation as the degree to which a
firm operates internationally, while Ruzzier et al. (2006) too define internationalisation
as an outward movement of a firm’s international operations. Thee two definitions (e.g.
Elango, 2000 & Ruzzier et al., 2006) seem similar to that of Johanson & Vahlne (1977).
Other writers argue that a holistic definition of internationalisation must take into a
consideration of both the outward and the inward processes, since the phenomenon is
bi-directional (Etemad & Wright, 1999; Jones, 1999; Rialp & Railp, 2001). This is
clearly a robust approach because while a firm can sell goods or services in the
international market, it may also purchase from international suppliers. Although
inconsistencies in the definitions of internationalisation are recognised, it can be stated
that researchers adapt a definition based on the purpose of their research.
However, a recent argument about the definition of SME internationalisation seems to
shift the focus from whether or not the event is outward or inward or both. The current
argument is that internationalisation phenomena encompass the overall behaviour of the
small business with regard to how it mobilises the resources that are needed for its
outward internationalisation (McDougall et al., 1994; Reuber & Fischer, 1997;
Westhead et al., 2001; Brush et al., 2002; Ibeh, 2003a; 2004; Westhead et al., 2004;
Ibeh, 2005; Ibeh & Wheeler, 2005; Ruzzier et al., 2006).
These researchers imply that SMEs internationalisation can be understood better from
the point of view of how the firm organises the process of assembling and expanding
the essential stock of resources for internationalisation activity. Therefore, while it is

Page 404

agreed that the internationalisation of SMEs can be both inward and outward, the thesis
extends the outward dimension of the SMEs’ internationalisation and similarly views
internationalisation as the process of assembling various resource stocks by the SME
towards its internationalisation activity. The outward internationalisation is of particular
focus because it is argued that it offers many micro and macro benefits to countries
compared to import business or inward internationalisation (Ruzzier et al., 2006).
Lastly, it is worthy of note that even though exporting is one of the approaches to a
firm’s internationalisation (sub-Section 1.11.1); in this thesis the two concepts (export
and internationalisation) are used interchangeably. This implies that, for example, SME
internationalisation is commonly referred in the thesis as the same as SME export
behaviour.

1.9

DEFINITION OF RESOURCES

The concept of resources originates from Penrose (1959), who conceptualises firms as a
collection of productive resources that enables them to compete successfully against
other firms. As a result, it is commonly argued that the resource-based view (RBV)
builds on the writings of Penrose (Wernerfelt, 1984; Barney, 1991). The RBV’s
framework remains one of the significant frameworks in SMEs internationalisation
research field (McDougall et al., 1994; Westhead et al., 2001; Rialp et al., 2005;
Ruzzier et al., 2006), with their key argument being that SMEs’ internationalisation
behaviour

centres

on

resource

mobilisation

and

development

toward

the

internationalisation activity. As a result, the whole argument behind the phenomenon
has become a resource mobilisation oriented one.

Page 405

Likewise, with the concepts of a ‘SME’ and ‘exporting’, as discussed above, the
definition of resources also vary from researcher to researcher. For example, Ruzzier et
al. (2006: 97) defines resources as “stocks of available tangible or intangible factors that
are owned or controlled by the organisation and converted into products or services by
using varieties of other resources and [a] co-ordinating mechanism”, and in this study,
the authors’ sub-divide resources into organisational, financial, human and social
capital. In addition, Toulan (2002) views resources as a firm’s stock of assets, and
categorises them into physical, human, organisational and financial capital as well as
technological resources. Dhanaraj & Beamish (2003) also group resources into
organisational or managerial, entrepreneurial and technological categories, while Brush
et al. (2002) group them into human, organisational and financial divisions. In addition,
Barney (1991) defines resources as the immutable, rare and non-substitutable stock of
available assets, tangible or intangible owned by a firm. Therefore, while various
classifications differ, what is seen in the literature is that each author’s, or group of
authors; conceptualisation is also based on the purpose a study seeks to address. This
thesis extends Barney’s (1991) conceptualisation of resources.
However, because Barney indicated that the relationship between his resources
attributes (i.e. rarity, immutable, and non-substitutability etc.) and the performance of a
firm’s is causally ambiguous, researchers (e.g. Westhead et al., 2001; Hall & Cook,
2009) distinguish between the strong versions of Barney (1991) from the weak version.
According to these authors, the weak version of Barney’s framework relaxes its core
attributes (i.e. rarity, immutable, and non-substitutability etc.) in its empirical
application. As a result, this thesis uses the weak version of Barney (1991) to

Page 406

operationalise the concept of resources and so does not seek to examine resource rarity,
immutability, non-substitutability etcetera.
1.10

ENTREPRENEURSHIP AND INTERNATIONAL

ENTREPRENEURSHIP
According to Shaw & Darroch (2004), entrepreneurs are people who take risks and start
something new. Other researchers extend this entrepreneurial behaviour and argue that
entrepreneurs are mainly people who possess the ability to spy, identify and assemble
resources in order to seize business opportunities (Schumpeter, 1961; McDougall et al.,
1994; Covin & Slevin, 1991; Ibrahim, 2004; Jones & Coviello, 2005). Researchers (e.g.,
Dimitratos & Jones, 2005; Crick & Spence, 2005) do not refute this line of debate, but
argue that, this line of thinking omits the role of ‘chance’ in the entrepreneurial process.
Such researchers consider that in real life, some particular owner-managers
(entrepreneurs) react to serendipitous events, which is different to systematic
opportunity identification.
An entrepreneurial event (i.e. chance opportunity and systematic opportunity
identification) cannot be said to be limited to the domestic market, but extends into the
international market as well. This view has led to the recent growing popular
phenomenon referred to as ‘international entrepreneurship’ within the small business
internationalisation research field (i.e. entrepreneurship across the national borders).
Different writers have given a variety of definitions to the phenomenon ‘international
entrepreneurship’, the most common one being “a combination of innovative, proactive,
and risk-seeking approaches that cross national borders and are intended to create value
in organisations” (McDougall et al., 2000: 903).

Page 407

This definition has been criticised because it was geared towards international new
venture firms (the born global firm), and the original authors revised it later as “the
discovery, enactment, evaluation and exploitation of opportunities-across national
borders-to create future goods and services” (Oviatt & McDougall, 2005: 540). In
addition, other researchers also defined the phenomenon as “a research approach to the
internationalisation of the small businesses from the entrepreneurial perspective”
(Ruzzier et al., 2006: 489). The definitions of Oviatt & McDougall (2005) are broad
because they cover the ordinary SMEs as well as international new venture firms.
However, in this thesis, the definition of Ruzzier et al. (2006) is adopted because their
definition views international entrepreneurship simply as the SME internationalisation
event from the perspective of the entrepreneur owner-manager, which is also the focus
of the thesis.

Page 408
